### ab_test_large_sample

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a clean **randomized experiment** — large sample, random assignment, clear outcome. You're in the best possible situation for causal inference.

**Preliminary recommendation**: Simple difference in conversion rates (or a proportion z-test), optionally with regression adjustment (CUPED-style) for variance reduction.

A couple of quick questions to finalize the plan:

1. **Outcome granularity**: Is conversion binary (converted / didn't convert), or do you also care about revenue per user or order value?

2. **Any complications?**: Did users stay strictly in one group (no crossover), and was the test run for its full planned duration — or was it stopped early?

3. **Goal depth**: Are you just estimating the **average** lift, or do you also want to know which *types of users* benefited most (personalization / HTE)?

These answers will determine whether a simple z-test wraps this up, or whether we add regression adjustment or subgroup analysis to the plan. What do you know?

</details>

### multiple_viable_methods

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": ["rdd"], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — and your setup is actually quite strong for it. You have a clear treatment group (50+ employees), a natural control group (under 50), and a rich panel with 18 months on each side of the policy.

That said, there's a critical design issue I want to flag before we go further.

---

**The treatment definition trap you need to avoid**

Your treatment assignment is based on employee count — but employee count can *change* in response to the minimum wage itself. If businesses near the threshold shed workers to fall below 50 and escape the policy, then measuring treatment status using *current* headcount contaminates your treated/control classification with a post-treatment outcome.

This is a classic **post-treatment conditioning** problem: the variable you're using to define treatment groups is itself potentially affected by treatment.

**The fix**: Define treatment status using employee count *before* the policy — ideally at least a few months prior. Businesses that had ≥ 50 employees then are "treated," regardless of what happened after.

---

**Follow-up question — the most important one**

Do businesses in your data move around the 50-employee threshold? Specifically:

- Are there many businesses *near* the cutoff (say, 40–60 employees)?
- Do you observe notable movement *across* the 50-employee line in the post-period?

This matters for two reasons:
1. It determines how serious the manipulation concern is for DiD
2. If there's a clean density of businesses near the cutoff, you'd also have a strong **regression discontinuity (RDD)** available — treating employee count as a running variable and comparing businesses just above and below 50. That would be an even sharper identification strategy, and a natural robustness check on the DiD.

</details>

---

### no_randomization_rich_covariates

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **matching / propensity score weighting / doubly-robust** problem — specifically a **P10 (selection on observables)** case. With rich demographics and no randomization, the strongest available strategy here is to control for confounders directly.

That said, I want to flag one thing before going further that could significantly *strengthen* your identification:

---

**You mentioned "prior earnings"** — that's a key detail. Do you have earnings observations from **multiple time periods before the program started**? For example, earnings 1 year before, 2 years before, etc.?

If yes → this opens a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** path, which is considerably more credible than matching alone. DiD would control for time-invariant unobservables (e.g., motivation, ability) that your demographics can't capture — exactly the kind of confounding that makes self-selection dangerous.

If no → we proceed with matching/PSW/DR, but I want to be transparent that this relies entirely on the assumption that your demographic variables capture *all* reasons why people selected into the program. If unobserved factors (like motivation or social networks) drove selection *and* affect earnings, estimates will be biased.

---

**Two quick questions to finalize the plan:**

1. **Pre-treatment earnings**: Do you have earnings from multiple periods before the program, or just a single prior-earnings snapshot?

2. **Objective** (from your description I'd guess "Evaluation", but confirming): Are you trying to measure the effect of a program that already happened — or deciding whether to scale/continue it?

</details>

---

### post_treatment_conditioning

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": false, "primary_method": false, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 0.25, "trap_detected": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

There's a critical issue to flag before we go further — and it's one of the most common mistakes in email campaign analysis.

## ⚠️ Post-Treatment Conditioning Trap

**Comparing openers vs. non-openers is not a valid way to measure the email's effect.** Here's why:

Email opening is a *downstream behavior caused by the email itself* — it's a post-treatment variable. Customers who open emails are systematically different from those who don't: they're more engaged, more likely to purchase, and would likely spend more *regardless* of the discount. Conditioning on opening doesn't isolate the email's effect — it smuggles in selection bias.

Concretely: if you compare openers vs. non-openers and find a $10 lift, you can't tell how much is the discount vs. "these people were already more likely to buy."

---

## The Right Question to Ask First

> **Did you send the email to all 100,000 customers, or was there a holdout group that didn't receive it?**

This determines the whole identification strategy:

| Situation | Recommended Approach |
|---|---|
| **Holdout group exists** | Compare all email recipients vs. holdout — this gives you the **Intent-to-Treat (ITT)** effect cleanly |
| **No holdout — everyone got the email** | You have no clean control group; we'd need to discuss options (historical comparison, matching, etc.) |
| **You want effect of *opening specifically*** | Use **Instrumental Variables (IV)**: email receipt as the instrument for opening — this estimates the LATE among compliers |

---

What's your setup — is there a group of customers who *didn't* receive the email?

</details>

---

### sharp_cutoff

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **Regression Discontinuity Design (RDD)** problem — and a textbook sharp one. The scholarship is deterministically assigned at a GPA cutoff of 3.5, meaning students just below and just above that threshold are likely comparable in every other way. That cutoff does the causal identification work for you.

Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan.

---

**1. Is the cutoff strict?**

Does *everyone* above 3.5 receive the scholarship, and *no one* below? Or does the probability of receiving it just jump at 3.5 (e.g., students can appeal, or there's discretion)?

- Strict → **Sharp RDD** (cleanest case)
- Probabilistic jump → **Fuzzy RDD** (uses the cutoff as an instrument — slightly more complex)

---

**2. How is GPA measured?**

Is it continuous (e.g., 3.4872), or rounded/binned (e.g., reported to one decimal)? Heavy rounding can create problems near the cutoff and affects how we handle bandwidth and manipulation tests.

---

**3. What's the business objective?**

Just want to confirm: are you trying to **evaluate** the scholarship's effect (it already exists and you're looking back), or is there a forward-looking **policy decision** riding on this — like whether to expand, change the cutoff, or reallocate budget?

</details>

---

### single_treated_unit

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **Synthetic Control (SCM)** problem — one treated unit (California), many pre-treatment quarters (~40), and a large donor pool (49 states). This is the canonical synthetic control setup.

DiD/TWFE is a valid alternative, but with only one treated unit, SC gives you a more principled counterfactual and better diagnostics.

---

A few quick questions to refine the plan:

**1. What's your exact outcome metric?** Total employment levels, employment rate, or something sector-specific (e.g., low-wage workers)?

**2. Did California have *any* anticipation period?** Minimum wage laws are often announced before they take effect — did employers start adjusting behavior before 2020?

**3. Are there other states that changed their minimum wage around 2020?** Even if they didn't implement the *same* law, large wage changes in donor states could contaminate the synthetic control.

**4. What's the business/policy objective?** Are you trying to *evaluate* the 2020 law after the fact, or inform a future policy decision?

These will shape which donor states to include and whether we need to worry about a fuzzy treatment onset.

</details>

### small_sample_panel

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — specifically a classic two-way fixed effects (TWFE) setup with a single treatment date. You have the ideal ingredients: panel data, multiple pre- and post-treatment periods, and a clear control group.

Let me ask a couple of questions to sharpen the plan.

---

**First — assignment mechanism**: Were the 15 treated stores *chosen* for the new layout, or were they randomly selected? For example, did a regional manager decide which stores got it first, or was it a deliberate pilot rollout?

This matters because if store selection was non-random (e.g., larger stores, higher-performing stores, specific regions), we need to think carefully about whether the control stores are a credible counterfactual — and whether the parallel trends assumption holds.

</details>

### staggered_rollout

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a **staggered difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — and an important detail: because stores were treated at *different times*, you'll need a staggered DiD estimator (Callaway-Sant'Anna or Sun-Abraham), **not** naive TWFE. Naive TWFE can give biased or even sign-reversed estimates when treatment effects vary across cohorts, which is common in wave rollouts like this.

You've got a strong setup: 500 units, 2 years of weekly data, clear treatment cohorts. Let me ask a few things to sharpen the plan.

---

**1 — Business objective**

Are you trying to *evaluate* a decision already made ("did it work?"), or is this more *optimization* — e.g., informing whether to keep, modify, or roll back the policy?

---

**2 — Effect timing**

How quickly would you expect a new return policy to show up in sales? Immediately (customers change behavior within weeks), or with a lag (word spreads, trust builds over months)? This matters for how we specify the event study window.

---

**3 — The "never treated" group**

All 500 stores eventually got the policy (250 in June). That means there's **no permanently untreated group**. Callaway-Sant'Anna can still work using "not-yet-treated" stores as controls — but I want to flag this upfront. Do you know whether the June stores were chosen for a reason (e.g., lower-performing stores rolled out last), or was the wave order roughly arbitrary?

</details>

---

### time_series_no_control

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is an **interrupted time series (ITS)** problem — specifically a great candidate for **CausalImpact** (Bayesian structural time series). You have a single treated unit, a clear intervention date, and a long pre-treatment history to build a counterfactual from.

Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan:

---

**First: do you have any related time series that were *not* affected by the campaign?**

For example — traffic from a country where the campaign didn't air, a competitor's publicly available traffic index, search volume for an unrelated category, or any other metric that tracks similar seasonality/trends but wasn't influenced by the TV ads.

Adding control series dramatically improves CausalImpact's counterfactual. Without them, it relies purely on the pre-treatment trend — which works, but is more sensitive to assumptions.

</details>

### weak_instrument

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "primary_method": true, "alternative_discussed": [], "asked_coverage": 1.0, "false_positives": [], "completeness": true, "warnings_coverage": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is an **Instrumental Variables (IV)** problem — specifically an **encouragement design**. Let me explain why before asking anything else.

---

### Why IV?

You have random assignment of the *email*, but you want the effect of *using the savings feature*. You can't just compare users vs. non-users of the feature — people who chose to use it are likely different in ways that also affect deposits (more financially engaged, etc.). That's selection bias.

The fix: use the **email as an instrument** for feature usage. Because the email was randomly assigned, it's as-if random — and it affects deposits *only* through whether people used the feature (exclusion restriction). This gives you the **LATE** (Local Average Treatment Effect): the effect of using the feature **among people who used it because they got the email** (compliers).

Your first stage looks strong: 30% vs. 5% uptake = a **25 percentage point jump**. That's a healthy instrument — weak instrument bias is unlikely to be a concern here.

---

### A few things to verify before I save the plan:

**1. The exclusion restriction** — Did the email *only* nudge people toward using the savings feature, or could it have directly affected deposit behavior in other ways? (e.g., did the email content itself contain savings tips, reminders about deposit goals, etc.?) If so, the instrument may be invalid.

**2. Population definition** — Is this all customers who were eligible for the email, or a subset? I want to make sure we're not conditioning on anything post-treatment.

**3. One-sided or two-sided non-compliance?** — Can anyone who *didn't* receive the email still use the savings feature? You mentioned 5% of non-emailed customers used it — so yes, there are "always-takers." That's fine, but good to flag. Your LATE will exclude them.

**4. Outcome timing** — Is total deposits measured over a fixed window after the email send? How long?

What's a short name for this project so I can save the plan?

</details>

---

### audit_did_nonparallel

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

No existing plan on file. Proceeding with a full cold audit. Here's my assessment:

---

# Audit Report: Inventory System → Store Sales (DiD)

**Date**: 2026-04-01
**Method audited**: Difference-in-Differences (Classic 2x2, TWFE)
**Overall assessment**: 🔴 **Red** — one fatal gap and several serious threats that need to be addressed before trusting the ATT estimate of 3.2.

---

## Summary

The design is sound in structure — a clean single treatment date, 50/50 split, 24 months of panel data, and correctly clustered standard errors. But the most important assumption in DiD (parallel trends) was never verified, which means the causal interpretation is currently unsupported. Beyond that, there are meaningful threats from treatment selection, anticipation, and possible spillovers between stores. None of these are fatal to the method — they're fixable — but they need to be addressed before you can defend the 3.2 estimate.

---

## Findings

---

### 🔴 Fatal — Parallel Trends Never Tested

**Category**: Assumption Violations

**Explanation**: Parallel trends is the load-bearing assumption in DiD. It says: *if the inventory system had never rolled out, treated and control stores would have followed the same sales trajectory.* You cannot test this directly in the post-period (it's counterfactual by definition), but you *can* test whether trends were parallel in months 1–11. The fact that this was skipped means you currently have no evidence the core identifying assumption holds. Any upward pre-trend in treated stores — driven by, say, stores being selected because they were growing faster — would inflate the 3.2 estimate and make it look like a causal effect when it isn't.

**Diagnostic & Fix**:

```r
library(fixest)
library(ggplot2)

# Create time-to-treatment variable (month 12 is treatment onset)
df$time_to_treat <- df$month - 12

# Event study: coefficients at t = -1 are the reference (set to 0)
# Pre-treatment coefficients should hover near zero
# Post-treatment coefficients show the dynamic treatment effect
es <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, ref = -1) | store + month,
  data = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

# Plot — this is the main deliverable
iplot(es,
      main = "Event Study: Pre-Trends Check",
      xlab  = "Months Relative to Inventory System Rollout",
      ylab  = "Estimated Coefficient on Sales")

# Joint significance test of pre-treatment periods
# H0: all pre-treatment coefficients = 0
# A significant p-value here is a red flag
wald(es, keep = "time_to_treat::-")
```

**What to look for**: Pre-treatment coefficients (months -11 through -2) should be statistically indistinguishable from zero and show no clear upward or downward trend. If they do trend, parallel trends is violated and your 3.2 estimate is not credible as-is.

---

### 🔴 Serious — Treatment Selection Mechanism Unknown

**Category**: Identification Threats

**Explanation**: Which 50 stores were assigned to treatment, and *why*? This matters enormously. DiD can tolerate treated and control groups having different *levels* of sales — that's what the fixed effects absorb. But it cannot tolerate them having different *trends*. Common selection patterns that would break DiD:

- **Worst-performing stores treated first** (regression to the mean masquerades as an effect)
- **Largest or highest-revenue stores treated first** (they may already be on a different growth trajectory)
- **Stores in certain regions treated first** (regional macro shocks contaminate the estimate)

If selection was random or administrative (e.g., rollout order determined by IT logistics, not store performance), parallel trends is more plausible. If selection was performance-based, you have a serious identification problem.

**Diagnostic**:

```r
library(dplyr)
library(ggplot2)

# Step 1: Compare pre-treatment sales levels and trends by group
pre_df <- df |> filter(month < 12)

# Average sales by group and month — plot these side by side
pre_df |>
  group_by(month, treated) |>
  summarize(mean_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop") |>
  ggplot(aes(x = month, y = mean_sales, color = factor(treated))) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_point() +
  scale_color_manual(values = c("0" = "steelblue", "1" = "tomato"),
                     labels = c("Control", "Treated")) +
  labs(title = "Pre-Treatment Sales Trends by Group",
       x = "Month", y = "Mean Sales", color = "Group") +
  theme_minimal()

# Step 2: Baseline characteristics comparison
df |>
  filter(month == 1) |>
  group_by(treated) |>
  summarize(
    mean_sales  = mean(sales),
    sd_sales    = sd(sales),
    n           = n()
  )
```

**Suggested fix**: Document the selection mechanism. If it was performance-based, consider (a) using matching/reweighting to find a better control group, (b) adding store-level linear time trends (controversial but sometimes defensible), or (c) switching to synthetic control if treated stores are outliers.

---

### 🟡 Serious — Anticipation Effects Not Ruled Out

**Category**: Assumption Violations

**Explanation**: Was the inventory system rollout announced before month 12? If store managers knew it was coming, they may have already started adjusting their ordering behavior — running down existing inventory, changing reorder patterns — *before* the system technically went live. This is the anticipation problem: treated stores deviate from their counterfactual trend before the treatment date, which (a) contaminates your pre-trends test (making it harder to detect violations) and (b) biases your ATT estimate downward (the "pre" period already has some of the treatment effect baked in).

**Diagnostic**: The event study above will also reveal anticipation — look specifically at whether t = -2 or t = -3 coefficients start moving in the same direction as the post-treatment effect. A "ramp-up" just before month 12 is the signature.

```r
# After running the event study above, inspect these specific periods
tidy_es <- broom::tidy(es)
tidy_es |>
  filter(grepl("time_to_treat::-[23]", term)) |>
  select(term, estimate, std.error, p.value)
```

**Suggested fix**: If anticipation is present, redefine the treatment date to the announcement date, or use `anticipation = 2` in the Callaway-Sant'Anna framework. If announcement date isn't recoverable, drop months -2 and -1 as "contaminated" periods and re-estimate.

---

### 🟡 Serious — Spillovers Between Treated and Control Stores

**Category**: Assumption Violations (SUTVA)

**Explanation**: SUTVA requires that treated stores' outcomes don't affect control stores' outcomes. In a retail context, this is violated if:

- Stores share a **regional distribution center** — if the new inventory system improves stock availability at treated stores, it may divert product from shared warehouses, reducing availability at control stores
- Stores **compete for the same customers** — if treated stores improve (faster checkout, better stock), customers may shift away from nearby control stores
- Stores share **management resources** — if regional managers allocate attention to the new system, control stores may be neglected

Any of these would make the control group look *worse* than it would have been without any treatment — inflating your ATT estimate by pushing down the control group counterfactual.

**Diagnostic**:

```r
# If you have location data (region, city, zip code):
# Check whether control stores near treated stores behave differently
# from control stores far from treated stores

# Create a "near treated" indicator for control stores
# (requires distance or region data)
control_df <- df |> filter(treated == 0, month >= 12)

# If region is available:
spillover_test <- feols(
  sales ~ post * near_treated | store + month,
  data = control_df,
  cluster = ~store
)
summary(spillover_test)
# Significant positive coefficient on post:near_treated = spillover likely
```

**Suggested fix**: If you have location data, check whether control stores near treated stores behave differently post-treatment. If spillovers are plausible from the business context, use geographically distant stores as controls or acknowledge the estimate as a lower bound on the true effect.

---

### 🟡 Serious — Seasonality Risk at the Treatment Cutoff

**Category**: Data Threats / Identification Threats

**Explanation**: Treatment begins at month 12. If month 1 is January, then month 12 is December — a massive seasonal peak for most retail categories. The time fixed effects in your model absorb *average* seasonal patterns across all stores, but they cannot absorb *differential* seasonal patterns between treated and control groups. If treated stores differ from control stores in how much their sales peak in December (e.g., treated stores are in higher-traffic locations, or sell more seasonal products), the time FEs won't fully control for this — and the December-to-January transition will contaminate your estimate.

Even if month 12 isn't December, any seasonal pattern that interacts with treatment group membership is a threat.

**Diagnostic**:

```r
# Plot monthly sales by group for the full 24 months
df |>
  group_by(month, treated) |>
  summarize(mean_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop") |>
  ggplot(aes(x = month, y = mean_sales, color = factor(treated))) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 12, linetype = "dashed", color = "gray40") +
  scale_color_manual(values = c("0" = "steelblue", "1" = "tomato"),
                     labels = c("Control", "Treated")) +
  labs(title = "Sales Trends by Group (Full 24 Months)",
       subtitle = "Dashed line = treatment onset",
       x = "Month", y = "Mean Sales") +
  theme_minimal()
```

**Suggested fix**: Do you know the calendar month of month 1? If the treatment date coincides with a major seasonal shift, consider controlling for month-of-year fixed effects separately from panel time FEs, or using a season × treated interaction as a robustness check.

---

### 🟠 Minor — Clustering with 50 Units: Wild Bootstrap Recommended

**Category**: Statistical Threats

**Explanation**: You're clustering at the store level with 50 treated and 50 control stores. The rule of thumb is that cluster-robust standard errors become unreliable below ~40–50 clusters, and you're right at that threshold. This doesn't mean your SEs are wrong, but they may be slightly anti-conservative (too small), making your p = 0.004 look more significant than it should be. The wild cluster bootstrap is more reliable in small-cluster settings because it doesn't rely on asymptotic approximations.

**Diagnostic & Fix**:

```r
library(fixest)
library(fwildclusterboot)

# Your original model
mod <- feols(sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
             data = df, cluster = ~store)

# Wild cluster bootstrap for more reliable inference
boot_res <- boottest(
  mod,
  clustid = "store",
  param   = "treated:post",
  B       = 9999,
  seed    = 42
)
summary(boot_res)
# Compare p-value to your original 0.004
```

**What to expect**: If the wild bootstrap p-value remains well below 0.05, your inference is robust. If it jumps above 0.05, the original standard errors were too optimistic.

---

### 🟢 Non-Issue — TWFE Functional Form Bias

**Category**: Assumption Violations

**Explanation**: I want to flag this explicitly as a *non-problem* here. The TWFE heterogeneity bias (Goodman-Bacon, de Chaisemartin) only applies to **staggered adoption** designs where different units are treated at different times. In your design, all 50 treated stores receive treatment at exactly the same time (month 12). This means TWFE is using a clean 2×2 comparison — treated pre vs. post, control pre vs. post — with no "forbidden comparisons" against already-treated units. Your specification is correct for this design.

---

### 🟢 Non-Issue — Stable Composition (Likely Fine)

**Category**: Assumption Violations

**Explanation**: With 100 stores × 24 months, this looks like a balanced panel. But it's worth a quick verification:

```r
# Confirm the panel is balanced
df |>
  count(store) |>
  summarize(min_obs = min(n), max_obs = max(n), n_stores = n())
# Should show: min_obs = max_obs = 24, n_stores = 100

# Check attrition by group
df |>
  group_by(store, treated) |>
  summarize(periods = n_distinct(month), .groups = "drop") |>
  group_by(treated) |>
  summarize(mean_periods = mean(periods), fully_observed = sum(periods == 24))
```

---

## Recommendations (Prioritized)

| Priority | Action | Why |
|---|---|---|
| **1** | Run the event study and pre-trends test | Untested parallel trends = no causal claim |
| **2** | Document and interrogate treatment selection | Performance-based selection invalidates the design |
| **3** | Check for anticipation (t = -2, -3 in event study) | Could bias ATT down by contaminating the pre-period |
| **4** | Assess spillover risk from business context | Shared distribution or customer competition would inflate ATT |
| **5** | Identify the calendar start month | If month 12 = December, seasonality interaction is a real threat |
| **6** | Run wild cluster bootstrap | 50 clusters is borderline; confirm inference is reliable |

**Bottom line**: The 3.2 estimate is plausible but not yet defensible. The event study (Priority 1) is the single most important next step — it will simultaneously address parallel trends, anticipation, and give you a richer picture of the treatment effect dynamics. If pre-trends look clean and selection was not performance-based, the design is solid. If either of those fails, you'll need to revisit the control group construction before publishing this result.

## The Core Verdict

Three findings dominate everything else:

| # | Finding | Why it matters |
|---|---------|----------------|

### audit_matching_post_treatment

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

No prior plan file — I'll work from the inline summary. The assumption checklist is already loaded in my context. Here is the full audit.

---

# Audit Report: Job Training Program — Propensity Score Matching

**Date:** 2026-04-01
**Method audited:** Propensity Score Matching (PSM / ATT)
**Overall assessment:** 🔴 **Red** — one fatal flaw invalidates the current estimate before anything else is considered

---

## Summary

The analysis contains a **fatal post-treatment conditioning error**: `employment_status_6mo` was measured *after the treatment began* and should never appear in the propensity score model. This single mistake means the reported ATT of $2,800 is biased — almost certainly downward — and the "perfect" balance result is an artifact of the bad control, not a sign of a healthy design. Two additional serious threats (unmeasured confounders, clustering) also need attention before results are trustworthy.

---

## Findings

---

### 🔴 FATAL — Post-treatment variable in the propensity score model

**Category:** 1 — Assumption Violations / 2 — Identification Threats

**Explanation:**

`employment_status_6mo` is measured 6 months *after the training program started*. Including it in the propensity score model is a textbook "bad control" — a variable that sits on the causal pathway from treatment to outcome.

Here is why it is fatal, not merely careless:

The propensity score is meant to summarize pre-treatment differences between participants and non-participants. Every variable in the model is interpreted as a confounder — a cause of *both* who gets treated and what their outcome would be absent treatment. `employment_status_6mo` does not meet that definition. The training program itself likely affects whether someone is employed at 6 months. When you match on it, you are asking: *"Among people with the same 6-month employment status, what is the effect of training?"* That is a fundamentally different question from the ATT.

Two causal mechanisms make this dangerous:

1. **Mediator blocking.** If the causal path is `Training → Employment at 6mo → Earnings at 12mo`, then conditioning on employment status partially or fully blocks the mechanism you are trying to measure. The ATT shrinks — it now estimates only the direct effect of training on earnings that does *not* operate through getting a job sooner. This is an incomplete, misleading picture of the program's value.

2. **Collider bias.** If unmeasured factors (e.g., motivation, local labor market conditions) affect *both* employment at 6 months *and* earnings, conditioning on employment status opens a spurious non-causal association between training and earnings even if none exists.

**The "excellent balance" result (SMD = 0.04) is a red flag, not a green one.** Achieving such tight balance on a post-treatment variable means the matched sample has been distorted to equate treated and controls on something treatment already changed. The balance diagnostic is answering the wrong question.

**Diagnostic / Fix:**

Remove `employment_status_6mo` from the propensity score model immediately. Re-estimate using only confirmed pre-treatment variables: `age`, `education`, `prior_earnings`, `industry`. Re-check balance and re-run the ATT.

```r
library(MatchIt)
library(cobalt)

# Corrected propensity score model — pre-treatment covariates only
m_correct <- matchit(
  treatment ~ age + education + prior_earnings + industry,
  data = df,
  method = "nearest",
  distance = "glm",
  caliper = 0.2,        # NOTE: see separate finding on caliper units
  estimand = "ATT"
)

# Check balance on pre-treatment covariates only
bal <- bal.tab(m_correct, thresholds = c(m = 0.1))
print(bal)
love.plot(bal, threshold = 0.1,
          title = "Balance After Removing Post-Treatment Variable")
```

```python
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
import numpy as np

# Corrected: pre-treatment only
pre_treatment_covs = ['age', 'education', 'prior_earnings', 'industry_encoded']

X = df[pre_treatment_covs].values
D = df['treatment'].values

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
ps_model.fit(X, D)
df['ps_corrected'] = ps_model.predict_proba(X)[:, 1]

# Compare PS distributions before/after removing post-treatment var
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
fig, ax = plt.subplots()
ax.hist(df.loc[df.treatment==1, 'ps_corrected'], bins=40, alpha=0.5, label='Treated')
ax.hist(df.loc[df.treatment==0, 'ps_corrected'], bins=40, alpha=0.5, label='Control')
ax.set_title('Corrected PS Distribution (pre-treatment covariates only)')
ax.legend()
plt.show()
```

> **Expect the ATT to rise after this correction.** If the program helps people find employment sooner, and employment drives earnings, the current estimate is suppressing part of the real effect.

---

### 🟠 SERIOUS — Unconfoundedness is not credible without motivation or ability measures

**Category:** 1 — Assumption Violations

**Explanation:**

Even with the post-treatment variable removed, conditional independence is hard to defend for a voluntary job training program. The people who *choose to enroll* are systematically different from those who do not — in ways that are almost never fully observed. Specifically:

- **Motivation / work ethic** — people who sought out a training program likely have higher job-search intensity than observationally similar non-participants. This would *upward*-bias the ATT.
- **Ability / cognitive skills** — correlated with both selection into training and earnings outcomes, typically not captured by education level alone.
- **Social/professional networks** — people who learned about the program through employment services may have stronger job-placement networks.

`prior_earnings` and `employment history` help, but they are proxies for these underlying traits, not the traits themselves. The E-value calculation below tells you how robust your conclusion is to unobserved confounders of this kind.

**Diagnostic:**

```r
library(EValue)

# Convert your ATT to a standardized mean difference or risk ratio first
# Example: if ATT = $2800, control mean earnings ~$25000, SD ~$8000
smd_estimate <- 2800 / 8000  # ≈ 0.35

# E-value for a standardized mean difference
evalues.MD(est = 0.35, se = 600/8000, true = 0)
# Output: minimum confounding strength (RR scale) needed to explain away the result
```

```python
import numpy as np

def e_value_from_rr(rr):
    """E-value: min RR for both confounder-treatment and confounder-outcome
    associations needed to fully explain away observed RR."""
    return rr + np.sqrt(rr * (rr - 1))

# Convert ATT to approximate risk ratio
# Example: control mean $25,000, ATT $2,800 → ratio ≈ 1.112
rr_approx = (25000 + 2800) / 25000
e_val = e_value_from_rr(rr_approx)
print(f"Approximate E-value: {e_val:.2f}")
# Interpret: an unmeasured confounder would need RR > e_val with BOTH
# treatment and outcome to explain away the result
```

**Suggested fix:** Report the E-value alongside the ATT. If it is low (say, < 1.5), the finding is fragile. Also conduct Rosenbaum sensitivity bounds on the matched sample (`rbounds::psens()` in R) to show how much hidden bias would overturn significance.

---

### 🟠 SERIOUS — Caliper of 0.2 is likely too wide (wrong scale)

**Category:** 4 — Statistical Threats

**Explanation:**

The standard recommendation from Rosenbaum & Rubin is a caliper of **0.2 standard deviations of the *logit* of the propensity score** — not 0.2 on the raw propensity score scale. This distinction matters a lot. On the raw PS scale, 0.2 is enormous: if the treated group's PS ranges from 0.3 to 0.8, a caliper of 0.2 allows matches between units that are essentially incomparable. It also means that near the extremes (where PS is close to 0 or 1), the caliper allows very different units to be matched because the PS scale is compressed there but the logit scale is not.

**Diagnostic / Fix:**

```r
library(MatchIt)

# WRONG: caliper = 0.2 on the raw PS scale
m_wrong <- matchit(treatment ~ age + education + prior_earnings + industry,
                   data = df, method = "nearest", distance = "glm",
                   caliper = 0.2)  # ← this is 0.2 on PS scale

# CORRECT: caliper = 0.2 on the logit PS scale (MatchIt default)
m_correct <- matchit(treatment ~ age + education + prior_earnings + industry,
                     data = df, method = "nearest",
                     distance = "glm",   # uses logit PS by default
                     caliper = 0.2,      # now interpreted as 0.2 SD of logit(PS)
                     std.caliper = TRUE) # explicit: caliper in SD units

# How many treated units were dropped due to the caliper?
summary(m_correct)
# If many treated units are unmatched, balance is for a selected subset — report this
```

```python
# In Python with psmpy or manual matching:
from scipy.special import logit

# Compute logit PS
df['logit_ps'] = logit(np.clip(df['ps_corrected'], 1e-6, 1-1e-6))

# Correct caliper in logit units
caliper_logit = 0.2 * df['logit_ps'].std()
print(f"Correct caliper on logit scale: {caliper_logit:.4f}")
print(f"Approx equivalent on PS scale (at PS=0.5): {caliper_logit/4:.4f}")
# Compare to what 0.2 on raw PS would mean
```

---

### 🟡 MINOR — Standard errors may need clustering

**Category:** 4 — Statistical Threats

**Explanation:**

Job training programs are typically offered through specific employers, training centers, geographic regions, or government programs. If multiple participants come from the same employer or region, their earnings are correlated — violating the independence assumption behind standard SEs. With SE = $600 and ATT = $2,800, the t-stat is about 4.7, so clustering would need to inflate SEs by ~2× to change the inference. But it is worth verifying.

**Diagnostic / Fix:**

```r
library(MatchIt)
library(fixest)

# After matching, extract matched data
matched_data <- match.data(m_correct)

# Cluster-robust SEs by employer or region
model_clustered <- feols(
  annual_earnings ~ treatment + age + education + prior_earnings + industry,
  data = matched_data,
  cluster = ~employer_id   # or region_id, training_center_id
)
summary(model_clustered)
```

```python
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf

# Cluster-robust SEs
model = smf.ols(
    'annual_earnings ~ treatment + age + education + prior_earnings + C(industry)',
    data=matched_data
).fit(cov_type='cluster', cov_kwds={'groups': matched_data['employer_id']})

print(model.summary())
print(f"\nClustered SE on treatment: {model.bse['treatment']:.2f}")
```

---

### 🟡 MINOR — External validity limited to program participants (ATT scope)

**Category:** 5 — External Validity

**Explanation:**

The ATT answers: *"What was the effect of training on those who participated?"* It does not answer: *"What would happen if we expanded the program to everyone who didn't enroll?"* These are different questions. People who chose not to enroll likely have lower baseline motivation or face structural barriers that training alone may not overcome. If the goal is program scale-up, the ATT may overstate the average benefit of broader rollout.

This is not a flaw in the analysis as specified, but it should be stated explicitly when reporting results. If the ATE is the policy-relevant quantity, PSM is poorly suited to estimate it — a different design (e.g., lottery-based enrollment) would be needed.

---

## Recommendations (Prioritized)

| Priority | Action |
|----------|--------|
| **1 — Do now** | Remove `employment_status_6mo` from the propensity score model. Re-run matching on pre-treatment covariates only. Re-check balance and re-estimate ATT. |
| **2 — Do now** | Verify the caliper is applied on the logit PS scale (0.2 SD of logit), not the raw PS scale. |
| **3 — Before reporting** | Compute E-value and Rosenbaum sensitivity bounds. Report these alongside the ATT — they are the honest acknowledgment that conditional independence is assumed, not proven. |
| **4 — Before reporting** | Check whether clustering is needed. Identify the natural clustering unit (employer, region, training center). |
| **5 — In the write-up** | Explicitly scope the result as an ATT and discuss whether this is the policy-relevant estimand for the decision at hand. |

### did_clean_parallel

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Classic **2×2 DiD** — single treatment date (month 12), two groups, panel structure. Standard TWFE with unit + time fixed effects is the right tool. No staggered complications.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Since you said pre-trends look parallel, let me quickly walk through all five:

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| **Parallel trends** | 🟡 Claimed — verify visually + stat test | Code below |
| **No anticipation** | 🟡 Check pre-period event study | Did stores know in advance? |
| **Stable composition** | 🟢 Likely OK | 100 stores, balanced panel — confirm below |
| **No spillovers** | 🟡 Flag | Could treated stores affect nearby control stores? |
| **Functional form** | 🟢 Not a concern | Single treatment date → TWFE is unbiased |

**Why functional form doesn't matter here**: The "forbidden comparisons" problem only arises when units are treated at *different* times. With a single treatment date, every treated unit forms a clean before/after comparison — no already-treated units are used as controls.

**Critical Python pitfall I'll handle in the code**: `treated` is time-invariant (same value for all months within a store), so entity fixed effects *fully absorb it*. Including `treated` as a regressor alongside `EntityEffects` causes perfect multicollinearity. Only the `treated * post` interaction term belongs in the formula — it varies over time.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.ticker as mtick
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS

# ── 1. Data prep ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

# Interaction term: the DiD estimator
df['treated_post'] = df['treated'] * df['post']

# Time-to-treatment (treatment at month 12)
TREAT_MONTH = 12
df['time_to_treat'] = df['month'] - TREAT_MONTH

# ── 2. Main DiD estimate ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# NOTE: Do NOT include 'treated' or 'post' directly.
#   - 'treated' is time-invariant → absorbed by EntityEffects
#   - 'post' is unit-invariant   → absorbed by TimeEffects
#   Only 'treated_post' varies across both dimensions.

df_panel = df.set_index(['store', 'month'])

mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    'sales ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects',
    data=df_panel
)
res = mod.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)
print(res.summary)

# Extract point estimate + 95% CI
att       = res.params['treated_post']
ci_lower  = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'lower']
ci_upper  = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'upper']
pval      = res.pvalues['treated_post']

print(f"\n── DiD Treatment Effect ──────────────────")
print(f"  ATT:    {att:+.3f}")
print(f"  95% CI: [{ci_lower:.3f}, {ci_upper:.3f}]")
print(f"  p-value: {pval:.4f}")

# ── 3. Parallel trends check — raw group means ───────────────────────────────

group_means = (
    df.groupby(['month', 'treated'])['sales']
    .mean()
    .reset_index()
    .rename(columns={'treated': 'group', 'sales': 'mean_sales'})
)
group_means['group'] = group_means['group'].map({1: 'Treated', 0: 'Control'})

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(10, 5))
for grp, grp_df in group_means.groupby('group'):
    ax.plot(grp_df['month'], grp_df['mean_sales'], marker='o',
            label=grp, linewidth=2)

ax.axvline(x=TREAT_MONTH - 0.5, color='black', linestyle='--',
           linewidth=1.2, label='Treatment onset')
ax.set_xlabel('Month')
ax.set_ylabel('Mean Sales')
ax.set_title('Parallel Trends: Group Means Over Time')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('parallel_trends.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 4. Event study — dynamic treatment effects ───────────────────────────────

periods = sorted(df['time_to_treat'].unique())
periods_no_ref = [p for p in periods if p != -1]   # t = -1 is the reference

# Create one dummy per period
df_es = df.copy()
for p in periods_no_ref:
    df_es[f'ttp_{p}'] = (df_es['time_to_treat'] == p).astype(int)

ttp_cols = [f'ttp_{p}' for p in periods_no_ref]
formula_es = 'sales ~ ' + ' + '.join(ttp_cols) + ' + EntityEffects + TimeEffects'

df_es_panel = df_es.set_index(['store', 'month'])
mod_es = PanelOLS.from_formula(formula_es, data=df_es_panel)
res_es = mod_es.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)

# Collect coefficients (insert 0 at reference period t = -1)
coefs = []
for p in periods:
    if p == -1:
        coefs.append({'period': p, 'coef': 0.0, 'lower': 0.0, 'upper': 0.0})
    else:
        col = f'ttp_{p}'
        coefs.append({
            'period': p,
            'coef':  res_es.params[col],
            'lower': res_es.conf_int().loc[col, 'lower'],
            'upper': res_es.conf_int().loc[col, 'upper'],
        })
es_df = pd.DataFrame(coefs)

# Plot
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 5))
pre  = es_df[es_df['period'] < 0]
post = es_df[es_df['period'] >= 0]

for subset, color in [(pre, 'steelblue'), (post, 'darkorange')]:
    ax.errorbar(
        subset['period'], subset['coef'],
        yerr=[subset['coef'] - subset['lower'],
              subset['upper'] - subset['coef']],
        fmt='o-', color=color, capsize=4, linewidth=1.8
    )

ax.axhline(0, color='black', linewidth=0.8, linestyle='-')
ax.axvline(-0.5, color='black', linewidth=1, linestyle='--', label='Treatment onset')
ax.set_xlabel('Months Relative to Treatment')
ax.set_ylabel('Estimated Effect on Sales')
ax.set_title('Event Study: Treatment Effect Dynamics')
ax.legend(['Pre-treatment (should be ≈ 0)', 'Post-treatment'])
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('event_study.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 5. Pre-trends joint significance test ────────────────────────────────────
pre_cols = [f'ttp_{p}' for p in periods_no_ref if p < 0]
pre_coefs = res_es.params[pre_cols]
pre_pvals = res_es.pvalues[pre_cols]

print("\n── Pre-Treatment Coefficients (should be ≈ 0) ──")
for col, c, p in zip(pre_cols, pre_coefs, pre_pvals):
    period = int(col.replace('ttp_', ''))
    print(f"  t = {period:+3d}: coef = {c:+.3f}  (p = {p:.3f})")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Run a **placebo treatment date** in the pre-period to confirm the null:

```python
# ── Placebo test: fake treatment at month 6 ──────────────────────────────────
# Use only pre-treatment data (months 1–11)

df_pre = df[df['month'] < TREAT_MONTH].copy()
PLACEBO_MONTH = 6

df_pre['placebo_post']    = (df_pre['month'] >= PLACEBO_MONTH).astype(int)
df_pre['placebo_treated_post'] = df_pre['treated'] * df_pre['placebo_post']

df_pre_panel = df_pre.set_index(['store', 'month'])

mod_placebo = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    'sales ~ placebo_treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects',
    data=df_pre_panel
)
res_placebo = mod_placebo.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)

att_p  = res_placebo.params['placebo_treated_post']
pval_p = res_placebo.pvalues['placebo_treated_post']
print(f"\n── Placebo Test (fake treatment at month {PLACEBO_MONTH}) ──")
print(f"  Placebo ATT: {att_p:+.3f}  (p = {pval_p:.4f})")
print("  ✓ Reassuring if p > 0.10 and coefficient is near zero.")
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation template

Once you run the code, fill this in:

> The DiD estimate shows that the new inventory system changed sales by **[ATT]** units per month (95% CI: [lower, upper], p = [p-value]).
>
> The event study confirms pre-treatment coefficients are indistinguishable from zero (supporting parallel trends), with effects emerging at month 0 and [persisting / growing / fading] over the post-period.
>
> **Caveats**: (1) Parallel trends holds in the pre-period but cannot be verified post-treatment. (2) If treated and control stores share a market, spillover effects may compress the true estimate.

---

### Two things to verify before trusting the result

1. **Run the placebo** — if it returns a big, significant coefficient, your pre-trends aren't as clean as they look.
2. **Check panel balance** — `df.groupby('store')['month'].count()` should be 24 for every store. If not, flag which stores are missing and why.

Want me to run the robustness checks or move on to writing up findings?

### did_nonparallel_trends

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Your design maps cleanly to a **classic TWFE DiD** — single treatment date, balanced panel, one treated cohort. That's the simplest and most credible variant.

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Unit | `store` (100 stores) |
| Time | `month` (24 periods) |
| Treatment | `treated == 1`, starts at `month == 12` |
| Outcome | `sales` |
| Variant | Classic TWFE (single treatment date) |

One thing to flag before moving on: because you have **unit (store) fixed effects**, the time-invariant `treated` column is fully absorbed by those FEs. Including it directly as a regressor causes perfect multicollinearity. The identifying term is only the **interaction** `treated:post`, which varies across both stores and time. This is exactly what we want — and it's handled correctly in the code below.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Partially testable — check this first*

**What it means here**: Without the new inventory system, treated stores and control stores would have seen similar *changes* in sales month-over-month. Their sales levels can differ (FEs handle that), but their *trajectories* must have been similar.

**Why it matters**: If treated stores were already growing faster before month 12, DiD will attribute that pre-existing trend to the inventory system — overstating the effect.

**How to check**: Run an event study. Pre-treatment coefficients should hover near zero with no systematic trend.

```r
library(fixest)

df <- df |>
  dplyr::mutate(time_to_treat = month - 12)

es <- feols(sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store + month,
            data = df, cluster = ~store)

iplot(es,
      main  = "Event Study: Pre-Trends Check",
      xlab  = "Months Relative to Inventory System Launch",
      ylab  = "Estimated Sales Effect")
abline(h = 0, lty = 2, col = "red")
```

**What to look for**: Coefficients at `time_to_treat = -2, -3, ...` should be near zero and show no trend. A clean pre-period gives you confidence; a rising pre-trend is a red flag.

---

### 2. No Anticipation 🟡 *Also visible in the event study*

**What it means here**: Store managers didn't start ordering differently or changing operations *before* month 12 in anticipation of the new system (e.g., from an announcement in month 10).

**Why it matters**: If treated stores already changed behavior at month 10, the DiD estimate shrinks — the "pre" period isn't really untreated.

**How to check**: Look at the same event study plot above. Coefficients at `t = -2` or `t = -3` that are significant and trending toward the post-treatment direction suggest anticipation.

> **Quick question**: Was there an announcement period before rollout? If so, we may want to trim the pre-treatment window.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Likely fine, worth confirming*

**What it means here**: The same 100 stores are in the data for all 24 months. No stores were added or removed *because of* the inventory system.

**Why it matters**: If underperforming stores got the system and then closed, you'd only observe survivors — biasing the estimate upward.

```r
# Check: is the panel balanced?
df |>
  dplyr::group_by(store) |>
  dplyr::summarize(n_months = dplyr::n_distinct(month), .groups = "drop") |>
  dplyr::count(n_months)
# All stores should show n_months == 24
```

With 100 stores × 24 months = 2,400 rows, this is likely fine — but run it as a sanity check.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) 🟡 *Context-dependent*

**What it means here**: Stores that got the new inventory system shouldn't be affecting the sales of control stores — e.g., through shared supply chains, price competition, or customers switching between stores.

**Why it matters**: If treated stores started stocking products that control stores ran out of, control stores' sales would *drop* — making the treatment look more effective than it is.

> **Quick question**: Are these stores geographically dispersed, or do some compete in overlapping markets? If they're in distinct locations, spillovers are unlikely. If they're competing chains in the same cities, worth flagging.

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Not a concern here*

**What it means here**: The standard TWFE concern (biased weights from "forbidden comparisons") only applies to **staggered adoption** — where different units are treated at different times. Since all your treated stores got the system at exactly month 12, this is not an issue. Standard TWFE is the right tool.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | ⚠️ Must test | Run event study below |
| No anticipation | ⚠️ Check context | Inspect pre-period plot |
| Stable composition | ✅ Likely fine | Verify balanced panel |
| No spillovers | 🟡 Context-dependent | Consider store geography |
| Correct functional form | ✅ Not applicable | Single treatment date |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
library(fixest)
library(tidyverse)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. Verify panel is balanced ───────────────────────────────────────────────
df |>
  group_by(store) |>
  summarize(n = n_distinct(month), .groups = "drop") |>
  count(n)  # should be all 24

# ── 2. Main DiD estimate ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Note: store FE absorbs 'treated'; month FE absorbs 'post'
# Only treated:post varies across both dimensions — that's our DiD coefficient
model_did <- feols(
  sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

summary(model_did)
modelsummary(model_did,
             stars     = TRUE,
             gof_map   = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
             title     = "DiD: Effect of Inventory System on Sales")

# ── 3. Effect size with 95% CI ────────────────────────────────────────────────
coef_did <- coef(model_did)["treated:post"]
ci_did   <- confint(model_did)["treated:post", ]

cat(sprintf(
  "\nEstimated effect: %.3f  (95%% CI: %.3f to %.3f)\n",
  coef_did, ci_did[1], ci_did[2]
))

# As % of pre-treatment control mean
pre_control_mean <- df |>
  filter(treated == 0, post == 0) |>
  pull(sales) |>
  mean()

cat(sprintf(
  "Effect as %% of pre-treatment control mean: %.1f%%\n",
  100 * coef_did / pre_control_mean
))

# ── 4. Event study (pre-trends + dynamic effects) ─────────────────────────────
df <- df |>
  mutate(time_to_treat = month - 12)  # 0 = first treated month

es <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

# Plot
iplot(es,
      main  = "Event Study: Effect of Inventory System on Sales",
      xlab  = "Months Relative to System Launch (Month 12)",
      ylab  = "Estimated Sales Effect (vs. Month 11)",
      col   = "steelblue",
      pt.join = TRUE)
abline(h = 0, lty = 2, col = "red")
abline(v = -0.5, lty = 3, col = "gray50")

# ── 5. Joint test: are pre-treatment coefficients = 0? ───────────────────────
# Extract pre-treatment coefficient names
pre_coef_names <- grep("time_to_treat::-", names(coef(es)), value = TRUE)

if (length(pre_coef_names) > 0) {
  wald_result <- wald(es, keep = pre_coef_names)
  cat("\nJoint test of pre-treatment coefficients (H0: all = 0):\n")
  print(wald_result)
}
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Two checks I'd recommend for your design:

```r
# ── Robustness 1: Placebo treatment date ─────────────────────────────────────
# Re-run DiD using only pre-treatment data (months 1–11)
# with a fake treatment date at month 6
# If DiD finds an effect here, parallel trends are suspect

df_pre <- df |>
  filter(month <= 11) |>
  mutate(post_placebo = as.integer(month >= 6))

model_placebo <- feols(
  sales ~ treated:post_placebo | store + month,
  data    = df_pre,
  cluster = ~store
)

cat("\nPlacebo DiD (fake treatment at month 6, pre-period only):\n")
summary(model_placebo)
# Desired result: small, insignificant coefficient

# ── Robustness 2: Varying the window around treatment ─────────────────────────
# Check if results are driven by months very far from treatment date
results_window <- map_dfr(3:11, function(w) {
  df_w <- df |>
    filter(abs(month - 12) < w | post == 0) |>
    filter(month >= (12 - w) & month <= (12 + w - 1))

  m <- feols(sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
             data = df_w, cluster = ~store)

  tibble(
    window    = w,
    estimate  = coef(m)["treated:post"],
    ci_lo     = confint(m)["treated:post", 1],
    ci_hi     = confint(m)["treated:post", 2]
  )
})

ggplot(results_window, aes(x = window, y = estimate)) +
  geom_point() +
  geom_errorbar(aes(ymin = ci_lo, ymax = ci_hi), width = 0.2) +
  geom_hline(yintercept = 0, lty = 2, col = "red") +
  labs(title = "Sensitivity to Bandwidth Choice",
       x = "Months Before/After Treatment Included",
       y = "DiD Estimate") +
  theme_minimal()
```

> The inventory system rollout is associated with a **[X] unit increase** in monthly sales per store (95% CI: [lower] to [upper]), representing a **[Y]%** increase relative to the pre-treatment average in control stores. The event study shows no evidence of differential pre-trends between treated and control stores, and a [flat/immediate/gradual] post-treatment pattern.

**Caveats to include**:
- If the event study pre-period isn't clean, note that parallel trends cannot be verified
- The estimate is a Local ATT — it applies to stores that received the system at month 12 and may not generalize to different rollout contexts
- Any spillovers through shared supply chains would bias the estimate upward

### experiments_noncompliance

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Setup

You have **two-sided non-compliance**: some assigned-to-treatment never took it, and some in control crossed over. This is the more complex case. You'll need two estimates:

- **ITT** (Intention-to-Treat): effect of *assignment* — always valid under randomization
- **CACE/LATE** (Complier Average Causal Effect): effect of *actually receiving* treatment — estimated via IV (2SLS), using assignment as the instrument

---

## Assumptions Check

Let me walk through the key ones for your situation:

**✅ Random assignment** — You ran an RCT, so this holds. We can verify with a balance table if you have baseline covariates (none visible in your sample, but worth checking if you have them).

**⚠️ Compliance** — Two-sided non-compliance confirmed. This is the central concern here. The CACE/LATE estimate via IV is only valid for **compliers** — units who take treatment if and only if assigned to it. The code below will show you your exact compliance and contamination rates.

**⚠️ Exclusion restriction (for IV)** — Assignment must affect the outcome *only through* treatment receipt. Think: could being *told* you're in the treatment group change behavior, regardless of whether you took it? If yes, this is a threat to the CACE estimate. The ITT is always safe.

**✅ SUTVA** — With individual-level randomization and 2000 participants, interference is unlikely unless participants interacted and shared the treatment.

**✅ No anticipation** — Assuming participants didn't know about the experiment beforehand.

---

## Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS
from scipy.stats import ttest_ind

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. Compliance audit ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print("=" * 55)
print("COMPLIANCE AUDIT")
print("=" * 55)

compliance_table = pd.crosstab(
    df["assigned_treatment"],
    df["received_treatment"],
    rownames=["Assigned"],
    colnames=["Received"],
    margins=True
)
print(compliance_table)

comply_rate = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 1, "received_treatment"].mean()
contam_rate = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 0, "received_treatment"].mean()

print(f"\nCompliance rate (treated group took it):   {comply_rate:.1%}")
print(f"Contamination rate (control group took it): {contam_rate:.1%}")
print(f"Overall correct assignment rate:            "
      f"{(df['assigned_treatment'] == df['received_treatment']).mean():.1%}")

# ── 2. Balance check (if you have baseline covariates) ───────────────────────
# Replace with your actual covariate columns
covariates = []  # e.g. ["age", "gender", "baseline_score"]

if covariates:
    print("\n" + "=" * 55)
    print("BALANCE TABLE (Standardized Mean Differences)")
    print("=" * 55)
    balance = []
    for cov in covariates:
        treated = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 1, cov]
        control = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 0, cov]
        pooled_sd = np.sqrt((treated.var() + control.var()) / 2)
        smd = (treated.mean() - control.mean()) / pooled_sd
        _, pval = ttest_ind(treated, control)
        balance.append({
            "covariate": cov,
            "mean_treated": treated.mean(),
            "mean_control": control.mean(),
            "SMD": round(smd, 3),
            "p_value": round(pval, 4),
            "balanced": "✅" if abs(smd) < 0.1 else "⚠️"
        })
    print(pd.DataFrame(balance).to_string(index=False))

# ── 3. ITT estimate (effect of assignment) ───────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("ITT: Effect of Assignment")
print("=" * 55)

itt_model = smf.ols("outcome ~ assigned_treatment", data=df).fit(
    cov_type="HC3"  # heteroskedasticity-robust SEs
)

itt_est = itt_model.params["assigned_treatment"]
itt_ci  = itt_model.conf_int().loc["assigned_treatment"]
itt_p   = itt_model.pvalues["assigned_treatment"]

print(f"ITT estimate:  {itt_est:+.4f}")
print(f"95% CI:        [{itt_ci[0]:+.4f}, {itt_ci[1]:+.4f}]")
print(f"p-value:       {itt_p:.4f}")

# ── 4. CACE/LATE via 2SLS (effect of receipt, for compliers) ─────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("CACE/LATE: 2SLS (Assignment instruments Receipt)")
print("=" * 55)

iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    "outcome ~ 1 + [received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment]",
    data=df
).fit(cov_type="robust")

cace_est = iv_model.params["received_treatment"]
cace_ci  = iv_model.conf_int().loc["received_treatment"]
cace_p   = iv_model.pvalues["received_treatment"]

print(f"CACE/LATE estimate: {cace_est:+.4f}")
print(f"95% CI:             [{cace_ci[0]:+.4f}, {cace_ci[1]:+.4f}]")
print(f"p-value:            {cace_p:.4f}")

# ── 5. First-stage F-statistic (instrument strength check) ───────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("FIRST STAGE: Instrument Strength")
print("=" * 55)

first_stage = smf.ols("received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment", data=df).fit()
f_stat = first_stage.fvalue
print(f"First-stage F-statistic: {f_stat:.2f}")
print("Rule of thumb: F > 10 required, F > 100 preferred")
if f_stat < 10:
    print("⚠️  WEAK INSTRUMENT — CACE estimate is unreliable")
elif f_stat < 100:
    print("⚠️  Adequate but not strong — interpret CACE with some caution")
else:
    print("✅  Strong instrument")

# ── 6. Quick ITT vs CACE comparison ─────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("SUMMARY")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"  ITT  (effect of assignment):  {itt_est:+.4f}")
print(f"  CACE (effect of receipt):     {cace_est:+.4f}")
print(f"\n  Implied compliance rate: {comply_rate:.1%} - {contam_rate:.1%}")
print(f"  CACE ≈ ITT / compliance gap  → {itt_est / (comply_rate - contam_rate):+.4f}  (sanity check)")
```

---

## Falsification

Run a **permutation test** to confirm the ITT is not a false positive:

```python
from scipy.stats import permutation_test

def itt_statistic(treatment, outcome, axis):
    return np.mean(outcome[treatment == 1]) - np.mean(outcome[treatment == 0])

result = permutation_test(
    (df["assigned_treatment"].values, df["outcome"].values),
    statistic=lambda t, o, axis: np.mean(o[t == 1]) - np.mean(o[t == 0]),
    n_resamples=5000,
    alternative="two-sided"
)

print(f"Observed ITT: {itt_est:+.4f}")
print(f"Permutation p-value: {result.pvalue:.4f}")
```

---

## How to interpret your results

Once you run this, here's the framework:

| Estimate | What it answers | When to report it |
|---|---|---|
| **ITT** | "What's the effect of *offering* treatment?" | Always — policy-relevant if you can't force compliance |
| **CACE** | "What's the effect of *taking* treatment, for compliers?" | When you care about the biological/behavioral effect of the treatment itself |

**CACE > ITT** is expected with imperfect compliance — the IV scales up the ITT by dividing by the compliance gap. If your first-stage F is strong (≥ 100), trust the CACE. If it's weak, lean on the ITT.

Share your output and I'll help you write up the interpretation.

</details>

### iv_strong_instrument

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

# IV Analysis: Draft Lottery → Military Service → Earnings

Classic Angrist setup — one of the cleanest natural experiments in economics. Let's go through all 5 stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

| Element | Variable |
|---|---|
| **Instrument (Z)** | `draft_number` (lottery-assigned → exogenous) |
| **Treatment (D)** | `served` (binary) |
| **Outcome (Y)** | `earnings` |
| **Variant** | Standard 2SLS, single instrument |

**One thing to confirm before coding**: draft numbers work in one direction — *lower* numbers were called first, so higher `draft_number` should *reduce* service probability. Your first-stage coefficient should be **negative**. If the sign is positive in your data, `draft_number` may be coded inversely (e.g., rank-ordered differently). Keep an eye on that.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. Relevance — *Testable ✓*

> Does `draft_number` actually predict `served`?

This is the one assumption you can directly test. The lottery assigned numbers 1–365 (or similar); lower numbers faced higher induction risk, so they serve at higher rates. We need a strong first-stage F-stat — **above 10 at minimum, ideally above 100** (Lee et al. 2022 showed the old F > 10 rule is too lenient for reliable inference).

*We'll test this in the code.*

### 2. Exclusion Restriction — *Not directly testable ⚠️*

> Does `draft_number` affect `earnings` **only** through `served`?

This must be argued on substance. The lottery was random, so the number itself shouldn't predict earnings except via service. But ask yourself: could a low draft number affect earnings through any other path?

- Did men with low numbers invest less in education *anticipating* service (even if they ultimately didn't serve)?
- Did draft avoidance behaviors (emigrating, grad school enrollment) have their own earnings effects?

These are the classic concerns in this literature. In the original Angrist (1990) paper, the exclusion restriction was treated as highly plausible — a random number shouldn't affect your earnings except by determining whether you served. **I'd rate this as the strongest IV exclusion restriction argument in economics.**

### 3. Independence — *Partially testable ✓*

> Was `draft_number` truly randomly assigned — uncorrelated with unobserved earnings determinants?

The Vietnam lottery was random by design, so this is very credible. But the 1969 lottery had a known mechanical flaw where December birthdays got lower numbers (the capsules weren't well-mixed). If your data has birth month, you'd want to check balance.

*We'll do a simple balance check in the code.*

### 4. Monotonicity — *Not directly testable, plausible ✓*

> Does a lower draft number increase (never decrease) service probability for everyone?

This rules out "defiers" — men who would serve *if* their number was high but *not* if it was low. That's implausible here: the lottery determined induction orders, so a lower number can only increase your probability of being called up. **Monotonicity is highly credible in this design.**

**LATE interpretation**: Your IV estimate will be the effect of service for **compliers** — men who served *because* their draft number was low, and would not have served otherwise. It does not capture the effect for volunteers (always-takers) or those who avoided service regardless (never-takers).

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 0. Load data ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")  # adjust path

# ── 1. Quick look ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
summary(df[, c("draft_number", "served", "earnings")])
cat("Service rate:", mean(df$served), "\n")

# ── 2. First stage: does draft_number predict service? ────────────────────────
first_stage <- feols(served ~ draft_number, data = df)
summary(first_stage)

# Extract F-statistic
f_stat <- fitstat(first_stage, "f")$f$stat
cat("\nFirst-stage F-statistic:", round(f_stat, 2), "\n")
cat("Rule of thumb: F > 10 (Stock-Yogo); ideally F > 100 (Lee et al. 2022)\n")

# Expected: negative coefficient — higher draft number → less likely to serve
cat("First-stage coefficient on draft_number:",
    round(coef(first_stage)["draft_number"], 5), "\n")

# ── 3. Naive OLS (biased benchmark) ──────────────────────────────────────────
ols_model <- feols(earnings ~ served, data = df)

# ── 4. 2SLS / IV estimation ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
# fixest syntax: outcome ~ exogenous_controls | endogenous ~ instrument
iv_model <- feols(earnings ~ 1 | served ~ draft_number, data = df)
summary(iv_model)

# ── 5. Side-by-side comparison ────────────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  list("OLS (biased)" = ols_model, "IV / 2SLS" = iv_model),
  stars    = TRUE,
  gof_map  = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title    = "Effect of Military Service on Earnings"
)

# ── 6. Confidence interval for IV estimate ────────────────────────────────────
iv_coef <- coef(iv_model)["fit_served"]
iv_se   <- se(iv_model)["fit_served"]
ci_low  <- iv_coef - 1.96 * iv_se
ci_high <- iv_coef + 1.96 * iv_se
cat(sprintf("\nIV estimate: %.1f (95%% CI: %.1f, %.1f)\n",
            iv_coef, ci_low, ci_high))
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

Three checks I recommend for this design:

```r
# ── Check 1: Reduced form ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Regress earnings directly on draft_number (skips treatment entirely).
# If the instrument is valid and relevant, this should show a significant
# effect. It's more robust than 2SLS because it makes no structural assumptions.
reduced_form <- feols(earnings ~ draft_number, data = df)
summary(reduced_form)
# Expected: negative coefficient — lower draft number → lower earnings
# (because it increases service, and service reduces earnings)

# The IV estimate ≈ reduced-form coef / first-stage coef (Wald estimator)
cat("\nWald check — ratio of reduced form to first stage:\n")
rf_coef <- coef(reduced_form)["draft_number"]
fs_coef <- coef(first_stage)["draft_number"]
cat("Reduced form / First stage =", round(rf_coef / fs_coef, 2),
    " (should ≈ IV estimate)\n")

# ── Check 2: Balance on a pseudo-covariate ────────────────────────────────────
# Draft number should NOT predict pre-treatment characteristics.
# We don't have covariates here, but if you have birth year or any
# pre-service variable, run this:
#
# balance_check <- feols(birth_year ~ draft_number, data = df)
# summary(balance_check)
# Expected: near-zero, insignificant coefficient

# ── Check 3: Binary instrument (robustness) ───────────────────────────────────
# Create a "low draft number" indicator using median split.
# The IV estimate should be similar regardless of how we code the instrument.
df$low_draft <- as.integer(df$draft_number <= median(df$draft_number))

iv_binary <- feols(earnings ~ 1 | served ~ low_draft, data = df)

modelsummary(
  list("IV (continuous Z)" = iv_model, "IV (binary Z)" = iv_binary),
  stars   = TRUE,
  title   = "Robustness: Continuous vs. Binary Instrument"
)
# Stable estimates across both formulations → reassuring
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you run the code, fill in the blanks:

> "Using lottery-assigned draft number as an instrument for military service, the IV estimate suggests that military service **[increased/decreased]** annual earnings by **$[IV coefficient]** (95% CI: **[$lower, $upper]**).
>
> For comparison, the naive OLS estimate was **$[OLS coefficient]**. The gap between OLS and IV suggests **[positive selection bias / negative selection bias]** — men who served were **[more/less]** positively selected on unobservables than those who didn't.
>
> The first-stage F-statistic is **[F]**, indicating a **[strong/weak]** instrument.
>
> **Scope**: This is a Local Average Treatment Effect (LATE) — it applies to *compliers*, men whose service was determined by their lottery number. It does not generalize to volunteers or those who avoided service regardless of their number."

**Typical finding in this literature** (Angrist 1990): military service *reduced* earnings by ~15%, and OLS is upward biased because volunteers selected into service tend to have lower outside options.

- If F is weak (< 10), we need to talk about alternatives before proceeding
