### iv_weak_instrument

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

Great — this is a classic Card (1995) design. Here's what we're estimating:

| Role | Variable |
|---|---|
| Instrument (Z) | `distance` |
| Endogenous treatment (D) | `education` |
| Outcome (Y) | `earnings` |

A few quick questions before I generate code:

1. **Covariates?** Do you have variables like family background, race, region, or age? Distance to college famously proxies for urban/rural differences — controls can help shore up the exclusion restriction.
2. **Cross-sectional?** Looks like it, but confirming — no repeated observations per individual?

I'll proceed assuming no covariates for now (you can add them later), and flag where they'd slot in.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific setup.

---

### 1. Relevance ✅ (Testable)

**What it means here:** Living farther from a college must meaningfully *reduce* years of schooling. If the correlation is tiny, your IV estimate will be noisy and biased.

**Why it matters:** A weak instrument biases IV *toward* OLS — the very thing you're trying to fix. The rule of thumb is F > 10; modern standards recommend F > 100.

**Verdict:** Plausible. Distance is a real barrier to college attendance. But check the F-stat — the instrument's strength can vary a lot by sample.

---

### 2. Exclusion Restriction ⚠️ (NOT testable — must argue)

**What it means here:** Distance to college must affect earnings *only* through its effect on years of schooling — no direct path.

**Why it matters:** If distance is violated here, your IV estimate is inconsistent regardless of sample size.

**The classic threat:** Distance proxies for **urban vs. rural**. Cities have both more colleges (low distance) *and* higher-paying jobs. If that's true, distance has a direct effect on earnings that bypasses education entirely, and your IV is invalid.

**Question to ask yourself:** Could someone who lives far from a college earn less *even holding education constant*? If yes (e.g., rural labor markets pay less), the exclusion restriction is in trouble.

**Verdict:** 🚨 This is the weakest point of this instrument. You should note this caveat prominently. Controls for urbanicity/region help but don't fully fix it.

---

### 3. Independence ⚠️ (Partially testable)

**What it means here:** Where someone grew up (distance to college) should be unrelated to their unobserved ability, family wealth, or motivation.

**The threat:** Wealthy families cluster near universities. If distance correlates with parental education or income — and those affect earnings directly — the instrument is endogenous.

**Verdict:** Partially concerning. Card (1995) argued it holds *conditional on family background controls*. Without covariates, this is harder to defend.

---

### 4. Monotonicity ✅ (Plausible)

**What it means here:** Closer distance should increase education for everyone, or at least never *decrease* it for anyone. No "defiers."

**Verdict:** Very plausible. It's hard to construct a story where being closer to college causes someone to get *less* education.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Relevance | ✅ Check F-stat | Likely fine, verify |
| Exclusion restriction | ⚠️ Fragile | Urban/rural confounding is real |
| Independence | ⚠️ Moderate | Controls help significantly |
| Monotonicity | ✅ Plausible | No major concern |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 1. First Stage: Check instrument relevance ──────────────────────────────
first_stage = smf.ols('education ~ distance', data=df).fit()
print("=" * 60)
print("FIRST STAGE: education ~ distance")
print("=" * 60)
print(first_stage.summary())
print(f"\nFirst-stage F-statistic: {first_stage.fvalue:.2f}")
print(f"Rule of thumb: F > 10 (weak instrument threshold)")
print(f"Modern standard: F > 100 (Lee et al. 2022)")

# ── 2. Naive OLS (biased benchmark) ─────────────────────────────────────────
ols_model = smf.ols('earnings ~ education', data=df).fit()
print("\n" + "=" * 60)
print("NAIVE OLS: earnings ~ education  (biased — for comparison)")
print("=" * 60)
print(ols_model.summary())

# ── 3. 2SLS / IV Estimation ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    'earnings ~ 1 + [education ~ distance]',
    data=df
).fit(cov_type='robust')

print("\n" + "=" * 60)
print("2SLS IV ESTIMATE: earnings ~ education (instrumented by distance)")
print("=" * 60)
print(iv_model.summary)

# ── 4. Side-by-side comparison ───────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 60)
print("COMPARISON: OLS vs IV/2SLS")
print("=" * 60)
ols_coef = ols_model.params['education']
ols_ci   = ols_model.conf_int().loc['education']
iv_coef  = iv_model.params['education']
iv_ci    = iv_model.conf_int().loc['education']

print(f"{'':20} {'Coefficient':>14} {'95% CI':>25}")
print(f"{'OLS':20} {ols_coef:>14.2f} ({ols_ci[0]:>10.2f}, {ols_ci[1]:>10.2f})")
print(f"{'IV / 2SLS':20} {iv_coef:>14.2f} ({iv_ci['lower']:>10.2f}, {iv_ci['upper']:>10.2f})")

bias_direction = "upward" if iv_coef < ols_coef else "downward"
print(f"\nOLS appears {bias_direction} biased relative to IV.")
print("(Expected: upward bias if higher-ability people get more education)")

# ── 5. First-stage visual ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 4))
ax.scatter(df['distance'], df['education'], alpha=0.3, s=15, color='steelblue')
x_range = np.linspace(df['distance'].min(), df['distance'].max(), 200)
ax.plot(x_range,
        first_stage.params['Intercept'] + first_stage.params['distance'] * x_range,
        color='crimson', linewidth=2, label='First stage fit')
ax.set_xlabel('Distance to College')
ax.set_ylabel('Years of Education')
ax.set_title('First Stage: Distance → Education')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('first_stage.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()
```

> **To add covariates** (strongly recommended): replace the formula strings with:
> ```python
> 'education ~ distance + age + urban + parent_educ'   # first stage
> 'earnings ~ 1 + age + urban + parent_educ + [education ~ distance]'  # IV
> ```

## Stage 4: Falsification

Three checks I'd recommend for this design:

```python
# ── Check 1: Reduced form ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Regress earnings directly on distance (no 2nd stage).
# If IV is valid + relevant, this should be significant and negative
# (farther distance → less education → lower earnings).
reduced_form = smf.ols('earnings ~ distance', data=df).fit()
print("REDUCED FORM: earnings ~ distance")
print(reduced_form.summary())
# IV estimate ≈ reduced_form_coef / first_stage_coef
implied_iv = reduced_form.params['distance'] / first_stage.params['distance']
print(f"\nImplied IV from ratio: {implied_iv:.2f}  (should match 2SLS above)")

# ── Check 2: Stability to controls ──────────────────────────────────────────
# If you have covariates, rerun IV adding them one group at a time.
# A stable coefficient across specs supports the exclusion restriction.
# Example (if you have an 'urban' column):
# iv_with_urban = IV2SLS.from_formula(
#     'earnings ~ 1 + urban + [education ~ distance]', data=df
# ).fit(cov_type='robust')

# ── Check 3: Placebo outcome ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
# If you have a pre-treatment outcome (e.g., parent earnings), distance
# should NOT predict it. A significant effect would suggest confounding.
# iv_placebo = IV2SLS.from_formula(
#     'parent_earnings ~ 1 + [education ~ distance]', data=df
# ).fit(cov_type='robust')
```

The [upward/downward] difference suggests OLS was [over/under]estimating the return to education, consistent with [ability bias / selection story].

### matching_good_overlap

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1 — Setup Confirmed

You're estimating the **ATE** of `treatment` on `outcome`, conditioning on `x1` and `x2`, using Python. One important clarification before we touch code:

> ⚠️ **PSM naturally estimates the ATT** (effect on the treated), not the ATE. For ATE, every unit needs a counterfactual — treated units need matched controls *and* control units need matched treateds. The cleaner solution for ATE is **Inverse Probability Weighting (IPW)**, which re-weights the full sample. I'll implement both so you can see the difference.

---

## Stage 2 — Assumptions

Let's walk through each one for your specific setup.

---

### 1. Conditional Independence (CIA) 🔴 *Untestable — must argue from domain knowledge*

> "After conditioning on `x1` and `x2`, is treatment assignment independent of potential outcomes?"

You told me treatment was assigned *based on* `x1` and `x2` — that's a good sign. But the key question is: **are there other variables that drove assignment that you haven't measured?**

For example:
- Did a human decision-maker use additional information beyond `x1` and `x2`?
- Is there a self-selection mechanism (e.g., people opted in) where unobserved motivation matters?

If you're confident that `x1` and `x2` fully capture the selection process, CIA is plausible. If not, no amount of matching will fix omitted variable bias.

We'll run a **coefficient stability check** and compute **E-values** in Stage 4 to quantify how fragile the result is.

---

### 2. Overlap / Positivity 🟡 *Testable — we'll check this in Stage 3*

> "Does every unit (with any combination of `x1`, `x2` values) have a nonzero probability of being treated or untreated?"

With only two continuous covariates and n=1000, overlap is often good — but extreme `x1`/`x2` values can push propensity scores near 0 or 1. We'll plot this explicitly.

---

### 3. SUTVA (No Interference) 🟢 *Plausible if units are independent*

> "Could one person's treatment affect another person's outcome?"

With individual-level data and no mention of network/geographic structure, this is likely satisfied. Just confirm: are these individuals independent (no households, clusters, or social connections that matter for the outcome)?

---

### 4. Correct Model Specification 🟡 *Partially testable via balance diagnostics*

> "Is the logistic regression propensity score model correctly capturing the selection process?"

We'll check **covariate balance (SMD)** after weighting. If balance is poor, we'll add polynomial/interaction terms. We can also cross-validate the propensity model or use a gradient boosted classifier.

---

## Stage 3 — Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.gridspec as gridspec

# ── 0. Load your data ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")   # ← uncomment and point to your file
covariates = ['x1', 'x2']

# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
# STEP 1: PROPENSITY SCORE ESTIMATION
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
ps_model.fit(df[covariates], df['treatment'])
df['ps'] = ps_model.predict_proba(df[covariates])[:, 1]

print("Propensity score summary:")
print(df.groupby('treatment')['ps'].describe().round(3))

# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
# STEP 2: OVERLAP / POSITIVITY CHECK
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 4))
ax.hist(df.loc[df['treatment'] == 1, 'ps'], bins=40,
        alpha=0.55, label='Treated', density=True, color='tomato')
ax.hist(df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, 'ps'], bins=40,
        alpha=0.55, label='Control', density=True, color='steelblue')
ax.axvline(0.05, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=0.9, label='Trim threshold (0.05 / 0.95)')
ax.axvline(0.95, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=0.9)
ax.set_xlabel('Propensity Score')
ax.set_ylabel('Density')
ax.set_title('Overlap Check: Propensity Score Distribution by Group')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('overlap_check.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# Flag extreme scores — poor overlap region
n_extreme = ((df['ps'] < 0.05) | (df['ps'] > 0.95)).sum()
print(f"\nUnits with PS outside [0.05, 0.95]: {n_extreme} ({n_extreme/len(df)*100:.1f}%)")
# If this is large (>10%), consider trimming before proceeding.

# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
# STEP 3: IPW WEIGHTS FOR ATE
#   - Treated weight:  1 / ps
#   - Control weight:  1 / (1 - ps)
#   - Stabilized to reduce variance from extreme weights
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
p_treat = df['treatment'].mean()   # marginal probability of treatment

df['ipw_ate'] = np.where(
    df['treatment'] == 1,
    p_treat / df['ps'],                      # stabilized weight for treated
    (1 - p_treat) / (1 - df['ps'])           # stabilized weight for controls
)

# Sanity check: weights should sum to roughly n in each group
print("\nWeight summary (ATE):")
print(df.groupby('treatment')['ipw_ate'].describe().round(3))

# Trim extreme weights at 99th percentile to reduce variance
p99 = df['ipw_ate'].quantile(0.99)
df['ipw_ate_trim'] = df['ipw_ate'].clip(upper=p99)
print(f"\nWeight trimming: capped at {p99:.2f} (99th percentile)")

# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
# STEP 4: COVARIATE BALANCE DIAGNOSTICS
#   Target: all |SMD| < 0.1 after weighting
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
def weighted_smd(df, covariate, weight_col):
    """Standardized Mean Difference using weighted means."""
    t = df[df['treatment'] == 1]
    c = df[df['treatment'] == 0]

    mean_t = np.average(t[covariate], weights=t[weight_col])
    mean_c = np.average(c[covariate], weights=c[weight_col])

    # Pooled SD from unweighted data (standard denominator)
    pooled_sd = np.sqrt((t[covariate].std()**2 + c[covariate].std()**2) / 2)
    return (mean_t - mean_c) / pooled_sd

balance = []
for cov in covariates:
    # Unweighted SMD (before)
    t_raw = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 1, cov]
    c_raw = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, cov]
    pooled_sd = np.sqrt((t_raw.std()**2 + c_raw.std()**2) / 2)
    smd_before = (t_raw.mean() - c_raw.mean()) / pooled_sd

    # Weighted SMD (after IPW)
    smd_after = weighted_smd(df, cov, 'ipw_ate_trim')

    balance.append({
        'Covariate':   cov,
        'SMD (before)': round(smd_before, 3),
        'SMD (after)':  round(smd_after, 3),
        'Balanced':     abs(smd_after) < 0.1
    })

balance_df = pd.DataFrame(balance)
print("\nCovariate Balance:")
print(balance_df.to_string(index=False))
print(f"\nAll balanced |SMD| < 0.1: {balance_df['Balanced'].all()}")

# Love Plot
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 3.5))
y_pos = range(len(covariates))
ax.scatter(balance_df['SMD (before)'], y_pos, label='Before weighting',
           marker='o', color='tomato', s=90, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(balance_df['SMD (after)'],  y_pos, label='After IPW',
           marker='D', color='steelblue', s=90, zorder=3)
ax.axvline( 0.1, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(-0.1, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline( 0.0, color='black', linewidth=0.5)
ax.set_yticks(list(y_pos))
ax.set_yticklabels(covariates)
ax.set_xlabel('Standardized Mean Difference')
ax.set_title('Love Plot: Covariate Balance Before vs. After IPW')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('love_plot.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
# STEP 5: ATE ESTIMATION
#   Weighted regression with covariate adjustment (doubly-robust flavor)
#   → Consistent if EITHER the PS model OR the outcome model is correct
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
model = smf.wls(
    'outcome ~ treatment + x1 + x2',
    data=df,
    weights=df['ipw_ate_trim']
).fit(cov_type='HC3')   # heteroscedasticity-robust standard errors

ate   = model.params['treatment']
ci_lo = model.conf_int().loc['treatment', 0]
ci_hi = model.conf_int().loc['treatment', 1]
pval  = model.pvalues['treatment']

print(f"\n{'─'*45}")
print(f"  ATE estimate : {ate:.3f}")
print(f"  95% CI       : [{ci_lo:.3f}, {ci_hi:.3f}]")
print(f"  p-value      : {pval:.4f}")
print(f"{'─'*45}")
```

---

## Stage 4 — Robustness Checks

Two checks I'd recommend for your setup:

### Check A — Coefficient Stability (CIA plausibility)
```python
# If the treatment effect changes a lot as you add controls,
# it suggests unobserved confounders could change it further.
m_sparse = smf.ols('outcome ~ treatment', data=df).fit()
m_full   = smf.ols('outcome ~ treatment + x1 + x2', data=df).fit()

print(f"Treatment effect (no controls): {m_sparse.params['treatment']:.3f}")
print(f"Treatment effect (x1, x2):      {m_full.params['treatment']:.3f}")
print(f"Absolute change:                {abs(m_sparse.params['treatment'] - m_full.params['treatment']):.3f}")
# Large change → controls matter a lot → be cautious about unobservables
```

### Check B — Placebo Outcome
```python
# Create a fake pre-treatment outcome that treatment should NOT affect.
# Here we use a random permutation as a placeholder — replace with a
# real pre-treatment variable if you have one.
np.random.seed(42)
df['placebo_outcome'] = np.random.normal(0, 1, len(df))

model_placebo = smf.wls(
    'placebo_outcome ~ treatment + x1 + x2',
    data=df,
    weights=df['ipw_ate_trim']
).fit(cov_type='HC3')

print(f"\nPlacebo ATE: {model_placebo.params['treatment']:.3f}")
print(f"p-value:     {model_placebo.pvalues['treatment']:.4f}")
# Should be near zero and non-significant.
# A "significant" placebo effect is a red flag for residual confounding.
```

---

## Stage 5 — Interpreting Results

Once you run the code, here's how to read the output:

| Output | What it tells you |
|---|---|
| **Overlap plot** | If the PS distributions of treated/control barely overlap → results are fragile |
| **Love plot / SMD table** | All `\|SMD\| < 0.1` after weighting = good balance; remaining imbalance = propensity model needs refinement |
| **ATE + 95% CI** | The estimated average effect of treatment across *all* units (not just the treated) |
| **Coefficient stability** | Large change when adding controls = CIA is doing heavy lifting = be cautious |
| **Placebo outcome** | Near-zero, non-significant = reassuring; significant = residual confounding likely |

**Critical caveat**: this estimate is credible only if `x1` and `x2` capture the *full* selection mechanism. If treatment was also influenced by unobserved factors that affect `outcome`, the ATE will be biased. There's no test for this — it's a domain knowledge judgment call.

| Element | Value |

Before we move to assumptions and code, I need three more things:

---

**Q1 — Estimand**: Do you want the **ATT** (effect *on participants* — "did training help those who took it?") or the **ATE** (effect *on everyone* — "what if we rolled this out to all employees?")?

> Given self-selection, ATT is usually the more defensible choice: you're comparing participants to similar non-participants, not extrapolating to people who may be very different.

---

**Q2 — Selection mechanism**: Why did some employees join the training and others didn't? For example:
- Was it voluntary sign-up?
- Did managers nominate people?
- Was it offered to certain departments first?

Understanding *why* people selected in helps us judge whether age + tenure + department can plausibly capture all the confounding.

---

**Q3 — Unobserved confounders**: Can you think of variables that (a) predict who joins the training **and** (b) independently affect productivity — that you *don't* have in your data?

> Classic examples: motivation, prior performance, manager quality, team assignment. If these exist and are unmeasured, matching can't fix that — and we'd need to be explicit about the limitation.

### rdd_clean_cutoff

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Great — this is a clean **Sharp RDD**. Let me confirm what I'm working with:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Running variable | `test_score` |
| Cutoff | 70 |
| Treatment | `scholarship` (deterministic at cutoff) |
| Outcome | `gpa` |
| N | 2,000 students |
| Design | Sharp RDD |

The `scholarship` column already matches what we'd expect — 0 below 70, 1 above — so no fuzzy IV layer needed. On to assumptions.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. No Manipulation of the Running Variable
**What it means here**: Could students have known the exact cutoff (70) and found a way to push their score just above it — e.g., through appeals, rounding, or retakes? If so, students at 70–71 are systematically different from students at 68–69.

You said the distribution looks smooth — that's encouraging. We'll confirm formally with a **McCrary density test**.

**Plausibility check**: Were students aware of the 70-point threshold before taking the test? Was there any retake or appeal mechanism?

---

### 2. Continuity of Potential Outcomes at the Cutoff
**What it means here**: A student scoring 69.9 and one scoring 70.1 should be nearly identical *in everything except scholarship receipt*. Their GPAs, absent the scholarship, should be on a smooth curve through the cutoff.

**Testable**: We'll run `rdrobust` on any pre-determined covariates (e.g., prior GPA, demographics) as fake outcomes. They should show no jump at 70.

Your data only shows `test_score`, `scholarship`, and `gpa` — do you have any **pre-determined covariates** (e.g., baseline GPA, demographics, income)? If not, we'll rely on the density test and visual inspection.

---

### 3. No Compound Treatments at the Cutoff
**What it means here**: Is the scholarship the *only* thing that changes at score = 70? For example, does the same score also determine class placement, tutoring eligibility, or some other program?

This is **not testable from the data alone** — it requires institutional knowledge. Does anything else hinge on the 70-point threshold?

---

### 4. Local Identification (Inherent Limitation)
**What it means here**: The RDD will estimate the scholarship's effect on GPA **for students scoring near 70** — not for students scoring 50 or 90. If your policy question is about the program's effect on all students, RDD only partially answers it.

This isn't a violation — just important context for interpretation.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| No manipulation | ⚠️ Unverified | McCrary density test |
| Continuity at cutoff | ⚠️ Unverified | Covariate smoothness test (if covariates available) |
| No compound treatments | ❓ Institutional knowledge needed | Ask: anything else changes at 70? |
| Local identification | ✅ Inherent — just interpret carefully | — |

No fatal flags yet. Let's proceed to code.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from rdrobust import rdrobust, rdplot
from rddensity import rddensity, rdplotdensity

# ── 0. Setup ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cutoff = 70
Y = df['gpa'].values
X = df['test_score'].values

# ── 1. RD Plot — Visual inspection of the discontinuity ──────────────────────
# Look for a visible jump in GPA at test_score = 70
rdplot(Y, X, c=cutoff,
       title='RD Plot: GPA by Test Score',
       x_label='Test Score',
       y_label='GPA')
plt.show()

# ── 2. McCrary Density Test — Check for manipulation ─────────────────────────
# H0: density of test_score is continuous at cutoff
# Rejection (p < 0.05) suggests students sorted around 70
density_test = rddensity(X, c=cutoff)
print("\n── McCrary Density Test ──")
print(density_test)

# Visual density plot
rdplotdensity(density_test, X,
              title='Density of Test Score Around Cutoff',
              xlabel='Test Score',
              ylabel='Density')
plt.show()

# ── 3. Main RD Estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Uses local linear regression with MSE-optimal bandwidth
# Inference is bias-corrected and robust (Calonico, Cattaneo, Titiunik 2014)
rd_main = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff)
print("\n── Main RD Estimate ──")
print(rd_main)

# ── 4. Quick histogram — eyeball the distribution around 70 ──────────────────
plt.figure(figsize=(10, 5))
plt.hist(df['test_score'], bins=40, color='steelblue', edgecolor='white')
plt.axvline(x=cutoff, color='red', linestyle='--', linewidth=1.5, label='Cutoff (70)')
plt.xlabel('Test Score')
plt.ylabel('Count')
plt.title('Distribution of Test Scores')
plt.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()
```

**Key outputs to look for:**
- **RD plot**: A visible jump in average GPA at score = 70
- **Density test p-value**: > 0.05 → no evidence of manipulation ✓
- **RD estimate**: The coefficient is your causal estimate — the effect of receiving the scholarship on GPA, for students near the cutoff
- **Bandwidth (h)**: How many points around 70 were used (e.g., h = 8 means students scoring 62–78)

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

```python
# ── 1. Bandwidth Sensitivity ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
# The estimate should be stable across different bandwidths
# Large swings suggest the local linear fit is sensitive to bandwidth choice

opt_bw = rd_main.bws.values[0][0]  # optimal bandwidth from rdrobust
multipliers = [0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.25, 1.5]

print("\n── Bandwidth Sensitivity ──")
print(f"{'Bandwidth':>12} {'Estimate':>10} {'CI Lower':>10} {'CI Upper':>10} {'N (effective)':>15}")
print("-" * 60)

for m in multipliers:
    bw = opt_bw * m
    rd_bw = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff, h=bw)
    coef  = rd_bw.coef.values[0][0]
    ci_lo = rd_bw.ci.values[0][0]
    ci_hi = rd_bw.ci.values[0][1]
    n_eff = int(rd_bw.N_h.values[0][0]) + int(rd_bw.N_h.values[0][1])
    label = f"{bw:.2f} ({'optimal' if m == 1.0 else f'{int(m*100)}%'})"
    print(f"{label:>20} {coef:>10.4f} {ci_lo:>10.4f} {ci_hi:>10.4f} {n_eff:>15}")


# ── 2. Placebo Cutoffs ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Run the RDD at score values where there is NO treatment change
# Finding a "significant" effect at a placebo cutoff is a red flag

placebo_cutoffs = [55, 60, 65, 75, 80, 85]

print("\n── Placebo Cutoff Tests ──")
print(f"{'Cutoff':>10} {'Estimate':>10} {'p-value':>10}")
print("-" * 35)

for pc in placebo_cutoffs:
    try:
        rd_p = rdrobust(Y, X, c=pc)
        coef    = rd_p.coef.values[0][0]
        pval    = rd_p.pv.values[0][0]   # robust p-value
        flag    = " ⚠️" if pval < 0.05 else ""
        print(f"{pc:>10} {coef:>10.4f} {pval:>10.4f}{flag}")
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"{pc:>10} {'error':>10} — {e}")


# ── 3. Polynomial Order Sensitivity ──────────────────────────────────────────
# Local linear (p=1) vs. local quadratic (p=2) should agree

print("\n── Polynomial Order Sensitivity ──")
for p in [1, 2]:
    rd_p = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff, p=p)
    coef  = rd_p.coef.values[0][0]
    ci_lo = rd_p.ci.values[0][0]
    ci_hi = rd_p.ci.values[0][1]
    print(f"p={p}: estimate = {coef:.4f}, 95% CI = [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
```

**What you want to see:**
- Bandwidth sensitivity: estimates stay in the same ballpark (same sign, overlapping CIs)
- Placebo cutoffs: no significant effects — if p < 0.05 at a placebo, investigate
- Polynomial: p=1 and p=2 estimates close to each other

### rdd_manipulation

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

This is a **Sharp RDD**. Treatment (`scholarship`) is deterministic at the cutoff — every student with `test_score >= 70` receives it. Here's the full setup:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Running variable | `test_score` |
| Cutoff | `70` |
| Treatment | `scholarship` |
| Outcome | `gpa` |
| Variant | Sharp RDD |
| N | 2,000 students |

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption for your specific context.

---

### 1. No Manipulation of the Running Variable
**What it means here**: Can students precisely control their test score to land just above 70? If a meaningful number of students can push their score from 69 → 70, then students just above and below the cutoff are no longer comparable — students above are systematically more motivated/strategic, not just randomly better.

**Why it matters**: If manipulation exists, students just above 70 aren't a valid counterfactual for students just below. The "as-if random" assignment breaks down.

**Your context**: Test scores often have natural measurement noise, which *helps* here. But if students can retake the test, or if graders have discretion near the cutoff, manipulation is plausible.

⚠️ **Needs testing** — we'll run the McCrary density test.

---

### 2. Continuity of Potential Outcomes
**What it means here**: In a world without the scholarship, GPA would change smoothly as test scores pass through 70. There's no inherent reason why a student scoring 70 would have a very different GPA trajectory than one scoring 69 — the *only* reason for a jump at 70 is the scholarship itself.

**Why it matters**: If something else also causes a jump at 70, your estimate captures the combined effect of all those things, not just the scholarship.

**Your context**: This is partially testable by checking if pre-determined covariates (e.g., demographics, prior grades, socioeconomic status) are smooth through 70.

⚠️ **Needs testing** — we'll run covariate smoothness checks (assuming you have covariates).

---

### 3. No Compound Treatments at the Cutoff
**What it means here**: Is the scholarship the *only* thing that changes at `test_score = 70`? Or does, say, placement into an honors program *also* use 70 as its threshold? If both kick in simultaneously, your RDD estimate captures the combined effect.

**Why it matters**: You'd be estimating "scholarship + honors track" rather than "scholarship alone."

🔴 **Action needed** — I need to ask: **Are there other programs, class placements, or institutional policies that also use the 70-point cutoff?**

---

### 4. Local Identification
**What it means here**: Your estimate will be the effect of the scholarship *for students scoring near 70* — not for students scoring 50 or 90. Students at the margin of winning a scholarship may respond differently than strong students who comfortably qualify.

**Why it matters**: The result is internally valid but has limited generalizability. A dean who asks "what's the effect of expanding scholarships?" is asking a different question than what RDD answers.

✅ **Not a violation** — just an important interpretation caveat.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Testable? |
|---|---|---|
| No manipulation | ⚠️ Needs density test | Yes |
| Continuity of potential outcomes | ⚠️ Needs covariate checks | Partially |
| No compound treatment at cutoff | 🔴 Needs your input | No (domain knowledge) |
| Local identification | ✅ Accepted caveat | N/A |

**One question before code**: Are there other programs or policies using the 70-point cutoff at your institution?

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Regardless — here's the full analysis code. Run this and we'll interpret the results together.

```r
library(rdrobust)
library(rddensity)
library(ggplot2)

# ── 0. Load and prep ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")  # load your data

cutoff <- 70
Y <- df$gpa
X <- df$test_score

# ── 1. Visual inspection ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Always start here — does a discontinuity visually exist at 70?
rdplot(
  y     = Y,
  x     = X,
  c     = cutoff,
  title   = "RD Plot: Effect of Scholarship on GPA",
  x.label = "Test Score",
  y.label = "GPA"
)

# ── 2. McCrary density test — check for score manipulation ───────────────────
# H0: density of test_score is continuous at 70 (no bunching)
density_test <- rddensity(X = X, c = cutoff)
summary(density_test)

# Visual: is there a suspicious spike just above 70?
rdplotdensity(
  rdd    = density_test,
  X      = X,
  title  = "McCrary Density Test",
  xlabel = "Test Score",
  ylabel = "Density"
)

# ── 3. Covariate smoothness checks ───────────────────────────────────────────
# Replace with your actual pre-determined covariates (demographics, prior GPA, etc.)
# These SHOULD NOT show a jump at 70 — if they do, something else is going on.

# Example (uncomment and replace column names):
# covariates <- c("age", "prior_gpa", "ses_index")
# for (cov in covariates) {
#   rd_cov <- rdrobust(y = df[[cov]], x = X, c = cutoff)
#   cat("\n--- Covariate balance check:", cov, "---\n")
#   summary(rd_cov)
# }

# ── 4. Main RD estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# rdrobust automatically selects an MSE-optimal bandwidth via cross-validation
# The 'robust' confidence interval corrects for bias from local polynomial fitting
rd_main <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff)
summary(rd_main)

# Pull out key numbers cleanly
cat("\n━━━ Key Results ━━━\n")
cat("RD Estimate (bias-corrected):", round(rd_main$coef[1], 4), "\n")
cat("Robust 95% CI: [",
    round(rd_main$ci[3, 1], 4), ",",
    round(rd_main$ci[3, 2], 4), "]\n")
cat("Optimal bandwidth (h):", round(rd_main$bws[1, 1], 2), "\n")
cat("Obs. within bandwidth — Left:",  rd_main$N_h[1],
    " | Right:", rd_main$N_h[2], "\n")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Run these after the main estimate. Each one probes a different fragility.

```r
# ── 1. Bandwidth sensitivity ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Does the estimate hold if we widen or narrow the window around 70?
# Results should be qualitatively stable — if they flip signs at 75% BW,
# that's a red flag.

opt_bw <- rd_main$bws[1, 1]
bw_multipliers <- c(0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.25, 1.5)

bw_results <- data.frame()
for (m in bw_multipliers) {
  bw <- opt_bw * m
  rd_bw <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff, h = bw)
  bw_results <- rbind(bw_results, data.frame(
    multiplier = m,
    bandwidth  = round(bw, 2),
    estimate   = round(rd_bw$coef[1], 4),
    ci_low     = round(rd_bw$ci[3, 1], 4),
    ci_high    = round(rd_bw$ci[3, 2], 4),
    n_left     = rd_bw$N_h[1],
    n_right    = rd_bw$N_h[2]
  ))
}
print(bw_results)

# ── 2. Placebo cutoffs ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Run the RDD at cutoffs where NO treatment change occurs (e.g., 55, 62, 78, 85)
# You should NOT find significant effects — if you do, the model is misspecified.

placebo_cutoffs <- c(55, 62, 78, 85)
cat("\n━━━ Placebo Cutoff Tests ━━━\n")
for (pc in placebo_cutoffs) {
  # Exclude observations within the real treatment window to avoid contamination
  df_placebo <- df[df$test_score < (cutoff - 5) | df$test_score > (cutoff + 5), ]
  rd_placebo <- rdrobust(
    y = df_placebo$gpa,
    x = df_placebo$test_score,
    c = pc
  )
  cat("Placebo cutoff:", pc,
      "| Estimate:", round(rd_placebo$coef[1], 4),
      "| p-value:", round(rd_placebo$pv[3], 3), "\n")
}

# ── 3. Polynomial order sensitivity ─────────────────────────────────────────
# Compare local linear (p=1, default) vs. local quadratic (p=2)
# Large differences suggest the functional form is doing a lot of work.

rd_p1 <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff, p = 1)
rd_p2 <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff, p = 2)

cat("\n━━━ Polynomial Order Sensitivity ━━━\n")
cat("Local Linear  (p=1): Estimate =", round(rd_p1$coef[1], 4),
    " | CI: [", round(rd_p1$ci[3,1], 4), ",", round(rd_p1$ci[3,2], 4), "]\n")
cat("Local Quadratic (p=2): Estimate =", round(rd_p2$coef[1], 4),
    " | CI: [", round(rd_p2$ci[3,1], 4), ",", round(rd_p2$ci[3,2], 4), "]\n")
```

### sc_poor_prefit

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Good setup. Let me confirm the key parameters I've extracted, then work through all five stages.

**What I'm reading from your setup:**
- 1 treated unit (State A), treatment at period 20
- 10 donor states, periods 1–40
- 19 pre-treatment periods → borderline (the method requires ≥10–20; you're at the minimum, so pre-fit quality will be important)
- 20 post-treatment periods → good
- No additional covariates mentioned (will use pre-treatment outcome lags)
- R → using `Synth` package

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption and what it means for your setup.

### ① No interference between units
**What it means**: Your 10 donor states' outcomes should not be affected by whatever policy State A enacted at period 20. If, say, State A's policy caused firms to relocate to donor states, those donor states' outcomes would shift — making the synthetic counterfactual look artificially good or bad.

**Testable?** Only suggestively — plot donor outcomes around period 20 and look for concurrent breaks.

**Your situation**: You'll need to argue this on substantive grounds. Do you know what the policy was and whether neighboring/trading-partner states could have been affected?

---

### ② Convex hull
**What it means**: State A's pre-treatment outcome trajectory needs to be *within* the range spanned by your 10 donors. If State A is an extreme outlier (much higher/lower than all donors), no weighted combination of donors can replicate it without extrapolating.

**Testable?** Yes — after fitting, check if pre-RMSPE is small and whether weights spread across several donors (vs. all weight on 1–2 units).

**If violated**: Switch to **augmented synthetic control** (`augsynth`), which adds ridge regularization to handle extrapolation. I'll show you that path if the standard model doesn't fit well.

---

### ③ Pre-treatment fit quality
**What it means**: The synthetic control must track State A's outcome closely during periods 1–19. If it can't replicate the past, you can't trust its prediction of the counterfactual future.

**Testable?** Yes — compute RMSPE over periods 1–19. Rule of thumb: RMSPE should be small relative to the outcome's scale (ideally < 10% of the SD). Your 19 pre-periods is workable but not generous — a noisy outcome could challenge the fit.

---

### ④ No structural breaks
**What it means**: The relationship between State A and its donors should be stable throughout the study window. If some external shock in period, say, 10 differentially affected State A but not the donors, the factor structure shifts even before treatment.

**Testable?** Yes — in-time placebo: pretend treatment happened at period 10. If a gap opens between periods 10–19, the relationship isn't stable.

---

### ⑤ No anticipation
**What it means**: State A's outcome should not start changing *before* period 20 in response to the policy (e.g., if it was announced well in advance). If the treated unit reacts early, the pre-period is contaminated and the synthetic control is trained on a period where treatment was already partially in effect.

**Your situation**: Does the pre-treatment trend look stable all the way to period 19? The trends plot in the code below will reveal this.

---

**Assumption summary:**

| Assumption | Testable? | Status |
|---|---|---|
| No interference | No (substantive) | ⚠️ Argue based on domain knowledge |
| Convex hull | Partially | 🔍 Check after fitting |
| Pre-treatment fit | Yes | 🔍 Check RMSPE — 19 periods is borderline |
| No structural breaks | Partially | 🔍 Run in-time placebo |
| No anticipation | Visually | 🔍 Inspect pre-period trend |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
library(Synth)
library(tidyverse)

# ── 0. Prepare data ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Synth requires numeric unit IDs — create them from the state column
df_synth <- df %>%
  mutate(state_id = as.integer(factor(state))) %>%
  as.data.frame()  # Synth does not accept tibbles

# Identify numeric IDs
treated_id <- df_synth %>% filter(state == "A") %>% pull(state_id) %>% unique()
donor_ids  <- df_synth %>% filter(state != "A") %>% pull(state_id) %>% unique() %>% sort()

cat("Treated unit ID:", treated_id, "\n")
cat("Donor unit IDs:", paste(donor_ids, collapse = ", "), "\n")

# Quick visual: pre-treatment trends (eyeball for anticipation + fit potential)
df_synth %>%
  mutate(group = if_else(state == "A", "State A", "Donors")) %>%
  group_by(period, group) %>%
  summarise(mean_outcome = mean(outcome), .groups = "drop") %>%
  ggplot(aes(x = period, y = mean_outcome, color = group)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 20, linetype = "dashed", color = "red") +
  labs(title = "Pre-treatment Trends: State A vs Donor Pool Average",
       x = "Period", y = "Outcome", color = NULL) +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 1. dataprep ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
dataprep_out <- dataprep(
  foo                   = df_synth,
  predictors            = "outcome",       # pre-treatment outcome mean as predictor
  predictors.op         = "mean",
  dependent             = "outcome",
  unit.variable         = "state_id",
  time.variable         = "period",
  treatment.identifier  = treated_id,
  controls.identifier   = donor_ids,
  time.predictors.prior = 1:19,            # pre-treatment window
  time.optimize.ssr     = 1:19,            # minimize MSPE over pre-treatment
  time.plot             = 1:40             # full study window
)

# ── 2. Estimate synthetic control ────────────────────────────────────────────
synth_out <- synth(dataprep_out)

# ── 3. Summary tables ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
synth_tables <- synth.tab(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out
)

# Donor weights — check for concentration on 1–2 units (convex hull concern)
cat("\n── Donor unit weights ──\n")
print(synth_tables$tab.w)

# Predictor balance — check if pre-treatment outcome mean is well matched
cat("\n── Predictor balance ──\n")
print(synth_tables$tab.pred)

# ── 4. Pre-treatment RMSPE ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
pre_gaps  <- dataprep_out$Y1plot[1:19] -
             (dataprep_out$Y0plot[1:19, , drop = FALSE] %*% synth_out$solution.w)
rmspe_pre <- sqrt(mean(pre_gaps^2))
outcome_sd <- sd(dataprep_out$Y1plot[1:19])

cat("\n── Pre-treatment fit ──\n")
cat("RMSPE (pre-treatment):", round(rmspe_pre, 4), "\n")
cat("Outcome SD (pre-treatment):", round(outcome_sd, 4), "\n")
cat("RMSPE / SD:", round(rmspe_pre / outcome_sd, 4),
    " (target: < 0.10)\n")

# ── 5. Plots ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# (a) Treated vs synthetic trajectory
path.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Outcome",
  Xlab         = "Period",
  Legend       = c("State A", "Synthetic State A"),
  Main         = "State A vs Synthetic Control"
)
abline(v = 20, lty = 2, col = "red")

# (b) Gap plot: treatment effect over time
gaps.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Gap (State A − Synthetic)",
  Xlab         = "Period",
  Main         = "Estimated Treatment Effect: State A"
)
abline(v = 20, lty = 2, col = "red")

# (c) Donor weight bar chart
synth_tables$tab.w %>%
  as.data.frame() %>%
  rownames_to_column("donor") %>%
  rename(weight = w.weights) %>%
  filter(weight > 0.001) %>%
  ggplot(aes(x = reorder(donor, weight), y = weight)) +
  geom_col(fill = "steelblue") +
  coord_flip() +
  labs(title = "Synthetic Control Donor Weights",
       x = "Donor Unit", y = "Weight") +
  theme_minimal()
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

### In-space placebo (primary inference)

The logic: apply the exact same SC method to each of the 10 donor states, pretending each one was "treated" at period 20. If State A's effect is real, its post/pre RMSPE ratio should be an outlier compared to those pseudo-effects. The proportion of units with a ratio ≥ State A's is a Fisher exact p-value.

```r
# ── In-space placebo ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
compute_rmspe_ratio <- function(focal_id, all_ids, df_synth) {
  other_ids <- setdiff(all_ids, focal_id)

  dp <- tryCatch(
    dataprep(
      foo                   = df_synth,
      predictors            = "outcome",
      predictors.op         = "mean",
      dependent             = "outcome",
      unit.variable         = "state_id",
      time.variable         = "period",
      treatment.identifier  = focal_id,
      controls.identifier   = other_ids,
      time.predictors.prior = 1:19,
      time.optimize.ssr     = 1:19,
      time.plot             = 1:40
    ),
    error = function(e) NULL
  )
  if (is.null(dp)) return(NULL)

  so <- tryCatch(synth(dp, quiet = TRUE), error = function(e) NULL)
  if (is.null(so)) return(NULL)

  gaps       <- dp$Y1plot - (dp$Y0plot %*% so$solution.w)
  rmspe_pre  <- sqrt(mean(gaps[1:19]^2))
  rmspe_post <- sqrt(mean(gaps[20:40]^2))

  data.frame(
    unit_id    = focal_id,
    is_treated = (focal_id == treated_id),
    rmspe_pre  = rmspe_pre,
    rmspe_post = rmspe_post,
    ratio      = rmspe_post / rmspe_pre
  )
}

all_ids <- c(treated_id, donor_ids)
placebo_results <- purrr::map_dfr(
  all_ids,
  ~ compute_rmspe_ratio(.x, all_ids, df_synth)
)

# Fisher's exact p-value
treated_ratio <- placebo_results %>% filter(is_treated) %>% pull(ratio)
p_value <- mean(placebo_results$ratio >= treated_ratio)

cat("\n── Placebo inference ──\n")
cat("State A post/pre RMSPE ratio:", round(treated_ratio, 3), "\n")
cat("Fisher's exact p-value:", round(p_value, 3), "\n")
cat("(", sum(placebo_results$ratio >= treated_ratio), "of",
    nrow(placebo_results), "units have ratio >= State A)\n")

# Rank table
placebo_results %>% arrange(desc(ratio)) %>% print()

# Visualize
ggplot(placebo_results,
       aes(x = reorder(factor(unit_id), ratio), y = ratio, fill = is_treated)) +
  geom_col() +
  scale_fill_manual(values = c("FALSE" = "gray75", "TRUE" = "steelblue"),
                    labels = c("Donor", "State A")) +
  coord_flip() +
  labs(title = "In-Space Placebo: Post/Pre RMSPE Ratios",
       x = "Unit", y = "Post/Pre RMSPE Ratio", fill = NULL) +
  theme_minimal()
```

### In-time placebo (structural stability)

Backdate treatment to period 10. There should be no gap between periods 10–19 — if there is, the factor structure is unstable.

```r
# ── In-time placebo ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
dataprep_placebo <- dataprep(
  foo                   = df_synth %>% filter(period <= 19),  # pre-treatment only
  predictors            = "outcome",
  predictors.op         = "mean",
  dependent             = "outcome",
  unit.variable         = "state_id",
  time.variable         = "period",
  treatment.identifier  = treated_id,
  controls.identifier   = donor_ids,
  time.predictors.prior = 1:9,    # fit on first half of pre-period
  time.optimize.ssr     = 1:9,
  time.plot             = 1:19
)

synth_placebo <- synth(dataprep_placebo)

# The gap in periods 10–19 (post-placebo, pre-actual treatment) should ≈ 0
gaps_placebo <- dataprep_placebo$Y1plot -
                (dataprep_placebo$Y0plot %*% synth_placebo$solution.w)
cat("\nPlacebo gap (periods 10–19) mean:", round(mean(gaps_placebo[10:19]), 4), "\n")
cat("If non-trivial relative to the outcome scale, the relationship may not be stable.\n")

path.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_placebo,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_placebo,
  Ylab         = "Outcome",
  Xlab         = "Period",
  Legend       = c("State A", "Synthetic (placebo)"),
  Main         = "In-Time Placebo: Fake Treatment at Period 10"
)
abline(v = 10, lty = 2, col = "red")
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation template

Once you have results, here's how to read them:

**Pre-treatment fit**
- RMSPE/SD < 0.10 → good fit, proceed with confidence
- RMSPE/SD 0.10–0.25 → acceptable, flag as a limitation
- RMSPE/SD > 0.25 → poor fit; consider `augsynth` before drawing conclusions

**Donor weights**
- If 1–2 donors absorb nearly all weight → synthetic control is essentially a simple comparison to those states; flag this
- If spread across 3–5+ donors → more credible

**Placebo p-value**
- p ≤ 0.10 → suggestive of a genuine effect (with only 11 units, exact p-values can only be multiples of 1/11 ≈ 0.09)
- p > 0.20 → effect not distinguishable from noise

**In-time placebo**
- Flat gap in periods 10–19 → factor structure is stable ✅
- Growing gap in periods 10–19 → relationship shifts before treatment, results are suspect ⚠️

---

## If pre-treatment fit is poor → upgrade to Augmented SC

```r
library(augsynth)

# ASCM adds a ridge-regularized outcome model on top of SC weights,
# allowing the treated unit to lie partially outside the convex hull
asyn <- augsynth(
  outcome ~ treated,
  unit   = state_id,
  time   = period,
  data   = df_synth,
  progfunc = "Ridge",
  scm      = TRUE
)

summary(asyn)
plot(asyn)
```

Two quick questions before I lock in the method and generate code — both materially affect what I build:

**1. Do you have any control time series?**
A control series is a monthly outcome that was *not* affected by the policy change but shares similar trends (e.g., a comparable region, a related but untreated metric). If yes → **CausalImpact** (Bayesian structural time series, much stronger identification). If no → **CausalArima** or **segmented regression**.

**2. Is there meaningful seasonality in this outcome?**
With monthly data the model needs to handle annual cycles. A quick look at your pre-period ACF will tell us — but you may already know (e.g., "this is healthcare admissions" → yes; "this is a policy compliance rate" → maybe not).

### timeseries_structural_break

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": false, "flags_coverage": 0.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

Here's what I have from your description:

| Parameter | Value |
|---|---|
| Pre-treatment periods | Months 1–36 (n = 36) |
| Post-treatment periods | Months 37–60 (n = 24) |
| Intervention point | Month 37 |
| Data frequency | Monthly |
| Control series | None mentioned |
| Language | Python |

**Method recommendation**: Since you have no control series, I'll use **segmented regression (ITS)** — it estimates a level shift and slope change at the intervention. I'll also show you how to upgrade to **CausalArima** if you want an ARIMA-based counterfactual.

Two quick questions before assumptions — these affect the analysis meaningfully:
1. **Seasonality**: Is there a known seasonal pattern in this outcome (e.g., annual cycles)?
2. **Concurrent events**: Did anything else change around month 37 that might also affect the outcome?

I'll proceed through all stages now; flag these when you can.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### ✅ 1. Adequate pre-period length
You have **36 pre-treatment observations** — exactly 3 full annual cycles if the data is seasonal. That meets the minimum threshold (≥ 30–50 for basic models; ≥ 3 × season length for seasonal). **Status: OK, but tight if you have strong seasonality.**

### ⚠️ 2. No concurrent confounding events
This is the single biggest threat in ITS. If anything else changed at month 37 (a new regulation, a global shock, a marketing push), there's no statistical way to separate those effects from the policy. **Status: Must be argued from context — not testable.**

→ *Please confirm: was month 37 a clean break with no other major events?*

### ✅ / ⚠️ 3. Pre-period stationarity
The series needs to be stationary (or properly differenced) in the pre-period. Trending series can produce spurious "effects." **Status: Testable — code below.**

### ✅ / ⚠️ 4. Pre-period model fit
The segmented regression model must capture the pre-treatment pattern well. Poor fit = unreliable counterfactual. **Status: Testable — code below.**

### ⚠️ 5. Structural breaks in pre-period
If the pre-treatment series itself had a shift or break (unrelated to the policy), the counterfactual projection will be contaminated. **Status: Testable — included in diagnostics.**

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.gridspec as gridspec
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import acorr_ljungbox
from statsmodels.tsa.stattools import adfuller, kpss
from statsmodels.graphics.tsaplots import plot_acf
from scipy.stats import shapiro

# ── 0. Load / define your data ──────────────────────────────────────────────
# Replace this with your actual DataFrame
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")
# Assumes columns: time (1..60), outcome (float), post (0/1)

INTERVENTION = 37  # first post-treatment period

# ── 1. Build ITS variables ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = df.copy()
df["time_since"] = np.maximum(0, df["time"] - (INTERVENTION - 1))
# time       : linear time trend (1, 2, 3, …)
# post       : level shift indicator (0 before, 1 from month 37)
# time_since : slope change — counts up from 0 starting at intervention

# ── 2. Stationarity check (pre-period only) ──────────────────────────────────
pre = df[df["post"] == 0]["outcome"].values

adf_stat, adf_p, *_ = adfuller(pre, autolag="AIC")
kpss_stat, kpss_p, *_ = kpss(pre, regression="c", nlags="auto")

print("─── Stationarity ───────────────────────────────")
print(f"ADF  p = {adf_p:.3f}  {'✓ stationary' if adf_p < 0.05 else '⚠ unit root likely'}")
print(f"KPSS p = {kpss_p:.3f}  {'✓ stationary' if kpss_p > 0.05 else '⚠ non-stationary'}")

# ── 3. Structural break check (CUSUM on pre-period) ─────────────────────────
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import recursive_olsresiduals
pre_df = df[df["post"] == 0].copy()
pre_model_check = smf.ols("outcome ~ time", data=pre_df).fit()
_, cusum, cusum_ci = recursive_olsresiduals(pre_model_check, alpha=0.05)

plt.figure(figsize=(9, 3))
plt.plot(range(len(cusum)), cusum, label="CUSUM")
plt.axhline(cusum_ci[0], color="red", ls="--", label="95% bounds")
plt.axhline(cusum_ci[1], color="red", ls="--")
plt.title("CUSUM Test — Pre-Period Structural Stability")
plt.xlabel("Pre-period observation")
plt.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()
# If the CUSUM line crosses the red bounds → structural break in pre-period ⚠

# ── 4. Fit segmented regression ──────────────────────────────────────────────
model = smf.ols(
    "outcome ~ time + post + time_since",
    data=df
).fit(cov_type="HAC", cov_kwds={"maxlags": 4})  # HAC SEs for autocorrelation

print("\n─── Model Summary ──────────────────────────────")
print(model.summary())
print(f"\nLevel change at intervention  : {model.params['post']:+.3f} "
      f"(p = {model.pvalues['post']:.3f})")
print(f"Slope change after intervention: {model.params['time_since']:+.3f} "
      f"(p = {model.pvalues['time_since']:.3f})")

# ── 5. Build counterfactual ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
counterfactual_df = df.copy()
counterfactual_df["post"] = 0
counterfactual_df["time_since"] = 0  # no intervention — trend continues
df["counterfactual"] = model.predict(counterfactual_df)
df["fitted"] = model.fittedvalues

# Cumulative effect (sum of gaps in post-period)
post_df = df[df["post"] == 1]
df["point_effect"] = df["outcome"] - df["counterfactual"]
cumulative_effect = post_df["point_effect"].sum()
avg_effect = post_df["point_effect"].mean()

print(f"\nAverage per-period effect : {avg_effect:+.3f}")
print(f"Cumulative effect         : {cumulative_effect:+.3f}")

# ── 6. Pre-period model fit diagnostics ─────────────────────────────────────
pre_residuals = model.resid[df["post"] == 0]
mape = np.mean(np.abs(pre_residuals / df.loc[df["post"] == 0, "outcome"])) * 100

lb = acorr_ljungbox(pre_residuals, lags=10, return_df=True)
sw_stat, sw_p = shapiro(pre_residuals)

print("\n─── Pre-Period Model Fit ────────────────────────")
print(f"MAPE            : {mape:.1f}%  {'✓' if mape < 10 else '⚠ poor fit'}")
print(f"Ljung-Box p     : {lb['lb_pvalue'].min():.3f}  "
      f"{'✓ white noise' if lb['lb_pvalue'].min() > 0.05 else '⚠ autocorrelation remains'}")
print(f"Shapiro-Wilk p  : {sw_p:.3f}  "
      f"{'✓ normal residuals' if sw_p > 0.05 else '⚠ non-normal residuals'}")

# ── 7. Plots ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig = plt.figure(figsize=(12, 8))
gs = gridspec.GridSpec(2, 2, figure=fig)

# Panel A: Observed vs fitted + counterfactual
ax1 = fig.add_subplot(gs[0, :])
ax1.plot(df["time"], df["outcome"], "k-o", ms=4, label="Observed")
ax1.plot(df["time"], df["fitted"], "b-", label="Fitted")
ax1.plot(df["time"], df["counterfactual"], "b--", label="Counterfactual")
ax1.axvline(INTERVENTION - 0.5, color="red", ls="--", label="Intervention")
ax1.fill_between(
    post_df["time"],
    post_df["counterfactual"],
    post_df["outcome"],
    alpha=0.2, color="green", label="Estimated effect"
)
ax1.set_title("Interrupted Time Series — Observed vs Counterfactual")
ax1.set_xlabel("Time (months)")
ax1.set_ylabel("Outcome")
ax1.legend()

# Panel B: Point effect over time
ax2 = fig.add_subplot(gs[1, 0])
ax2.bar(post_df["time"], post_df["point_effect"], color="steelblue", alpha=0.8)
ax2.axhline(0, color="black", lw=0.8)
ax2.set_title("Point Effect by Period (Post Only)")
ax2.set_xlabel("Time (months)")
ax2.set_ylabel("Estimated effect")

# Panel C: Pre-period residuals ACF
ax3 = fig.add_subplot(gs[1, 1])
plot_acf(pre_residuals, ax=ax3, title="Pre-Period Residuals ACF", lags=15)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Run both checks before trusting results.

### Check 1 — Placebo date (fake intervention in pre-period)
```python
# Use month 19 as a fake intervention (midpoint of pre-period)
PLACEBO_DATE = 19

df_placebo = df[df["time"] < INTERVENTION].copy()
df_placebo["post_placebo"] = (df_placebo["time"] >= PLACEBO_DATE).astype(int)
df_placebo["time_since_placebo"] = np.maximum(
    0, df_placebo["time"] - (PLACEBO_DATE - 1)
)

placebo_model = smf.ols(
    "outcome ~ time + post_placebo + time_since_placebo",
    data=df_placebo
).fit(cov_type="HAC", cov_kwds={"maxlags": 4})

print("─── Placebo Test ───────────────────────────────")
print(f"Placebo level change: {placebo_model.params['post_placebo']:+.3f} "
      f"(p = {placebo_model.pvalues['post_placebo']:.3f})")
print(f"Placebo slope change: {placebo_model.params['time_since_placebo']:+.3f} "
      f"(p = {placebo_model.pvalues['time_since_placebo']:.3f})")
# Desired: both p > 0.05 (no spurious effect at the fake date)
```

### Check 2 — Pre-period hold-out prediction
```python
# Train on months 1-24, predict months 25-36 → should fit well
train = df[df["time"] <= 24].copy()
holdout = df[(df["time"] > 24) & (df["post"] == 0)].copy()

train_model = smf.ols("outcome ~ time", data=train).fit()
holdout["predicted"] = train_model.predict(holdout)
holdout_mape = np.mean(
    np.abs((holdout["outcome"] - holdout["predicted"]) / holdout["outcome"])
) * 100

print(f"Hold-out MAPE (months 25–36): {holdout_mape:.1f}%")
# < 10% → model generalises well in the pre-period
# > 15% → counterfactual projection is suspect
```

> Based on the interrupted time series analysis, the policy intervention at month 37 is associated with an estimated **level change of `[post coef]`** units (p = `[p]`) and a **slope change of `[time_since coef]`** units per month (p = `[p]`). The cumulative effect over the 24 post-treatment months is `[cumulative_effect]` units.
>
> Pre-period model fit: MAPE = `[X]`% — `[good / acceptable / poor]`.

## What to watch for

| Check | Target | Action if failing |
|---|---|---|
| ADF p < 0.05 | Stationary pre-period | Difference the series, refit |
| CUSUM stays in bounds | No pre-period structural break | Investigate what changed mid-pre-period |
| Pre-period MAPE < 10% | Good model fit | Add seasonal dummies or try CausalArima |
| Ljung-Box p > 0.05 | No residual autocorrelation | HAC SEs already protect inference; consider ARIMA errors |
| Placebo p > 0.05 | No spurious effect | Investigate model specification |

Let me know your MAPE, the Ljung-Box result, and whether the placebo test came up clean — I'll help interpret and adjust from there.

### did_basic_2x2

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 4.828365714285701, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.3333333333333333, "must_include_results": {"parallel_trends": true, "confidence_interval": false, "clustered_standard_errors": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## DiD Analysis: New Display → Sales

**Setup**: Single treatment date (month 6), two groups → **Classic TWFE DiD** with store + month fixed effects.

One key implementation note before the code: with entity (store) fixed effects, the time-invariant `treated` dummy must **not** enter as a regressor — entity FE absorb it completely, causing perfect multicollinearity. Only `treated_post` (which varies over time) goes in as a regressor.

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 1. Treatment indicators ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
df['treated']      = (df['store'] <= 25).astype(int)   # time-invariant group flag
df['post']         = (df['month'] >= 6).astype(int)    # post-treatment period
df['treated_post'] = df['treated'] * df['post']        # DiD interaction (varies over time)

# ── 2. Main DiD estimate (TWFE) ──────────────────────────────────────────────
# 'treated' is absorbed by EntityEffects → include only treated_post
df_panel = df.set_index(['store', 'month'])

mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    'sales ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects',
    data=df_panel
)
res = mod.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)  # cluster SEs at store level

att        = res.params['treated_post']
ci_low     = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'lower']
ci_high    = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'upper']
p_val      = res.pvalues['treated_post']

print(res.summary)
print(f"\n── Main Result ──────────────────────────────")
print(f"ATT (treatment effect):  {att:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI:                  [{ci_low:.4f}, {ci_high:.4f}]")
print(f"p-value:                 {p_val:.4f}")
print(f'ESTIMATE:{att}')

# ── 3. Pre-trends check (event study) ───────────────────────────────────────
# Create time-to-treatment dummies; exclude t = -1 as reference period
df['time_to_treat'] = df['month'] - 6   # 0 = first treated month
periods = [p for p in sorted(df['time_to_treat'].unique()) if p != -1]

for p in periods:
    df[f'ttp_{p}'] = ((df['treated'] == 1) & (df['time_to_treat'] == p)).astype(int)

ttp_cols = [f'ttp_{p}' for p in periods]
es_formula = 'sales ~ ' + ' + '.join(ttp_cols) + ' + EntityEffects + TimeEffects'

es_mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(es_formula, data=df.set_index(['store', 'month']))
es_res = es_mod.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)

# ── 4. Event study plot ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
coefs    = [es_res.params.get(f'ttp_{p}', 0.0)                         for p in periods]
ci_lo    = [es_res.conf_int().get('lower', {}).get(f'ttp_{p}', 0.0)    for p in periods]
ci_hi    = [es_res.conf_int().get('upper', {}).get(f'ttp_{p}', 0.0)    for p in periods]

# Insert 0 at reference period (t = -1) for a complete plot
all_periods = sorted(periods + [-1])
plot_coefs  = []
plot_lo, plot_hi = [], []
for p in all_periods:
    if p == -1:
        plot_coefs.append(0); plot_lo.append(0); plot_hi.append(0)
    else:
        idx = periods.index(p)
        plot_coefs.append(coefs[idx])
        plot_lo.append(ci_lo[idx])
        plot_hi.append(ci_hi[idx])

err_lo = [c - l for c, l in zip(plot_coefs, plot_lo)]
err_hi = [h - c for c, h in zip(plot_coefs, plot_hi)]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(10, 5))
ax.errorbar(all_periods, plot_coefs,
            yerr=[err_lo, err_hi],
            fmt='o-', capsize=4, color='steelblue', label='Estimate ± 95% CI')
ax.axhline(0,   color='black',  linewidth=0.8, linestyle='--')
ax.axvline(-0.5, color='red',   linewidth=1.2, linestyle=':', label='Treatment onset')
ax.set_xlabel('Months Relative to Treatment (month 6 = 0)')
ax.set_ylabel('Effect on Sales')
ax.set_title('Event Study: New Display → Sales\n(pre-treatment coefficients should ≈ 0)')
ax.legend(); plt.tight_layout(); plt.savefig('event_study.png', dpi=150)
print("\nEvent study plot saved → event_study.png")

# ── 5. Plain-language summary ────────────────────────────────────────────────
direction = "increased" if att > 0 else "decreased"
sig_flag  = "statistically significant" if p_val < 0.05 else "not statistically significant"
print(f"""
── Interpretation ───────────────────────────────
The new display {direction} average monthly sales by {abs(att):.2f} units
(95% CI: [{ci_low:.2f}, {ci_high:.2f}]), which is {sig_flag} (p = {p_val:.3f}).

Assumptions to verify:
  • Parallel trends  → inspect the pre-period coefficients in the event study
  • No anticipation  → coefficients at t = -2, -3 should be near zero
  • No spillovers    → treated/control stores should not share customers
""")
```

---

### What the code does

| Stage | Detail |
|---|---|
| **Treatment var** | `treated_post` only — `treated` is absorbed by store FE |
| **Fixed effects** | Store FE (absorb time-invariant differences) + month FE (absorb common time trends) |
| **SEs** | Clustered at the store level — accounts for within-store serial correlation |
| **Event study** | Month-by-month interaction for treated stores; t = −1 is the reference period |

**Next step**: If the event study shows any pre-treatment drift, that's a parallel trends violation — come back and we can explore control group reweighting or a more robust estimator.

</details>

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": false, "execution_error": null, "estimate": null, "estimation_accurate": null, "diagnostic_coverage": 1.0, "must_include_results": {"treatment": true, "control": true, "pre": true, "post": true}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Here's a classic 2×2 DiD exercise. The scenario is realistic, the data is clean, and the true effect is hidden at the bottom — scroll down only when you're ready to check your work.

---

## 📋 Scenario

You're a data analyst at **RetailCo**, a national chain with 100 stores. In month 13, the company rolled out a **new store layout** — better signage, reorganized product placement, wider aisles — in 50 pilot stores. The other 50 stores kept the original layout throughout.

You have 24 months of monthly revenue data (in thousands of dollars) for all 100 stores: 12 months before the rollout and 12 months after.

**Your manager's question:** *Did the new layout actually increase revenue, and by how much?*

---

## 🐍 Python DGP

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(303)
n_stores = 100
n_months = 24
treat_month = 13

store_id = np.repeat(np.arange(1, n_stores + 1), n_months)
month = np.tile(np.arange(1, n_months + 1), n_stores)

# Stores 1–50 receive the new layout
treated = (store_id <= 50).astype(int)
post = (month >= treat_month).astype(int)

store_fe = np.repeat(np.random.normal(0, 3, n_stores), n_months)
time_trend = 0.2 * month

true_att = 5.0  # ← don't peek yet!

revenue = (
    50
    + store_fe
    + time_trend
    + true_att * treated * post
    + np.random.normal(0, 2, n_stores * n_months)
)

df = pd.DataFrame({
    "store_id": store_id,
    "month": month,
    "treated": treated,
    "post": post,
    "revenue": np.round(revenue, 2),
})

df.to_csv("data.csv", index=False)
print(f"Dataset saved: {df.shape[0]} rows × {df.shape[1]} cols")
print(f"Treated stores: {df[df.treated==1].store_id.nunique()}  |  "
      f"Control stores: {df[df.treated==0].store_id.nunique()}")
print(f"Pre-period months: {treat_month - 1}  |  "
      f"Post-period months: {n_months - treat_month + 1}")

# --- True effect (check your work here) ---
print(f"ESTIMATE:{true_att}")
```

---

## 🎯 What to do

1. **Load the CSV** and explore the data — distributions, pre-treatment trends, balance.
2. **State the identifying assumption** you need for DiD to be valid. How would you test it?
3. **Estimate the ATT** using at least one DiD specification.
4. **Check your work** by running the DGP code and looking at the `ESTIMATE:` line.

> **Tip if you get stuck:** start by plotting average monthly revenue separately for treated vs. control stores. What do you see in the pre-period? That shape tells you a lot about what assumption you're leaning on.

---

<details>
<summary>📐 Hint on what to estimate</summary>

A 2×2 DiD can be estimated as a simple regression:

```
revenue ~ treated + post + treated:post + store_fe + ε
```

The coefficient on `treated × post` is your ATT estimate. Make sure you're clustering standard errors at the store level — stores are your unit of randomization.

</details>

</details>

### exercise_dgp_iv

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": false, "execution_error": null, "estimate": null, "estimation_accurate": null, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.6666666666666666, "must_include_results": {"instrument": true, "endogenous": true, "first_stage": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Here's a **Basic IV exercise** using DGP-05 from the library.

---

## Scenario

You work as a data analyst at a regional health insurer. Leadership believes the company's **voluntary wellness program** reduces members' annual medical spending — but there's a problem: healthier, more motivated members are much more likely to enroll, so a naive comparison is hopelessly confounded.

Your colleague suggests a fix: last year, a subset of members was **randomly selected** to receive an encouragement letter urging them to enroll. The letter was randomized, so it should be unrelated to underlying health — it only affects spending *through* its effect on enrollment.

**Your manager's question:** Does enrolling in the wellness program actually reduce medical spending? If so, by how much?

You have a dataset of 3,000 members. Take a look and tell me: what causal method would you use and why?

---

## DGP — Save & Run This

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(505)
n = 3000

# ── Instrument: randomly assigned encouragement letter ──────────────────────
Z = np.random.binomial(1, 0.5, n)

# ── Unobserved confounder: health motivation ─────────────────────────────────
# Higher motivation → more likely to enroll AND lower spending
# This is NOT in the dataset — it's the source of endogeneity
U = np.random.normal(0, 1, n)

# ── Treatment: enrollment in wellness program ────────────────────────────────
# Letter strongly predicts enrollment (first-stage F >> 10)
def logistic(x):
    return 1 / (1 + np.exp(-x))

p_enroll = logistic(-1.0 + 1.5 * Z + 1.0 * U)
D = np.random.binomial(1, p_enroll)

# ── Outcome: annual medical spending (thousands of dollars) ─────────────────
# U directly lowers spending (healthier people spend less)
# D reduces spending by $10k among compliers (true LATE)
Y = 50 - 10 * D - 8 * U + np.random.normal(0, 10, n)

df = pd.DataFrame({
    "id":        np.arange(1, n + 1),
    "encouraged": Z,   # instrument  — randomly assigned
    "enrolled":   D,   # treatment   — endogenous
    "spending":   np.round(Y, 2),   # outcome (thousands)
})

df.to_csv("wellness_iv.csv", index=False)

# ── Sanity checks ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
enroll_rate_encouraged = D[Z == 1].mean()
enroll_rate_control    = D[Z == 0].mean()
print(f"Enrollment rate (encouraged): {enroll_rate_encouraged:.3f}")
print(f"Enrollment rate (control):    {enroll_rate_control:.3f}")
print(f"First-stage Δ:                {enroll_rate_encouraged - enroll_rate_control:.3f}")

# ── True effect ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
true_late = -10.0
print(f"ESTIMATE:{true_late}")
```

---

Load `wellness_iv.csv` and try to estimate the causal effect of `enrolled` on `spending`. Think about:

1. **Why is OLS biased here?** Run it first and note the estimate.
2. **What method do you need, and why does `encouraged` qualify as a valid instrument?**
3. **What estimand does your method recover?** (ATE? ATT? Something else?)
4. **What diagnostics should you run before trusting the estimate?**

The true LATE is hidden above in the `ESTIMATE:` print — run the DGP script when you're ready to check your work, or ask me to reveal it. Good luck!

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

You have a clean individual-level A/B test: binary outcome, 5,000 users per group, no covariates visible in the sample. I'll run a difference-in-means estimate plus a regression model with robust SEs.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions (Quick Pass)

| Assumption | Status | Note |
|---|---|---|
| Random assignment | ✅ Assumed | Verify with balance table if covariates exist |
| SUTVA | ✅ Plausible | Checkout flows are unlikely to have spillovers |
| No differential attrition | ✅ Equal group sizes (5k/5k) | |
| Compliance | ✅ Assumed | `treated` column reflects assignment |

---

## Stage 3: Analysis Code

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy.stats import ttest_ind

# ── 1. Sanity check ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print("=== Group sizes ===")
print(df.groupby('treated')['conversion'].agg(['count', 'mean', 'std'])
        .rename(columns={'count': 'n', 'mean': 'conversion_rate'}))

# ── 2. Difference in means (raw) ─────────────────────────────────────────────
treated_outcomes = df.loc[df['treated'] == 1, 'conversion']
control_outcomes = df.loc[df['treated'] == 0, 'conversion']

diff = treated_outcomes.mean() - control_outcomes.mean()
t_stat, p_value = ttest_ind(treated_outcomes, control_outcomes)

print(f"\n=== Difference in Means ===")
print(f"Control conversion rate:   {control_outcomes.mean():.4f}")
print(f"Treatment conversion rate: {treated_outcomes.mean():.4f}")
print(f"Raw difference (ATE):      {diff:.4f}")
print(f"t-statistic: {t_stat:.4f}, p-value: {p_value:.4f}")

# ── 3. OLS regression with HC3 robust SEs ────────────────────────────────────
model = smf.ols('conversion ~ treated', data=df).fit(cov_type='HC3')
ate    = model.params['treated']
ci_lo  = model.conf_int().loc['treated', 0]
ci_hi  = model.conf_int().loc['treated', 1]

print(f"\n=== OLS Regression (HC3 robust SEs) ===")
print(f"ATE:      {ate:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI:   [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"p-value:  {model.pvalues['treated']:.4f}")
print(model.summary().tables[1])

# ── 4. Effect size (percentage point lift) ───────────────────────────────────
baseline = control_outcomes.mean()
rel_lift = (ate / baseline) * 100 if baseline > 0 else np.nan
print(f"\n=== Interpretation ===")
print(f"Absolute lift: {ate*100:.2f} percentage points")
print(f"Relative lift: {rel_lift:.1f}% over baseline")

# ── Required output ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print(f'ESTIMATE:{ate:.6f}')
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you run this, the output will read as:

> The new checkout flow changed conversion by **X percentage points** (95% CI: [lo, hi]). This represents a **Y% relative lift** over the control baseline. With 5,000 users per group, the experiment has strong power to detect effects ≥ ~1 pp.

---

**Next steps:**
- If you have user-level covariates (device type, prior visits, etc.), add them to the regression for precision gains — the point estimate won't change much, but the CI will tighten.
- Run `/causal-auditor` to stress-test for any remaining threats to validity.

</details>
