### iv_strong_instrument

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": -5581.812588667875, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 1.0, "must_include_results": {"first_stage": true, "confidence_interval": true}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS

# ── 0. Create binary instrument ───────────────────────────────────────────────
df = df.copy()
df['eligible'] = (df['draft_number'] > 50).astype(int)

# ── 1. First stage: instrument relevance ──────────────────────────────────────
first_stage = smf.ols('served ~ eligible', data=df).fit()
print("=== FIRST STAGE ===")
print(first_stage.summary())
print(f"First-stage F-statistic : {first_stage.fvalue:.2f}")
print(f"First-stage coef (eligible): {first_stage.params['eligible']:.4f}")

if first_stage.fvalue < 10:
    print("⚠️  WARNING: Weak instrument (F < 10). IV estimates will be unreliable.")
elif first_stage.fvalue < 100:
    print("⚠️  NOTE: Moderate instrument strength (10 ≤ F < 100). Interpret with care.")
else:
    print("✅  Strong instrument (F ≥ 100).")

# ── 2. Naive OLS (for bias comparison) ────────────────────────────────────────
ols_model = smf.ols('earnings ~ served', data=df).fit()
ols_coef  = ols_model.params['served']
print(f"\n=== NAIVE OLS ===")
print(f"OLS estimate of served → earnings : {ols_coef:.4f}")
print("(Likely biased — volunteers may differ systematically from non-volunteers)")

# ── 3. 2SLS / IV estimation ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    'earnings ~ 1 + [served ~ eligible]',
    data=df
).fit(cov_type='robust')

print("\n=== 2SLS (IV) RESULTS ===")
print(iv_model.summary)

# ── 4. Extract LATE and confidence interval ───────────────────────────────────
late   = iv_model.params['served']
ci_low = iv_model.conf_int().loc['served', 'lower']
ci_hi  = iv_model.conf_int().loc['served', 'upper']
pval   = iv_model.pvalues['served']

print(f"\n=== CAUSAL ESTIMATE (LATE) ===")
print(f"IV estimate  : {late:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI       : [{ci_low:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"p-value      : {pval:.4f}")
print(f"\nInterpretation: Among compliers — men who served because their draft")
print(f"number was > 50 but would not have served otherwise — military service")
print(f"changed earnings by ${late:,.2f} on average.")
print(f"\nBias direction: OLS ({ols_coef:.2f}) vs IV ({late:.2f})")

# ── 5. Required output ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print(f'ESTIMATE:{late}')
```

---

### What this code does

| Step | Purpose |
|---|---|
| `eligible = draft_number > 50` | Constructs the binary instrument from the lottery threshold |
| **First stage** | Regresses `served ~ eligible`; checks F-statistic for instrument strength |
| **Naive OLS** | Estimates `earnings ~ served` — biased because volunteers self-select |
| **2SLS via `IV2SLS`** | `[served ~ eligible]` syntax tells linearmodels that `served` is endogenous, instrumented by `eligible` |
| **LATE printout** | The causal estimate applies only to **compliers** — men who served *because* their draft number was > 50 |

### Key assumption reminders

- **Exclusion restriction** — draft eligibility must affect earnings *only* through military service. If high lottery numbers correlate with other outcomes (e.g., college enrollment decisions), this would be violated.
- **Monotonicity** — no "defiers" who avoided service *because* they were eligible. Plausible here (a lottery can't cause someone to enlist *less*).
- **Relevance** — check the first-stage F printed above; the classic Angrist (1990) draft lottery instrument is typically strong.

</details>

---

### matching_ate

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 5.040391655572052, "estimation_accurate": false, "diagnostic_coverage": 1.0, "must_include_results": {"propensity_score": true}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## IPW Analysis — ATE Estimation in Python

Here's a complete implementation following the 5-stage pattern. I'll flag key decisions as we go.

---

### A note on identification before we code

With only **age, income, and education** as covariates, the CIA (conditional independence assumption) requires that *no other variable* drives both selection into treatment and the outcome. That's a strong claim — but it's what you've asserted by specifying those three covariates. I'll include a sensitivity check at the end.

---

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import warnings
warnings.filterwarnings("ignore")

# ── 1. Propensity Score Estimation ────────────────────────────────────────────
# Standardize before logistic regression so coefficients are on the same scale
covariates = ["age", "income", "education"]
X = df[covariates].values
D = df["treatment"].values

scaler = StandardScaler()
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
ps_model.fit(X_scaled, D)
df["ps"] = ps_model.predict_proba(X_scaled)[:, 1]

# ── 2. Overlap / Positivity Check ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# If distributions don't overlap, IPW extrapolates — estimates become unreliable
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 5))
ax.hist(df.loc[D == 1, "ps"], bins=30, alpha=0.5, label="Treated", density=True)
ax.hist(df.loc[D == 0, "ps"], bins=30, alpha=0.5, label="Control", density=True)
ax.set_xlabel("Propensity Score")
ax.set_ylabel("Density")
ax.set_title("Propensity Score Overlap by Treatment Group")
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("ps_overlap.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

print("── Propensity Score Diagnostics ─────────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  Treated PS range : [{df.loc[D==1, 'ps'].min():.4f}, {df.loc[D==1, 'ps'].max():.4f}]")
print(f"  Control PS range : [{df.loc[D==0, 'ps'].min():.4f}, {df.loc[D==0, 'ps'].max():.4f}]")
print(f"  PS < 0.05        : {np.mean(df['ps'] < 0.05):.4f}  ← near-zero overlap")
print(f"  PS > 0.95        : {np.mean(df['ps'] > 0.95):.4f}  ← near-zero overlap")

# ── 3. IPW Weights (ATE) ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# For ATE: treated weight = 1/e(x), control weight = 1/(1-e(x))
# Stabilized weights (p_treat / e(x) vs (1-p_treat) / (1-e(x))) reduce variance
# while preserving the same estimand — always prefer stabilized for IPW

p_treat = D.mean()
df["ipw_ate"] = np.where(
    D == 1,
    p_treat / df["ps"],               # stabilized treated weight
    (1 - p_treat) / (1 - df["ps"])    # stabilized control weight
)

print("\n── IPW Weight Diagnostics ────────────────────────────────────────────────")
print(df["ipw_ate"].describe().round(4).to_string())

# Flag extreme weights — a sign of overlap problems
extreme_pct = np.mean(df["ipw_ate"] > 10)
if extreme_pct > 0.01:
    print(f"\n  ⚠ {extreme_pct:.1%} of units have weights > 10. "
          f"Consider trimming or doubly-robust estimation.")

# ── 4. Covariate Balance Check ────────────────────────────────────────────────
# The whole point of IPW is to rebalance the sample.
# SMD < 0.1 after weighting = propensity model was adequate.

def weighted_smd(treated_vals, control_vals, control_weights):
    m_t = treated_vals.mean()
    s_t = treated_vals.std()
    m_c = np.average(control_vals, weights=control_weights)
    return (m_t - m_c) / s_t if s_t > 0 else 0.0

balance_rows = []
for cov in covariates:
    t_vals = df.loc[D == 1, cov].values
    c_vals = df.loc[D == 0, cov].values
    c_wts  = df.loc[D == 0, "ipw_ate"].values
    balance_rows.append({
        "covariate"  : cov,
        "SMD_before" : round(weighted_smd(t_vals, c_vals, np.ones(len(c_vals))), 4),
        "SMD_after"  : round(weighted_smd(t_vals, c_vals, c_wts), 4),
    })

balance_df = pd.DataFrame(balance_rows)
balance_df["balanced"] = balance_df["SMD_after"].abs() < 0.1

print("\n── Covariate Balance (target: |SMD| < 0.1 after weighting) ──────────────")
print(balance_df.to_string(index=False))

if not balance_df["balanced"].all():
    print("\n  ⚠ Some covariates remain imbalanced. Consider adding interactions "
          "or using a more flexible propensity model (e.g., GradientBoosting).")

# ── 5. ATE Estimation via Weighted Regression ─────────────────────────────────
# Weighted regression gives the IPW ATE estimate with correct standard errors
model_ipw = smf.wls("outcome ~ treatment", data=df, weights=df["ipw_ate"]).fit()

ate    = model_ipw.params["treatment"]
ci_lo  = model_ipw.conf_int().loc["treatment", 0]
ci_hi  = model_ipw.conf_int().loc["treatment", 1]
pval   = model_ipw.pvalues["treatment"]

print("\n── IPW ATE Results ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  ATE estimate : {ate:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI       : [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"  p-value      : {pval:.4f}")
print(f"\n  Interpretation: On average across the full population, treatment")
print(f"  changes the outcome by {ate:.4f} units (95% CI: {ci_lo:.4f} to {ci_hi:.4f}).")

print(f"\nESTIMATE:{ate}")

# ── 6. Robustness: Sensitivity to Unobserved Confounding ──────────────────────
# E-value: how strong would an unmeasured confounder need to be to explain away
# the entire estimated effect? Higher E-value = more robust finding.

def e_value_ols(beta, se):
    """
    Approximate E-value for a linear effect.
    Converts to a risk-ratio equivalent, then computes standard E-value.
    NOTE: this is approximate — use EValue R package for a rigorous version.
    """
    sd_y = df["outcome"].std()
    rr_approx = np.exp(0.91 * beta / sd_y)  # approximate RR conversion
    return rr_approx + np.sqrt(rr_approx * (rr_approx - 1))

e_val = e_value_ols(ate, model_ipw.bse["treatment"])
print(f"\n── Sensitivity Check (approximate E-value) ───────────────────────────────")
print(f"  E-value ≈ {e_val:.2f}")
print(f"  An unmeasured confounder would need a risk-ratio association of ~{e_val:.2f}")
print(f"  with BOTH treatment and outcome to fully explain away the estimated effect.")
print(f"  (Use the R `sensemakr` or `EValue` package for a rigorous version.)")
```

---

### What each stage gives you

| Stage | What to look for |
|---|---|
| **Overlap plot** | Distributions should visually overlap — separated humps = positivity violated |
| **Weight diagnostics** | Extreme weights (>10) signal near-zero PS values — overlap is thin |
| **SMD table** | All \|SMD\| < 0.1 after weighting = propensity model is adequate |
| **ATE estimate** | The causal effect, valid *only if* CIA holds |
| **E-value** | How fragile the result is to unobserved confounding |

### Critical assumption reminder

The CIA — that age, income, and education fully account for who got treated — is **not testable**. If there's any variable that drives treatment selection but isn't in that list (e.g., health status, job type, geography), your ATE is biased. The E-value in Stage 6 tells you how *large* that bias would need to be to overturn the result.

If CIA feels shaky, consider upgrading to **doubly-robust (AIPW)** via `econml.dr.LinearDRLearner`, which gives you a second chance at consistency if either the outcome model or propensity model is correct — but still can't rescue a fundamentally violated CIA.

</details>

---

### rdd_sharp

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 0.4781863, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.5, "must_include_results": {"bandwidth": true, "manipulation_test": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Here's the complete sharp RDD analysis following the template pattern:

```r
library(tidyverse)
library(rdrobust)
library(rddensity)

# ── Setup ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cutoff <- 70

df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    running_centered = test_score - cutoff,
    treated = as.integer(test_score >= cutoff)
  )

cat("Units below cutoff:", sum(df$test_score < cutoff), "\n")
cat("Units above cutoff:", sum(df$test_score >= cutoff), "\n")
cat("Mean GPA below:", round(mean(df$gpa[df$test_score < cutoff]), 3), "\n")
cat("Mean GPA above:", round(mean(df$gpa[df$test_score >= cutoff]), 3), "\n\n")

# ── RD Plot ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
rdplot(
  y       = df$gpa,
  x       = df$test_score,
  c       = cutoff,
  title   = "RD Plot: Scholarship Effect on College GPA",
  x.label = "Test Score",
  y.label = "College GPA"
)

# ── McCrary Density Test (manipulation check) ──────────────────────────────────
# H0: density is continuous at cutoff — no sorting around the 70-point threshold
density_test <- rddensity(X = df$test_score, c = cutoff)
summary(density_test)

rdplotdensity(density_test, df$test_score,
              title  = "McCrary Density Test",
              xlabel = "Test Score",
              ylabel = "Density")

# ── Main RD Estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Local polynomial RD with MSE-optimal bandwidth and robust bias-corrected CI
rd_est <- rdrobust(
  y = df$gpa,
  x = df$test_score,
  c = cutoff
)

summary(rd_est)

# Extract key results
rd_estimate <- rd_est$coef["Robust", 1]
cat("ESTIMATE:", rd_estimate, "\n")
cat("Bandwidth (h):", rd_est$bws["h", 1], "\n")
cat("Effective N (left/right):", rd_est$N_h, "\n")
cat("95% Robust CI: [",
    round(rd_est$ci["Robust", 1], 4), ",",
    round(rd_est$ci["Robust", 2], 4), "]\n\n")

# ── Bandwidth Sensitivity ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
opt_bw <- rd_est$bws["h", 1]
bandwidths <- opt_bw * c(0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.25, 1.5)

bw_results <- map_dfr(bandwidths, function(h) {
  fit <- rdrobust(y = df$gpa, x = df$test_score, c = cutoff, h = h)
  tibble(
    bandwidth = round(h, 2),
    estimate  = fit$coef["Conventional", 1],
    ci_lower  = fit$ci["Robust", 1],
    ci_upper  = fit$ci["Robust", 2]
  )
})

print(bw_results)

ggplot(bw_results, aes(x = bandwidth, y = estimate)) +
  geom_point(size = 3, color = "#377EB8") +
  geom_errorbar(aes(ymin = ci_lower, ymax = ci_upper), width = 0.2) +
  geom_hline(yintercept = 0, linetype = "dashed", color = "grey50") +
  labs(
    title = "Bandwidth Sensitivity Analysis",
    x = "Bandwidth", y = "RD Estimate (Effect on GPA)"
  ) +
  theme_minimal()

# ── Results Summary Table ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
rd_table <- tibble(
  Term             = "Scholarship → GPA",
  Conventional     = rd_est$coef["Conventional", 1],
  `Bias-Corrected` = rd_est$coef["Bias-Corrected", 1],
  Robust           = rd_est$coef["Robust", 1],
  `SE (Robust)`    = rd_est$se["Robust", 1],
  `p-value`        = rd_est$pv["Robust", 1],
  `CI Lower`       = rd_est$ci["Robust", 1],
  `CI Upper`       = rd_est$ci["Robust", 2],
  `BW (h)`         = rd_est$bws["h", 1],
  `N Left`         = rd_est$N_h[1],
  `N Right`        = rd_est$N_h[2]
)

knitr::kable(rd_table, digits = 3,
             caption = "Sharp RDD: Effect of Scholarship on College GPA")
```

---

### What each block does and why

| Block | Purpose |
|---|---|
| **RD plot** | Visual sanity check — you should see a visible jump at score = 70 |
| **McCrary density test** | Checks if students manipulated scores to just barely qualify. A spike at 70 would be a red flag. |
| **`rdrobust`** | Fits local linear regression on each side of 70 using an MSE-optimal bandwidth. The **Robust** row is the right one to report — it uses bias-corrected inference. |
| **Bandwidth sensitivity** | Confirms the estimate doesn't hinge on one particular bandwidth choice. Stable estimates across 50%–150% of the optimal BW build credibility. |

The `ESTIMATE:` line prints the **robust bias-corrected** coefficient — the causal effect of receiving the scholarship on college GPA for students scoring near the 70-point threshold.

</details>

---

### sc_basic

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 0.8066470230053554, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"pre_treatment_fit": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Synthetic Control via Constrained Optimization

Here's what the code does at each stage:
1. **Reshape** data to wide format (time × unit)
2. **Optimize** donor weights on the pre-treatment period using SLSQP with simplex constraints (w ≥ 0, Σw = 1)
3. **Project** the synthetic counterfactual into the post-treatment period
4. **Estimate** the ATT as the average post-treatment gap

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
from scipy.optimize import minimize

# ── 1. Setup ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
TREATED_UNIT    = 1
TREATMENT_TIME  = 21  # first treated period

donor_units = sorted(u for u in df["unit"].unique() if u != TREATED_UNIT)

# Wide format: index = time, columns = units
pivot = df.pivot(index="time", columns="unit", values="outcome")

pre_mask  = pivot.index < TREATMENT_TIME
post_mask = pivot.index >= TREATMENT_TIME

Y_pre_tr  = pivot.loc[pre_mask, TREATED_UNIT].values      # (T0,)
Y_pre_do  = pivot.loc[pre_mask, donor_units].values        # (T0, J)
Y_post_tr = pivot.loc[post_mask, TREATED_UNIT].values     # (T1,)
Y_post_do = pivot.loc[post_mask, donor_units].values       # (T1, J)

J = len(donor_units)

# ── 2. Constrained optimisation for SC weights ───────────────────────────────
# Minimise pre-treatment RMSPE subject to:
#   (a) weights sum to 1   — convex combination
#   (b) weights >= 0       — no short-selling donors

def objective(w):
    residuals = Y_pre_tr - Y_pre_do @ w
    return np.mean(residuals ** 2)          # MSE (monotone in RMSPE)

constraints = {"type": "eq", "fun": lambda w: w.sum() - 1.0}
bounds      = [(0.0, 1.0)] * J
w0          = np.full(J, 1.0 / J)          # equal-weight initialisation

result = minimize(
    objective,
    w0,
    method="SLSQP",
    bounds=bounds,
    constraints=constraints,
    options={"ftol": 1e-12, "maxiter": 2000},
)

if not result.success:
    print(f"[WARNING] Optimiser message: {result.message}")

# Clean up numerical noise and renormalise
weights = np.clip(result.x, 0, None)
weights /= weights.sum()

# ── 3. Pre-treatment fit diagnostics ────────────────────────────────────────
synth_pre  = Y_pre_do  @ weights
rmspe_pre  = np.sqrt(np.mean((Y_pre_tr - synth_pre) ** 2))
outcome_sd = Y_pre_tr.std()

print("=" * 50)
print("DONOR WEIGHTS")
print("-" * 50)
weight_df = (
    pd.DataFrame({"unit": donor_units, "weight": weights})
    .sort_values("weight", ascending=False)
)
for _, row in weight_df.iterrows():
    bar = "█" * int(row["weight"] * 40)
    print(f"  Unit {int(row['unit']):>2}: {row['weight']:.4f}  {bar}")

print(f"\nPre-treatment RMSPE : {rmspe_pre:.4f}")
print(f"Outcome SD (pre)    : {outcome_sd:.4f}")
print(f"RMSPE / SD ratio    : {rmspe_pre / outcome_sd:.4f}  "
      f"({'✓ good fit' if rmspe_pre / outcome_sd < 0.10 else '⚠ check fit'})")

# ── 4. Post-treatment gaps and ATT estimate ──────────────────────────────────
synth_post = Y_post_do @ weights
gaps       = Y_post_tr - synth_post

post_times = pivot.index[post_mask]
gap_df = pd.DataFrame({"period": post_times, "gap": gaps})

print("\n" + "=" * 50)
print("POST-TREATMENT GAPS")
print("-" * 50)
print(gap_df.to_string(index=False, float_format="{:.4f}".format))

gap_estimate  = gaps.mean()
gap_cumulative = gaps.sum()
rmspe_post    = np.sqrt(np.mean(gaps ** 2))
mspe_ratio    = rmspe_post / rmspe_pre  # used for permutation p-value

print("\n" + "=" * 50)
print("TREATMENT EFFECT SUMMARY")
print("-" * 50)
print(f"  Avg post-treatment gap (ATT) : {gap_estimate:.4f}")
print(f"  Cumulative effect            : {gap_cumulative:.4f}")
print(f"  Post RMSPE                   : {rmspe_post:.4f}")
print(f"  Post/Pre RMSPE ratio         : {mspe_ratio:.4f}")
print("=" * 50)

print(f"ESTIMATE:{gap_estimate}")
```

---

### What to look for in the output

| Diagnostic | What it tells you |
|---|---|
| **RMSPE / SD < 0.10** | Good pre-treatment fit — synthetic control is credible |
| **Weights spread across donors** | Genuine interpolation, not just mirroring one unit |
| **Post/Pre RMSPE ratio** | Core statistic for permutation inference — should be an outlier vs. placebos |
| **Gap sign consistency** | If gaps flip across post-periods, the effect may be transient |

---

### Next step: In-space placebo inference

To get a proper p-value, apply the same optimization to each **donor** unit as if it were treated, collect their RMSPE ratios, and count how many exceed the treated unit's ratio. Want me to add that permutation test?

</details>

### timeseries_causalimpact

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 3.3292, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"pre_treatment": false, "confidence_interval": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf

# ── ITS variables ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
intervention_period = 41

df = df.copy()
df['time']       = df['period']                                      # running clock
df['post']       = (df['period'] >= intervention_period).astype(int) # level shift
df['time_since'] = np.maximum(0, df['period'] - (intervention_period - 1))  # slope change

# ── Segmented regression ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# outcome ~ time          → pre-period trend
#         + post          → immediate level shift at intervention
#         + time_since    → change in slope after intervention
# HAC SEs guard against autocorrelation in monthly data
model = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post + time_since', data=df).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4}
)
print(model.summary())

# ── Average causal effect over the post-period ────────────────────────────────
# Counterfactual: what the trend predicts absent intervention
#   CF(t) = b0 + b_time * t        (post=0, time_since=0)
# Actual fitted:
#   Y(t)  = b0 + b_time * t + b_post + b_time_since * (t - 40)
#
# Effect at period t = b_post + b_time_since * (t - 40)
# Average over t = 41..60  →  b_post + b_time_since * mean(1..20)

b_post       = model.params['post']
b_time_since = model.params['time_since']

post_data     = df[df['post'] == 1].copy()
post_data['effect_t'] = b_post + b_time_since * post_data['time_since']
avg_effect    = post_data['effect_t'].mean()

# ── Quick diagnostics ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
pre_data      = df[df['post'] == 0]
pre_residuals = model.resid[pre_data.index]
mape_pre      = np.mean(np.abs(pre_residuals / pre_data['outcome'])) * 100

print("\n── Key results ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"Immediate level shift (b_post):          {b_post:+.3f}  "
      f"(p={model.pvalues['post']:.3f})")
print(f"Slope change per period (b_time_since):  {b_time_since:+.3f}  "
      f"(p={model.pvalues['time_since']:.3f})")
print(f"Average causal effect over post-period:  {avg_effect:+.3f}")
print(f"Pre-period MAPE:                         {mape_pre:.2f}%")
print(f"ESTIMATE:{avg_effect:.4f}")
```

---

### What each term captures

| Variable | Role |
|---|---|
| `time` | Pre-existing upward trend — the baseline slope |
| `post` | Immediate **level jump** when the intervention starts |
| `time_since` | Change in **slope** after the intervention (0 in pre-period) |

### How the average effect is computed

At any post-period time point $t$, the gap between the fitted line and its counterfactual projection is:

$$\text{Effect}(t) = \beta_{\text{post}} + \beta_{\text{time\_since}} \times (t - 40)$$

Averaging that over all 20 post-period observations gives the **average causal effect**. Because you have a mild upward trend in the pre-period, the HAC standard errors also guard against residual autocorrelation.

**One assumption to keep in mind**: the segmented regression assumes nothing else changed at period 41. If any concurrent event (a pricing change, a market shock, a seasonality quirk) coincided with the intervention, the estimate will absorb that too — there's no control series here to partial it out.

</details>

### auditor_subtle_flaw

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.5, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 0.5, "overall": 0.6666666666666666}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Here is the lesson entry to append to `references/lessons.md`:

```markdown
### Matching: Post-treatment variable included in propensity score model
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: User includes a covariate measured after treatment assignment in the propensity score model, often because it "predicts the outcome well"
**Mistake**: Skill failed to flag that conditioning on a post-treatment variable in the propensity model introduces mediation blocking and/or collider bias, invalidating the ATT estimate. Perfect SMD balance on a post-treatment variable is not evidence of good matching — it is evidence of a correctly-executed misspecification.
**Rule**: Before running any matching, always ask: "Was each covariate measured *before* treatment assignment?" Any covariate with a timestamp after treatment onset must be excluded. If the user describes the variable as "measured after the program started," flag it as Fatal immediately. Suggest finding a pre-treatment version of the same variable as a replacement.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

</details>

### auditor_thorough_analysis

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.5, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.8333333333333334}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

I can read but not write files directly from this agent — please add the following entry to `references/lessons.md`:

```markdown
### DiD: Anticipation undetectable when announcement predates data window
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: DiD analysis where treatment assignment was decided weeks or months before rollout, and the data collection window begins after the announcement
**Mistake**: Skill accepted flat event study pre-trends as evidence of no anticipation, without flagging that behavioral responses to pre-announcement knowledge would be absorbed into unit fixed effects and thus invisible to the standard test
**Rule**: Always ask: when was treatment assignment communicated to units, relative to when the data starts? If the announcement predates the data, the pre-trends test cannot detect anticipation. Flag this explicitly and recommend running `att_gt(..., anticipation = k)` sensitivity analysis.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

---

## The headline issues in plain English

The flat pre-trends and F-test (p = 0.72) are doing a lot of work here, and they deserve more skepticism than they're getting.

**The anticipation problem is subtle but serious**: a pre-trends test measures whether treated stores *changed trajectory* in the pre-period. But if store managers knew about the rollout before week 1 of your data, any behavioral response was already there from the start — it shows up as a difference in levels (absorbed by the fixed effect), not a difference in slopes. The event study literally cannot see it. You need the anticipation sensitivity analysis to know whether your estimate is stable.

**The measurement problem is concrete and testable today**: a 12.3% revenue jump from a POS system upgrade is large. Before attributing it to faster checkouts or better customer experience, you need to rule out that the new system simply counts transactions differently. Decompose into transaction count × basket size — this is the highest-value diagnostic you can run right now.

Everything else is fixable or qualifiable. But those two need attention before this number goes anywhere.

</details>

### did_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.7222222222222222}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## DiD Analysis: New Checkout System on Sales

Great setup — clean panel, single treatment date, balanced design. This is the ideal case for DiD. Let me walk you through all five stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup — What You Have

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | New checkout system, onset = month 12 |
| **Outcome** | Sales |
| **Design** | 100 stores × 24 months, single treatment date |
| **Variant** | **Classic TWFE** (two-way fixed effects) — not staggered |

Since all treated stores got the system at the same month, you don't need the more complex staggered estimators. Standard TWFE is appropriate here.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

### 1. ✅ Parallel Trends *(testable — most important)*

**What it means:** Without the new checkout system, treated and control stores would have grown at the same rate. Their sales levels can differ — what matters is the *trajectory* was similar before month 12.

**Why it matters:** If treated stores were already growing faster *before* the system launched, DiD will wrongly credit that growth to the checkout system.

**How to check:** We'll run an event study — plotting coefficients for each month relative to the treatment date. Pre-treatment coefficients should all hover near zero. I'll include the code in Stage 3.

> 🔍 *Does the treated group look similar to control in terms of sales trends pre-month 12? Any known reason they'd differ — e.g., were the treated stores larger or in faster-growing regions?*

---

### 2. ⚠️ No Anticipation *(partially testable)*

**What it means:** Stores didn't change behavior *before* month 12 in anticipation of the new system. For example, if store managers started training staff early and that affected sales, the effect "bleeds" into the pre-period.

**Why it matters:** If pre-period behavior changed, two things break: (a) your pre-trend test is contaminated, and (b) the DiD estimate is too small.

> 🔍 *Was the rollout announced in advance? Any reason stores would have known it was coming before month 12?*

---

### 3. ✅ Stable Composition *(testable)*

**What it means:** No stores entered or left the sample *because of* the treatment. With 100 stores × 24 months, this means a balanced panel of 2,400 rows. If some treated stores closed (or new ones opened) because of the system, your sample is biased.

> 🔍 *Is your panel fully balanced — all 100 stores observed in all 24 months?*

---

### 4. ✅ No Spillovers (SUTVA)

**What it means:** The new checkout system in treated stores doesn't affect *control* stores' sales. This would be violated if, say, faster checkout attracted customers away from nearby control stores.

**Why it matters:** If control stores lost sales because treated stores got better, DiD overestimates the effect.

> 🔍 *Are treated and control stores geographically intermixed (same markets)? Or are they in separate regions?*

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the complete analysis code. A few important notes before you run it:

- **Don't include `treated` as a standalone regressor** — store fixed effects already absorb everything time-invariant about each store (including which group it's in). Including `treated` separately causes perfect multicollinearity and will either crash or silently drop the variable.
- **`treated:post` is the key term** — it varies across both stores *and* time, so it's the only one that's identified after adding both sets of fixed effects.
- **Cluster standard errors at the store level** — observations within the same store are correlated over time; clustering corrects for this.

```r
# ── 0. Packages ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(fixest)       # fast fixed-effects estimation
library(tidyverse)    # data wrangling + ggplot2
library(modelsummary) # clean results tables

# ── 1. Data prep ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Confirm post is coded correctly (1 if month >= 12)
df <- df |>
  mutate(
    post          = as.integer(month >= 12),
    treated       = as.integer(treated),
    time_to_treat = month - 12   # periods relative to treatment (used in event study)
  )

# Sanity check: balanced panel?
df |>
  count(store) |>
  summarise(min_obs = min(n), max_obs = max(n), n_stores = n())
# Expect: min_obs = max_obs = 24, n_stores = 100

# ── 2. Main DiD estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Formula reads: "effect of treated:post, absorbing store and month fixed effects"
# Do NOT add `treated` or `post` as separate terms — FEs absorb them
model_did <- feols(
  sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store   # cluster SEs at store level
)

summary(model_did)

# Clean results table
modelsummary(
  model_did,
  stars    = TRUE,
  gof_map  = c("nobs", "r.squared", "FE: store", "FE: month"),
  title    = "DiD: Effect of New Checkout System on Sales"
)

# ── 3. Effect size with 95% confidence interval ───────────────────────────────
est    <- coef(model_did)["treated:post"]
ci     <- confint(model_did)["treated:post", ]
se     <- se(model_did)["treated:post"]

cat(sprintf(
  "\nDiD Estimate:  %.3f\n95%% CI:        [%.3f, %.3f]\nStd. Error:    %.3f\n",
  est, ci[1], ci[2], se
))

# ── 4. Event study — visual pre-trends check ──────────────────────────────────
# Reference period: month -1 (one period before treatment)
# Pre-treatment coefficients should be near zero; post-treatment shows the effect trajectory
es <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

iplot(
  es,
  main  = "Event Study: Effect of Checkout System on Sales",
  xlab  = "Months Relative to Treatment (0 = launch month)",
  ylab  = "Estimated Effect on Sales",
  col   = "steelblue"
)
abline(v = -0.5, lty = 2, col = "darkred")  # treatment onset line

# ── 5. Joint test of pre-treatment coefficients ──────────────────────────────
# H0: all pre-treatment effects = 0 (i.e., no pre-trend)
pre_treatment_vars <- grep("time_to_treat::-", names(coef(es)), value = TRUE)
wald(es, keep = pre_treatment_vars)
# p > 0.05 → no evidence of pre-trends ✓
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Check

Once you have your main estimate, run this **placebo test** to validate it:

```r
# ── Placebo: fake treatment at month 6 (pre-period only) ──────────────────────
# If DiD is valid, a fake treatment in the pre-period should find ~zero effect
df_pre <- df |>
  filter(month <= 11) |>              # keep only pre-treatment data
  mutate(post_placebo = as.integer(month >= 6))  # fake treatment midpoint

model_placebo <- feols(
  sales ~ treated:post_placebo | store + month,
  data    = df_pre,
  cluster = ~store
)

summary(model_placebo)
# Expect: coefficient near zero, not significant
# If it IS significant → pre-trends are a real concern
```

---

## Stage 5: How to Interpret Your Results

Once you run the code, here's how to read the output:

> *"The `treated:post` coefficient tells you the average sales change in treated stores after month 12, **relative to** the change in control stores over the same period. This isolates the checkout system's effect by removing any trends that affected all stores equally."*

**Example interpretation** (placeholder — fill in your actual numbers):

> "Treated stores saw sales increase by **X units** (95% CI: [A, B]) more than control stores after the new checkout system launched. This represents a **Y%** change relative to pre-treatment average sales."

**Report these caveats alongside your estimate:**
- The estimate is valid *only if* sales trends would have been parallel absent treatment — check your event study plot carefully
- If stores are in shared markets, some of the effect may reflect customer displacement from control stores, not true system-driven growth

---

## Next Steps

After you run the code, share:
1. The `treated:post` coefficient and p-value
2. Your event study plot (especially whether pre-treatment coefficients are near zero)
3. The Wald test p-value for pre-treatment coefficients

I'll help you interpret them and flag any concerns. When you're ready to stress-test the analysis further, `/causal-auditor` can probe for additional threats.

### did_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Great — this is a clear **staggered DiD** with three cohorts. Here's what I'm working with:

| | Detail |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | Training program rollout |
| **Cohorts** | Jan (n=100), Mar (n=200), Jun (n=200) |
| **Outcome** | Weekly sales |
| **Data** | ~500 stores × ~104 weeks panel |
| **Estimand** | Overall ATT |
| **Language** | Python |

**Critical design choice:** Standard TWFE is off the table. With three cohorts, TWFE uses already-treated stores as implicit controls for later-treated stores — "forbidden comparisons" that bias estimates when effects compound over time (as training effects likely do). We'll use **Callaway-Sant'Anna** via the `differences` package.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Needs testing*

**What it means here:** Without the training program, your Jan/Mar/Jun cohorts would have had similar sales *trends* — not necessarily the same levels, but the same trajectory week-over-week.

**Why this is tricky for you:** Stores weren't randomly assigned to waves. If your highest-performing stores got the program first (or last), pre-trends will diverge. Wave assignment mechanism matters a lot.

> **Question:** How were stores selected into each wave — geographic rollout, store size, performance, convenience?

We'll run an event study to check pre-trends regardless, but the answer shapes how worried to be.

---

### 2. No Anticipation 🚨 *You've already flagged a likely violation*

**What it means here:** Stores in the March and June waves shouldn't have changed their sales behavior before their official training date. But you said they may have started **informal training on their own** after hearing about the program.

**Why this matters:** If a March store started informal training in February, its pre-treatment period is contaminated. The DiD estimate for that cohort will be too small (the "pre" period already has some effect), and the pre-trends test will falsely look clean.

**Mitigation options — I'll need your input:**

- **Option A — Redefine treatment date to announcement date.** When did stores in later waves hear about it? If there was a company-wide announcement in (say) December, shift `first_treat` for all cohorts to that date.
- **Option B — Use the `anticipation` parameter** in Callaway-Sant'Anna: `ATTgt(..., anticipation=k)` drops the k periods before treatment from the "clean pre" window. If informal training started ~4 weeks before official rollout, set `anticipation=4`.
- **Option C — Sensitivity analysis.** Estimate with anticipation=0, 2, 4, 8 and show the range.

> **Question:** Do you have any signal on *when* later-wave stores started self-training — was it weeks or months before their wave? And was there a formal announcement date?

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Likely fine*

**What it means here:** Stores shouldn't open or close *because* of the training program. Since this is an internal operational rollout, I'd expect the panel to be reasonably balanced.

We'll verify this in code — unbalanced panels can silently bias estimates.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ⚠️ *Worth checking*

**What it means here:** A trained store shouldn't cause nearby *untrained* stores' sales to change. If stores in the same region compete for the same customers, trained stores improving service could pull sales from untreated stores — making the control group look worse and inflating your estimated effect.

> **Question:** Do stores compete geographically? Are the waves clustered by region?

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Handled by design*

**What it means here:** This is exactly why we're not using TWFE. With three cohorts and effects that likely grow over time (training compounds), TWFE weights can go negative. Callaway-Sant'Anna constructs clean 2×2 comparisons for each cohort × time cell and aggregates them properly.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | ⚠️ Testable | Event study — check pre-period |
| No anticipation | 🚨 Likely violated | Need announcement date or set `anticipation=k` |
| Stable composition | ✅ Probably fine | Verify panel balance in code |
| No spillovers | ⚠️ Context-dependent | Check if waves are regionally clustered |
| Correct functional form | ✅ Handled | Using Callaway-Sant'Anna, not TWFE |

**No fatal blockers** — but the anticipation issue needs a decision before we finalize the spec. While you think on those two questions, here's the full implementation:

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
# ── 0. Install if needed ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# pip install differences linearmodels pandas numpy matplotlib

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.ticker as mticker
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS
from differences import ATTgt

# ── 1. Load & validate data ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns: store_id, week, sales, first_treat_week
# first_treat_week = 0 for never-treated stores (if any exist),
#                  otherwise the week number when the store's wave began.
# If ALL stores are eventually treated, Callaway-Sant'Anna uses the
# "not-yet-treated" stores as the comparison group by default.

df = pd.read_csv("stores.csv")   # ← swap in your actual path

# Confirm structure
assert df[['store_id', 'week']].duplicated().sum() == 0, "Panel not unique on store×week"
print(df[['store_id', 'week', 'sales', 'first_treat_week']].head())
print(f"\nStores: {df['store_id'].nunique()}")
print(f"Weeks:  {df['week'].nunique()}")
print(f"Cohorts:\n{df.groupby('first_treat_week')['store_id'].nunique()}")

# ── 2. Panel balance check (Stable Composition) ───────────────────────────────
total_weeks = df['week'].nunique()
balance = (df.groupby('store_id')
             .agg(n_weeks=('week', 'nunique'),
                  cohort=('first_treat_week', 'first'))
             .reset_index())
balance['fully_observed'] = balance['n_weeks'] == total_weeks

print("\nPanel balance by cohort:")
print(balance.groupby('cohort').agg(
    n_stores=('store_id', 'count'),
    pct_fully_observed=('fully_observed', 'mean')
).round(3))

# ── 3. Pre-trends event study (Parallel Trends diagnostic) ───────────────────
# Build relative-time dummies manually for PanelOLS
# (We use the period t = -1 as the reference — omit it)

df['rel_time'] = df['week'] - df['first_treat_week']

# Bin distant leads/lags to avoid sparse cells
df['rel_time_binned'] = df['rel_time'].clip(lower=-26, upper=26)

ref_period = -1
periods = sorted(df['rel_time_binned'].unique())
periods = [p for p in periods if p != ref_period]

for p in periods:
    col = f"rt_{p}" if p >= 0 else f"rt_neg{abs(p)}"
    df[col] = (df['rel_time_binned'] == p).astype(int)

rt_cols = [f"rt_{p}" if p >= 0 else f"rt_neg{abs(p)}" for p in periods]

# Note: do NOT add a 'treated' dummy — entity FEs already absorb all
# time-invariant store characteristics.
formula = 'sales ~ ' + ' + '.join(rt_cols) + ' + EntityEffects + TimeEffects'
mod_es = PanelOLS.from_formula(formula, data=df.set_index(['store_id', 'week']))
res_es = mod_es.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)

# Plot event study
coefs = [res_es.params.get(c, np.nan) for c in rt_cols]
cis   = [(res_es.conf_int().loc[c, 'lower'] if c in res_es.conf_int().index else np.nan,
          res_es.conf_int().loc[c, 'upper'] if c in res_es.conf_int().index else np.nan)
         for c in rt_cols]
ci_lo = [x[0] for x in cis]
ci_hi = [x[1] for x in cis]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 5))
ax.errorbar(periods, coefs,
            yerr=[np.array(coefs) - np.array(ci_lo),
                  np.array(ci_hi) - np.array(coefs)],
            fmt='o-', capsize=3, color='steelblue', label='Estimate ± 95% CI')
ax.axhline(0,  color='red',  linestyle='--', linewidth=1)
ax.axvline(-0.5, color='gray', linestyle=':',  linewidth=1, label='Treatment onset')
ax.set_xlabel('Weeks Relative to Training Rollout')
ax.set_ylabel('Sales Effect')
ax.set_title('Event Study — Pre-Trends Check')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("event_study_pretrends.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Saved: event_study_pretrends.png")
# ← Pre-treatment coefficients should hover near zero.
#   A rising/falling trend before t=0 signals parallel trends violation.

# ── 4. Main estimate: Callaway-Sant'Anna ATT ──────────────────────────────────
# ANTICIPATION NOTE: If later-wave stores self-trained k weeks early,
# set anticipation=k below. Start with 0, then re-run with e.g. 4 or 8.

ANTICIPATION = 0   # ← set to k if informal pre-training suspected

att = ATTgt(
    data          = df,
    outcome_name  = 'sales',
    cohort_name   = 'first_treat_week',
    time_name     = 'week',
    unit_name     = 'store_id',
    anticipation  = ANTICIPATION,
    # base_period = 'universal'  # uncomment for universal base period
)
att.fit(n_jobs=-1)   # uses all CPU cores

# Aggregate to overall ATT
agg_simple = att.aggregate('simple')
print("\n── Callaway-Sant'Anna: Overall ATT ──")
print(agg_simple)

# Aggregate by cohort (Jan / Mar / Jun waves)
agg_cohort = att.aggregate('cohort')
print("\n── ATT by Cohort ──")
print(agg_cohort)

# Aggregate event-study style (dynamic effects)
agg_dynamic = att.aggregate('dynamic')
print("\n── Dynamic ATT (relative time) ──")
print(agg_dynamic)

# ── 5. Visualize dynamic ATT ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
# The differences package has a built-in plot method
att.plot(type='dynamic', title='Callaway-Sant'Anna: Dynamic Treatment Effects')
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("cs_dynamic_att.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Saved: cs_dynamic_att.png")

# ── 6. TWFE comparison (diagnostic only — not the main estimate) ──────────────
df['post'] = (df['week'] >= df['first_treat_week']).astype(int)
# 'post' varies within units over time → safe to include alongside entity FEs
twfe = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    'sales ~ post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects',
    data=df.set_index(['store_id', 'week'])
)
twfe_res = twfe.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)
print("\n── TWFE estimate (for comparison only) ──")
print(f"  Coefficient: {twfe_res.params['post']:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI: [{twfe_res.conf_int().loc['post','lower']:.4f}, "
      f"{twfe_res.conf_int().loc['post','upper']:.4f}]")
print("\n  ⚠️  TWFE is biased under heterogeneous treatment effects.")
print("       Use the Callaway-Sant'Anna estimate as your primary result.")
```

---

Run these two checks after the main analysis:

**Check 1 — Placebo outcome:** Re-run the full analysis on a sales metric that shouldn't be affected by training (e.g., number of transactions per day if training only affects basket size, or an unrelated product category). If you find a similar "effect," something else is driving the result.

**Check 2 — Anticipation sensitivity:** Re-run with `ANTICIPATION = 0, 4, 8` and compare the overall ATT. If the estimate is stable, the self-training concern isn't driving results. If it collapses, anticipation matters and you should investigate the announcement timing more carefully.

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great topic — instrument validity is one of the trickiest things to reason about in practice. I have a few IV scenarios that cover different failure modes, so one quick question before I generate:

**What difficulty level?**

- **Basic** — A clean, textbook-valid instrument. Good for building intuition on the mechanics (2SLS, first-stage F-stat, etc.) before worrying about violations.
- **Intermediate** — A weak instrument. The exclusion restriction holds, but the first stage is too feeble to trust standard 2SLS. You'll need to diagnose and apply weak-instrument-robust inference.
- **Advanced** — An instrument that *looks* valid on the surface but violates the exclusion restriction in a subtle, realistic way. You'll need to detect the problem and reason about the bias it causes.

Given your goal (understanding validity vs. invalidity), **Advanced** would hit it most directly — but Intermediate is also instructive if you want to warm up first. Your call.

</details>

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup — Confirming What I Know

Great setup — you've given me most of what I need. Let me confirm and fill in the gaps.

**What I have:**
- **Treatment**: New onboarding flow vs. existing flow
- **Outcome**: 7-day retention (binary: retained / not)
- **N**: ~10,000 users
- **Randomization**: Individual-level
- **Stage**: Analysis (data in hand)
- **Language**: Python ✓

**Two quick questions before I generate code:**

2. **Available covariates** — Do you have baseline variables (e.g., platform, user cohort, country, acquisition channel)? These are needed for the balance check and precision gains.

---

While you answer, let me walk through the assumptions so we don't waste time — these are worth understanding before touching the data.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. ✅ Random Assignment
*"Was the randomization mechanism implemented correctly? Could any user have influenced their own assignment?"*

Since this is a mobile app A/B test, the main risks are: a **leaky randomization seed** (e.g., using device ID in a way that correlates with user type), or **randomization after first action** (users who see the app launch screen before being bucketed). We'll run a balance table to verify.

> **Does your randomization happen at first app open, account creation, or somewhere else?**

---

### 2. ⚠️ SUTVA — No Interference
*"Could a user in the new onboarding flow affect the behavior of someone in the old flow?"*

For onboarding this is **usually fine** — users don't typically share their onboarding experience in ways that would change another user's retention. The main exception: **referral loops** (if a retained user can invite others who then get assigned to the control arm).

> **Does your app have a referral or social feature?** If yes, we flag this — if not, we can mark this assumption as plausible.

---

### 3. ⚠️ No Differential Attrition
*"Could users in one group be more likely to disappear from your data before the 7-day window closes?"*

This is the most common threat in mobile A/B tests. If the new onboarding flow causes more users to immediately uninstall (vs. just not returning), you'd see **lower attrition in the retention denominator for the control group** — inflating the treatment effect.

> **Is your denominator for 7-day retention "all assigned users" or "users who completed onboarding"?** This matters a lot.

---

### 4. 🔍 Compliance
*"Is everyone assigned to the new flow actually receiving it? Any chance users saw both versions?"*

One-sided non-compliance (some treatment users fell back to old flow due to bugs) is common in mobile. If so, we'll estimate both ITT (intent-to-treat) and CACE (complier average causal effect).

> **Do you have a log of who actually received each version, separate from who was assigned?**

---

### 5. ✅ No Anticipation
Not a concern here — users don't know they're in an experiment before onboarding.

---

**Assumption summary so far:**

| Assumption | Status | Key question |
|---|---|---|
| Random assignment | 🔍 Verify with balance test | Confirm randomization timing |
| SUTVA | ✅ Likely fine | Unless referral feature exists |
| No differential attrition | ⚠️ Flag | Denominator definition critical |
| Compliance | 🔍 Check | Do you have actual-receipt logs? |
| No anticipation | ✅ Clear | N/A |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation — Complete Python Code

Here's the full analysis. I've structured it so you can swap in your actual column names. I'll assume 50/50 split and that you have *some* covariates — adjust as needed.

```python
# ============================================================
# A/B Test Analysis: Onboarding Flow → 7-Day Retention
# ============================================================

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import seaborn as sns
from scipy import stats
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import statsmodels.api as sm
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_predict
from sklearn.metrics import roc_auc_score
import warnings
warnings.filterwarnings('ignore')

# ── Load your data ──────────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns:
#   user_id       : unique user identifier
#   treatment     : 1 = new onboarding, 0 = existing onboarding
#   retained_7d   : 1 = retained at 7 days, 0 = not retained
#   platform      : e.g. 'ios' / 'android'  (covariate)
#   country       : e.g. 'US', 'UK', ...    (covariate)
#   acquisition   : e.g. 'organic', 'paid'  (covariate)

df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

print(f"Total users: {len(df):,}")
print(f"Treatment: {df['treatment'].sum():,}  |  Control: {(1 - df['treatment']).sum():,}")
print(f"Overall 7-day retention: {df['retained_7d'].mean():.1%}")


# ============================================================
# STEP 1: Balance Check
# Did randomization produce comparable groups?
# ============================================================

print("\n" + "="*60)
print("STEP 1: BALANCE CHECK")
print("="*60)

covariates = ['platform_ios', 'country_US', 'acquisition_organic']
# (If covariates are categorical, you'll want to one-hot encode them first)
# Example: df = pd.get_dummies(df, columns=['platform', 'country', 'acquisition'])

balance = []
for cov in covariates:
    if cov not in df.columns:
        continue
    treated = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 1, cov]
    control = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, cov]
    pooled_std = np.sqrt((treated.var() + control.var()) / 2)
    smd = (treated.mean() - control.mean()) / pooled_std if pooled_std > 0 else 0
    stat, pval = stats.ttest_ind(treated, control)
    balance.append({
        'covariate': cov,
        'mean_treatment': treated.mean(),
        'mean_control': control.mean(),
        'SMD': round(smd, 4),
        'p_value': round(pval, 4),
        'balanced (|SMD| < 0.1)': abs(smd) < 0.1
    })

balance_df = pd.DataFrame(balance)
print(balance_df.to_string(index=False))
print("\n✅ All SMDs < 0.1 = good balance | ⚠️ SMD > 0.1 = investigate")

# ROC-AUC: can covariates predict treatment assignment?
# Should be ~0.5 if randomization worked
if covariates and all(c in df.columns for c in covariates):
    X = df[covariates].fillna(0).values
    D = df['treatment'].values
    model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000, random_state=42)
    cv_probs = cross_val_predict(model, X, D, cv=5, method='predict_proba')[:, 1]
    auc = roc_auc_score(D, cv_probs)
    print(f"\nBalance check AUC: {auc:.4f}  (target: ~0.50)")
    if auc > 0.6:
        print("⚠️  AUC > 0.6 — covariates predict treatment. Investigate randomization.")
    else:
        print("✅  AUC near 0.5 — randomization looks clean.")


# ============================================================
# STEP 2: Attrition Check
# Are users dropping from the sample at different rates?
# ============================================================

print("\n" + "="*60)
print("STEP 2: ATTRITION CHECK")
print("="*60)

# If 'retained_7d' is NaN for users who left your dataset entirely
df['missing_outcome'] = df['retained_7d'].isna().astype(int)
attrition = df.groupby('treatment').agg(
    n_users=('treatment', 'count'),
    n_missing=('missing_outcome', 'sum')
).assign(attrition_rate=lambda x: x['n_missing'] / x['n_users'])

print(attrition)

# Test for differential attrition
table = pd.crosstab(df['treatment'], df['missing_outcome'])
if table.shape == (2, 2):
    chi2, p, _, _ = stats.chi2_contingency(table)
    print(f"\nDifferential attrition test: chi2={chi2:.3f}, p={p:.4f}")
    if p < 0.05:
        print("⚠️  Differential attrition detected — results should be interpreted cautiously.")
    else:
        print("✅  No significant differential attrition.")


# ============================================================
# STEP 3: Main Effect — 7-Day Retention
# ============================================================

print("\n" + "="*60)
print("STEP 3: MAIN EFFECT ESTIMATE")
print("="*60)

# Drop rows with missing outcome for the main analysis
df_clean = df.dropna(subset=['retained_7d'])

# --- Retention rates by group ---
rates = df_clean.groupby('treatment')['retained_7d'].agg(['mean', 'count', 'sum'])
rates.index = ['Control', 'Treatment']
rates.columns = ['retention_rate', 'n_users', 'n_retained']
print("\nRetention rates:")
print(rates.round(4).to_string())

# --- Difference in proportions test ---
treat_retained = df_clean.loc[df_clean['treatment'] == 1, 'retained_7d']
ctrl_retained  = df_clean.loc[df_clean['treatment'] == 0, 'retained_7d']

p_t = treat_retained.mean()
p_c = ctrl_retained.mean()
n_t = len(treat_retained)
n_c = len(ctrl_retained)

# Risk difference (absolute effect)
risk_diff = p_t - p_c
se_rd = np.sqrt(p_t*(1-p_t)/n_t + p_c*(1-p_c)/n_c)
ci_low, ci_high = risk_diff - 1.96*se_rd, risk_diff + 1.96*se_rd
z_stat = risk_diff / se_rd
p_val = 2 * (1 - stats.norm.cdf(abs(z_stat)))

print(f"\n--- Difference in Proportions ---")
print(f"Treatment retention:  {p_t:.1%}")
print(f"Control retention:    {p_c:.1%}")
print(f"Risk difference (ATE): {risk_diff:+.1%}  (95% CI: {ci_low:+.1%} to {ci_high:+.1%})")
print(f"z-statistic: {z_stat:.3f} | p-value: {p_val:.4f}")

# Relative risk
rr = p_t / p_c
print(f"Relative risk: {rr:.3f}  ({(rr-1)*100:+.1f}% relative change in retention)")

# --- OLS regression (LPM) with covariate adjustment for precision ---
# Including pre-registered controls reduces variance, tightens CI
covariates_in_data = [c for c in covariates if c in df_clean.columns]
if covariates_in_data:
    formula_adj = f"retained_7d ~ treatment + {' + '.join(covariates_in_data)}"
else:
    formula_adj = "retained_7d ~ treatment"

model_simple = smf.ols("retained_7d ~ treatment", data=df_clean).fit(
    cov_type='HC2')  # heteroskedasticity-robust SEs (appropriate for binary outcomes)
model_adj    = smf.ols(formula_adj, data=df_clean).fit(
    cov_type='HC2')

print("\n--- OLS / Linear Probability Model ---")
print(f"Simple:   coef = {model_simple.params['treatment']:+.4f}, "
      f"SE = {model_simple.bse['treatment']:.4f}, "
      f"p = {model_simple.pvalues['treatment']:.4f}")
if covariates_in_data:
    print(f"Adjusted: coef = {model_adj.params['treatment']:+.4f}, "
          f"SE = {model_adj.bse['treatment']:.4f}, "
          f"p = {model_adj.pvalues['treatment']:.4f}")
    print("(Covariate-adjusted estimate is preferred for efficiency.)")

# --- Logistic regression for odds ratio ---
logit = smf.logit("retained_7d ~ treatment", data=df_clean).fit(disp=False)
or_val = np.exp(logit.params['treatment'])
or_ci  = np.exp(logit.conf_int().loc['treatment'])
print(f"\nOdds ratio: {or_val:.3f}  (95% CI: {or_ci[0]:.3f} to {or_ci[1]:.3f})")


# ============================================================
# STEP 4: Visualization
# ============================================================

fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 5))

# Bar chart: retention rates with CIs
groups = ['Control', 'Treatment']
means  = [p_c, p_t]
errors = [1.96 * np.sqrt(p_c*(1-p_c)/n_c), 1.96 * np.sqrt(p_t*(1-p_t)/n_t)]
colors = ['#6c757d', '#0d6efd']

axes[0].bar(groups, means, color=colors, alpha=0.8, width=0.4,
            yerr=errors, capsize=6, error_kw={'linewidth': 2})
axes[0].set_ylabel("7-Day Retention Rate")
axes[0].set_title("Retention: Treatment vs Control\n(bars = 95% CI)")
axes[0].yaxis.set_major_formatter(plt.FuncFormatter(lambda y, _: f'{y:.0%}'))
for i, (m, g) in enumerate(zip(means, groups)):
    axes[0].text(i, m + errors[i] + 0.005, f'{m:.1%}', ha='center', fontweight='bold')

# Effect size with CI
axes[1].axvline(0, color='black', linestyle='--', linewidth=1)
axes[1].errorbar(risk_diff, 0, xerr=[[risk_diff - ci_low], [ci_high - risk_diff]],
                 fmt='o', color='#0d6efd', markersize=10, capsize=8,
                 linewidth=2, label=f'Risk diff: {risk_diff:+.1%}')
axes[1].set_xlabel("Risk Difference (Treatment − Control)")
axes[1].set_title("Treatment Effect Estimate\n(point + 95% CI)")
axes[1].set_yticks([])
axes[1].axvspan(ci_low, ci_high, alpha=0.1, color='#0d6efd')

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("onboarding_ab_test_results.png", dpi=150, bbox_inches='tight')
plt.show()
print("Plot saved to onboarding_ab_test_results.png")


# ============================================================
# STEP 5: ITT vs CACE (if non-compliance exists)
# Uncomment if you have 'actual_treatment' column
# ============================================================

# from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS

# iv = IV2SLS.from_formula(
#     'retained_7d ~ 1 + [actual_treatment ~ treatment]',
#     data=df_clean
# )
# iv_res = iv.fit(cov_type='robust')
# print("\n--- CACE/LATE (IV estimate) ---")
# print(iv_res.summary)
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification Checks

I recommend two checks for this experiment:

**1. Placebo outcome** — Run the exact same analysis on a metric the new onboarding flow *shouldn't* affect, such as **Day 1 in-app purchases** or **session length before onboarding completes**. If you find a significant effect there, something is off.

**2. AA test (pre-experiment null)** — If you have a prior cohort of users who all experienced the *old* onboarding flow, split them randomly in half and run the same analysis. The estimated "effect" should be near zero. A nonzero result signals a pre-existing difference in how users are bucketed.

### experiments_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.3333333333333333, "safety": 0.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.4444444444444444}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## What You Have: Two-Sided Non-Compliance

With 15% of treated patients refusing and 5% of control patients crossing over, you have **two-sided non-compliance**. This gives you two valid estimands:

| Estimand | What it answers | Valid? |
|---|---|---|
| **ITT** | Effect of *being assigned* to the drug | ✅ Always |
| **CACE/LATE** | Effect of *actually taking* the drug, for compliers | ✅ With IV |

The CACE is what you want — but it requires using `assigned_treatment` as an instrument for `received_treatment`.

> **Why does this work?** Assignment is random (you controlled it), so it's correlated with actual receipt but has no direct effect on the outcome — the textbook exclusion restriction. The IV "scales up" the ITT by the compliance gap.

---

## Stage 2: Compliance Check

Before anything, verify the rates match what you expect:

```r
# Compliance rates
compliance_table <- table(
  assigned = df$assigned_treatment,
  received = df$received_treatment
)
print(compliance_table)

comply_treat  <- mean(df$received_treatment[df$assigned_treatment == 1])
contam_ctrl   <- mean(df$received_treatment[df$assigned_treatment == 0])
compliance_gap <- comply_treat - contam_ctrl

cat("Compliance rate (drug group):    ", comply_treat,  "\n")  # expect ~0.85
cat("Contamination rate (placebo):    ", contam_ctrl,   "\n")  # expect ~0.05
cat("Compliance gap (first stage):    ", compliance_gap,"\n")  # expect ~0.80
```

---

## Stage 3: ITT + CACE via IV

```r
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. ITT: effect of assignment ──────────────────────────────────────────
itt <- feols(outcome ~ assigned_treatment, data = df)

# ── 2. CACE/LATE via IV ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
#    Instrument: assigned_treatment → endogenous: received_treatment
cace <- feols(outcome ~ 1 | received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment,
              data = df)

# ── 3. First-stage F-statistic (must be >> 10) ───────────────────────────
first_stage <- feols(received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment, data = df)
cat("First-stage F:", fitstat(cace, "ivf")$ivf$stat, "\n")

# ── 4. Summary table ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  list("ITT" = itt, "CACE/LATE (IV)" = cace),
  gof_map  = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  stars    = TRUE,
  coef_rename = c(
    "assigned_treatment"    = "Assigned to drug (ITT)",
    "fit_received_treatment" = "Received drug (CACE)"
  )
)

# ── 5. Sanity check: CACE ≈ ITT / compliance_gap ─────────────────────────
itt_coef <- coef(itt)["assigned_treatment"]
cat("Manual CACE approximation:", round(itt_coef / compliance_gap, 4), "\n")
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpreting the Output

```
CACE = ITT / compliance_gap
     = ITT / (0.85 − 0.05)
     = ITT / 0.80
```

So the CACE will be **~25% larger** in magnitude than the ITT — reflecting that the ITT is diluted by the ~20% of patients who didn't do what they were assigned.

**Key caveats to report:**
- The CACE estimates the effect **only for compliers** (patients who would take the drug if assigned to it and wouldn't if assigned to placebo). It does not generalize to refusers.
- The IV estimate relies on the **exclusion restriction**: assignment affects outcome *only* through whether the patient received the drug. If assignment changed behavior in other ways (e.g., assigned-drug patients sought additional care), this is violated.
- Check your first-stage F — with 2,000 patients and an 80% compliance gap, it should be very strong (F >> 10). If not, something is wrong in the data.

</details>

---

### iv_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.0, "safety": 0.0, "actionable": 0.0, "overall": 0.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup — One Flag Before We Proceed

Welcome to your first IV analysis — great design choice. Before diving in, I need to flag something important about your data.

---

### 🚩 Data Mismatch

Your column names — `draft_number`, `served`, `earnings` — are the exact columns from **Angrist (1990)'s Vietnam draft lottery study**, one of the most famous IV papers ever written. That study used the Vietnam draft lottery number as an instrument for military service (`served`) on civilian earnings.

Your description, though, is a **savings product encouragement design** — phone call as instrument, product adoption as treatment, savings behavior as outcome.

Two possibilities:

| Scenario | What it means |
|---|---|
| **You're using the Angrist data as a stand-in** to test the workflow | Great — we can analyze it as-is. I'll just narrate it in that framing. |
| **Your real data has different column names** | Share the actual columns and we'll remap. |

Either way, here's how **your IV problem maps** to the framework:

---

### IV Structure

| Role | Your description | Data column (draft framing) |
|---|---|---|
| **Instrument Z** | Phone call (binary, randomized) | `draft_number` ⚠️ |
| **Endogenous treatment D** | Used savings product | `served` |
| **Outcome Y** | Savings behavior | `earnings` |

The ⚠️ on `draft_number`: it's **continuous** (values like 83, 156, 346), but your instrument is supposed to be **binary** (called vs. not called). If this is the Angrist data, the instrument is typically binarized — e.g., `eligible = (draft_number <= 195)`.

---

### Two Quick Questions to Unblock Us

1. **Is this the Angrist draft lottery data, or your actual savings data?**
2. **Is your phone call instrument already binary (0/1), or do you need to create it from a continuous eligibility score?**

Once confirmed, I'll walk you through all four IV assumptions for your specific context and generate the full Python analysis. The framework is the same regardless — just want to make sure we're naming things correctly from the start.

</details>

---

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Quick Flag Before We Dive In

Your data sample doesn't match your study description — the columns show `distance`, `education`, and `earnings`, but you described **tutoring** (treatment) and **test scores** (outcome). This looks like it might be from a different dataset (Card 1995 college proximity, perhaps?). Can you share the actual column names from your tutoring dataset?

That said, I have enough from your description to walk through the full IV setup. Let's do that now — and I want to spend real time on the weak instrument concern you flagged, because that's the most important issue here.

---

## Stage 1: Study Design

Here's what I'm working with:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Endogenous treatment** | Private tutoring (binary: yes/no, I assume) |
| **Instrument** | Tutoring center opened within 1km in past year (binary) |
| **Outcome** | Test scores |
| **N** | 3,000 students |
| **Language** | R |

**One clarifying question**: Is tutoring binary (enrolled / not enrolled) or continuous (hours per week)? This affects interpretation.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions — Let's Be Honest About Each One

### 1. Relevance (Strong First Stage) ⚠️ — Your Biggest Problem

> **Why it matters**: A weak instrument doesn't just give imprecise estimates — it gives *biased* ones, in the direction of OLS. The whole point of IV is to remove OLS bias; a weak instrument partially reintroduces it. With F < 10, your IV estimate could be *worse* than OLS.

You said "okay but not amazing." Let me be direct: **the modern threshold isn't F > 10 — it's F > 100** (Lee et al. 2022). An F of, say, 15-20 still produces meaningful bias. Here's the key intuition:

```
F ≈ 10  →  ~10% of OLS bias remains in IV estimate
F ≈ 100 →  ~1% of OLS bias remains
```

A new tutoring center opening nearby is a plausible shifter of enrollment, but proximity-based instruments in education are often weak because:
- Many families drive past the nearest center anyway
- Online tutoring dilutes the geographic effect
- Wealthier families (who tutor more) are less constrained by proximity

We'll **measure this precisely** in the code below.

---

### 2. Exclusion Restriction 🔴 — Requires Careful Argument

> **Why it matters**: The instrument must affect test scores *only* through tutoring enrollment — no other path. If tutoring center proximity affects outcomes another way, your estimate is biased regardless of sample size.

Ask yourself: **Can a new tutoring center within 1km affect test scores without anyone enrolling?**

Possible violations to think through:
- Tutoring centers sometimes offer free workshops, tutoring advice, or community study spaces → direct effect
- A new center may signal neighborhood gentrification / changing school quality
- Centers may affect *other* students' behavior (peer effects), shifting relative scores

**This is not testable directly.** You need to make a substantive case. Can you rule these out?

---

### 3. Independence (As-If Random) 🟡 — Partially Testable

> **Why it matters**: Tutoring centers don't open randomly — they open where there's demand (or profit opportunity). If wealthier, higher-achieving families cluster near new centers, your instrument is correlated with unobserved ability/motivation.

This is a real concern with geographic instruments. We can partially check this with observable balance.

---

### 4. Monotonicity 🟢 — Likely Plausible

> **Why it matters**: The instrument should push everyone the same direction. A center opening nearby shouldn't *decrease* tutoring enrollment for anyone.

This is plausible here — hard to imagine a new nearby tutoring center reducing someone's probability of enrolling. One edge case: if a center opening nearby displaces an existing center that was cheaper/better, some families might switch away from tutoring entirely. Unlikely but worth noting.

**LATE interpretation**: Your IV estimate applies to **compliers** — students who enrolled *because* a center opened nearby (they wouldn't have otherwise). This excludes students who would always/never tutor regardless. Keep this in mind when interpreting the magnitude.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Once you confirm variable names, here's the full analysis:

```r
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)
library(cobalt)

# ── 0. Rename to match your actual columns ────────────────────────────────────
# df <- your_data
# Adjust these:
# treatment  = tutoring (binary 0/1)
# instrument = center_opened (binary 0/1: tutoring center within 1km in past year)
# outcome    = test_score
# Controls   = whatever pre-treatment covariates you have (e.g., prior_score, income_decile)

# ── 1. FIRST STAGE: How strong is the instrument? ────────────────────────────
first_stage <- feols(tutoring ~ center_opened + prior_score + income_decile,
                     data = df, cluster = ~school_id)

summary(first_stage)

# The critical number
fs_f <- fitstat(first_stage, "ivf")$ivf$stat
cat("\n=== First-stage F-statistic:", round(fs_f, 2), "===\n")
cat("Stock-Yogo minimum (10% max bias): 16.38\n")
cat("Lee et al. 2022 recommended: >100 for reliable inference\n\n")

if (fs_f < 10) {
  cat("⛔ WEAK INSTRUMENT: F < 10. IV estimates are unreliable.\n")
} else if (fs_f < 100) {
  cat("⚠️  BORDERLINE: F between 10-100. Some bias remains. Consider LIML.\n")
} else {
  cat("✅ STRONG instrument. Proceed with 2SLS.\n")
}

# ── 2. NAIVE OLS (baseline — shows the bias we're trying to remove) ───────────
ols_model <- feols(test_score ~ tutoring + prior_score + income_decile,
                   data = df, cluster = ~school_id)

# ── 3. 2SLS ESTIMATION ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
iv_model <- feols(test_score ~ prior_score + income_decile |
                    tutoring ~ center_opened,
                  data = df, cluster = ~school_id)

# ── 4. Results side-by-side ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  list("OLS (biased)" = ols_model, "IV / 2SLS" = iv_model),
  stars = TRUE,
  gof_map = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title = "Effect of Private Tutoring on Test Scores"
)

# The gap between OLS and IV tells you the direction of selection bias:
# IV > OLS  → negative selection (lower-ability students enrolled more)
# IV < OLS  → positive selection (higher-ability / motivated students enrolled more)
# IV ≈ OLS  → either no endogeneity, or weak instrument absorbed the signal

# ── 5. If F is borderline: try LIML (less biased under weak instruments) ──────
liml_model <- feols(test_score ~ prior_score + income_decile |
                      tutoring ~ center_opened,
                    data = df, cluster = ~school_id,
                    lean = FALSE)  # fixest uses LIML when specified

# Compare: if 2SLS and LIML are very different, weak instrument bias is real
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification Checks

```r
# ── CHECK 1: Reduced form ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Regress outcome directly on instrument (skip the treatment)
# If the instrument is valid AND relevant, this should be significant.
# Reduced form is always more robust than 2SLS — report it alongside.
reduced_form <- feols(test_score ~ center_opened + prior_score + income_decile,
                      data = df, cluster = ~school_id)
summary(reduced_form)

# Sanity check: reduced_form coef ≈ first_stage coef × IV coef
cat("Reduced form / First stage ≈ IV estimate?\n")
cat("RF coef:", coef(reduced_form)["center_opened"], "\n")
cat("FS coef:", coef(first_stage)["center_opened"], "\n")
cat("Implied IV:", coef(reduced_form)["center_opened"] /
                   coef(first_stage)["center_opened"], "\n")

# ── CHECK 2: Balance — is the instrument as-if random? ────────────────────────
# The instrument should NOT be correlated with pre-treatment covariates
balance <- bal.tab(center_opened ~ prior_score + income_decile + distance_to_school,
                   data = df,
                   binary = "std", continuous = "std",
                   thresholds = c(m = 0.1))
print(balance)
love.plot(balance, threshold = 0.1,
          title = "Covariate Balance: Center Opened vs. Not")

# ── CHECK 3: Placebo test ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Use a pre-treatment outcome as the "outcome" — IV should have no effect
# (If you have a prior-year test score and can treat it as the outcome)
placebo_iv <- feols(prior_test_score ~ prior_score + income_decile |
                      tutoring ~ center_opened,
                    data = df, cluster = ~school_id)
summary(placebo_iv)
# A significant effect here is a red flag for exclusion violation
```

---

## The Weak Instrument Problem — What To Do

Since you flagged "okay but not amazing," here's your decision tree based on what the F-statistic turns out to be:

| F-statistic | Diagnosis | Recommended action |
|---|---|---|
| < 10 | Weak instrument | Don't proceed with IV. Report reduced form. Find better instrument. |
| 10–30 | Borderline | Use LIML instead of 2SLS. Report Anderson-Rubin CIs. Be honest in write-up. |
| 30–100 | Acceptable | Proceed, but report sensitivity. LIML as robustness check. |
| > 100 | Strong | Standard 2SLS is fine. |

For the **Anderson-Rubin confidence interval** (valid even with weak instruments):
```r
library(AER)

iv_aer <- ivreg(test_score ~ tutoring + prior_score + income_decile |
                  center_opened + prior_score + income_decile,
                data = df)

# AR confidence interval — robust to weak instruments
summary(iv_aer, diagnostics = TRUE)
# Look at: Wu-Hausman (tests endogeneity), Weak instruments (F-stat), Sargan (if >1 instrument)
```

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Good setup. Let me walk through all five stages. I'll flag the one column naming note upfront: your sample shows `x1`, `x2` — I'll write the code using your actual covariate names (`age`, `bmi`, `bp`, `severity`, `comorbidity`). Adjust if your column names differ.
