## Stage 1: Setup Confirmation

Here's what I'm working with:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | New drug (1) vs. standard care (0) |
| **Outcome** | Recovery time (continuous — lower = better) |
| **Covariates** | `age`, `bmi`, `bp`, `severity`, `comorbidity` |
| **N** | 2,000 (1,000 treated / 1,000 control) |
| **Language** | R |
| **Method** | Propensity Score Matching (MatchIt) + IPW fallback |

**One question before we proceed**: Do you want the **ATT** (effect of the drug *on patients who received it*) or the **ATE** (effect averaged over *all* 2,000 patients)?

- **ATT** is usually the right choice medically: "for patients who got this drug, did it help?"
- **ATE** answers: "if we gave this to everyone, what would happen on average?"

I'll assume **ATT** below (you can flip with one argument) since the 50/50 split suggests you're studying patients who were selected into treatment.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

I'll walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. Conditional Independence / Unconfoundedness ⚠️ *Untestable — most critical*

**What it means here**: After conditioning on age, BMI, blood pressure, severity, and comorbidity count, the reason a patient received the new drug vs. standard care is unrelated to how quickly they would have recovered anyway.

**Why it's hard**: Doctors likely prescribed the new drug *selectively* — perhaps to sicker patients, or healthier ones, or based on clinical intuition. If that selection criterion isn't fully captured in your 5 covariates, the estimate is biased.

**Questions to ask yourself**:
- Was treatment assigned based on physician discretion? If so, what drove that discretion?
- Is there a *severity threshold* or protocol that determined treatment — something like "only prescribed to patients with severity > X"? (That might actually be an RDD!)
- Could referral patterns, insurance, or hospital site affect both treatment and recovery?

**Red flag**: Disease severity score and comorbidity count are good. But if physicians also used lab values, imaging, or subjective judgment not in your data, CIA is likely violated. You'll need Rosenbaum bounds (Stage 4) to quantify the risk.

---

### 2. Overlap / Positivity ✅ *Testable — we'll check in Stage 3*

**What it means here**: Every type of patient (every combination of age/BMI/BP/severity/comorbidity) must have had *some chance* of receiving either treatment. If, say, all patients with severity > 8 received the new drug deterministically, we can't estimate the effect for those patients.

**Why it matters**: With 1,000 patients per group and 5 continuous covariates, overlap *could* be good — but we need to verify. The propensity score histogram will tell us immediately.

**Risk here**: Severity score and comorbidity count are the most likely sources of poor overlap. We'll check.

---

### 3. SUTVA (No Interference) ✅ *Likely satisfied*

**What it means here**: Patient A's treatment doesn't affect Patient B's recovery time.

**Assessment**: In a drug trial setting this is almost always fine — the drug affects each patient's biology independently. There's no obvious mechanism for interference unless patients share a care team whose capacity is constrained. I'll flag it but don't see a red flag here.

---

### 4. Correct Propensity Score Specification ✅ *Testable via balance*

**What it means here**: The logistic regression we use to estimate the propensity score (P(treatment | covariates)) must capture the true selection process. If treatment depends on *interactions* between severity and age, a main-effects-only model will produce bad weights.

**The good news**: For matching, we don't need the propensity score model to be statistically "correct" — we just need it to produce good covariate balance. We'll check balance with standardized mean differences (SMD < 0.1 threshold) and iterate if needed.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Testable? | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|---|
| Conditional independence | ❌ No | ⚠️ Needs defense | Rosenbaum bounds + E-value |
| Overlap | ✅ Yes | 🔲 Check PS histogram | Code below |
| SUTVA | ❌ No | ✅ Likely fine | Acknowledge in writeup |
| Correct PS model | ✅ Indirect | 🔲 Check balance (SMD) | Iterate if needed |

> **Honest warning**: This is selection-on-observables identification. If physicians used *any* information not in your 5 covariates to assign treatment, the estimate is biased. We'll quantify that risk in Stage 4 — but we can't eliminate it.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Complete R analysis — copy and adapt column names to your data.

```r
# ============================================================
# MATCHING ANALYSIS: Drug vs. Standard Care → Recovery Time
# ============================================================

library(MatchIt)       # matching engine
library(cobalt)        # balance diagnostics
library(marginaleffects) # ATT estimation
library(ggplot2)
library(dplyr)

# ── 0. Inspect raw data ──────────────────────────────────────
glimpse(df)

# Define covariate formula — update names if needed
covs <- c("age", "bmi", "bp", "severity", "comorbidity")
ps_formula <- as.formula(
  paste("treatment ~", paste(covs, collapse = " + "))
)

# ── 1. PRE-MATCH: Overlap check ──────────────────────────────
# Estimate propensity scores before matching to check overlap
m_null <- matchit(ps_formula, data = df,
                  method = NULL,          # no matching, just diagnostics
                  estimand = "ATT")

ps_scores <- data.frame(
  ps        = m_null$distance,
  treatment = df$treatment
)

ggplot(ps_scores, aes(x = ps, fill = factor(treatment))) +
  geom_histogram(alpha = 0.55, position = "identity", bins = 50) +
  scale_fill_manual(values = c("#3B82F6", "#EF4444"),
                    labels = c("Control", "Treated")) +
  labs(title = "Propensity Score Overlap (Pre-Matching)",
       x = "P(Treatment | Covariates)", y = "Count", fill = NULL) +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 13)

# Flag extreme propensity scores
cat("PS < 0.05 (near-zero treatment probability):", 
    mean(ps_scores$ps < 0.05), "\n")
cat("PS > 0.95 (near-certain treatment):          ", 
    mean(ps_scores$ps > 0.95), "\n")
# If either exceeds ~5%, overlap is a concern

# ── 2. MATCHING ───────────────────────────────────────────────
# Nearest-neighbor PSM, 1:1 without replacement, caliper = 0.2 SD
m_out <- matchit(
  ps_formula,
  data     = df,
  method   = "nearest",
  distance = "glm",          # logistic propensity score
  estimand = "ATT",
  ratio    = 1,
  replace  = FALSE,
  caliper  = 0.2,            # discard treated units with no close match
  std.caliper = TRUE         # caliper in SD units of the PS
)

print(summary(m_out, un = FALSE))
# Check: how many treated units were dropped due to caliper?
# m_out$nn gives matched/unmatched counts

# ── 3. BALANCE DIAGNOSTICS ───────────────────────────────────
# Standardized Mean Differences — target: all |SMD| < 0.10
bal <- bal.tab(m_out,
               thresholds = c(m = 0.1, v = 2),  # SMD + variance ratio
               un = TRUE)    # show pre- and post-match side by side
print(bal)

# Love plot — the standard visualization for covariate balance
love.plot(m_out,
          thresholds = c(m = 0.1),
          var.order  = "unadjusted",
          title      = "Covariate Balance: Before vs After Matching",
          colors     = c("#94A3B8", "#3B82F6"))

# ── 4. TREATMENT EFFECT ESTIMATION ───────────────────────────
# Extract matched dataset (includes matching weights)
m_data <- match.data(m_out)

# Regression on matched data with covariate adjustment
# (adds variance reduction; cluster SEs on matched pairs via `subclass`)
model <- lm(
  outcome ~ treatment + age + bmi + bp + severity + comorbidity,
  data    = m_data,
  weights = weights
)

# ATT via average marginal comparisons
att_est <- avg_comparisons(
  model,
  variables = "treatment",
  vcov      = ~subclass,    # cluster by matched pair
  newdata   = subset(m_data, treatment == 1),
  wts       = "weights"
)
print(att_est)
# Interpretation: ATT = expected change in recovery time (days)
#                 for treated patients due to the drug
#                 Negative = drug reduces recovery time ✓
```

> **Reading the output**: Look for `Estimate` (the ATT), `Std. Error`, and the 95% CI. A negative ATT means the drug reduces recovery time. Check the `bal.tab()` output — if any SMD exceeds 0.10 after matching, re-specify below.

**If balance is poor after matching**, try a more flexible specification:

```r
# Option A: Add interaction + polynomial terms to PS model
ps_formula_flex <- treatment ~ age + bmi + bp + severity + comorbidity +
                               I(age^2) + I(severity^2) +
                               age:severity + bmi:comorbidity

m_out_flex <- matchit(ps_formula_flex, data = df,
                      method = "nearest", estimand = "ATT",
                      caliper = 0.2, std.caliper = TRUE)
bal.tab(m_out_flex, thresholds = c(m = 0.1))

# Option B: Full matching (uses all units, often better balance)
m_out_full <- matchit(ps_formula, data = df,
                      method = "full", estimand = "ATT",
                      distance = "glm")
bal.tab(m_out_full, thresholds = c(m = 0.1))
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

Two checks I strongly recommend given the CIA concern:

### Check 1 — Rosenbaum Sensitivity Bounds

How strong would an *unmeasured confounder* need to be to explain away the estimated effect?

```r
library(rbounds)

# Extract matched pairs: outcomes for treated and matched controls
m_data_sorted <- m_data[order(m_data$subclass, m_data$treatment), ]

treated_outcomes <- m_data_sorted$outcome[m_data_sorted$treatment == 1]
control_outcomes <- m_data_sorted$outcome[m_data_sorted$treatment == 0]

# Gamma = 1 means no hidden bias; increase until p-value exceeds 0.05
psens(treated_outcomes, control_outcomes,
      Gamma    = 3,      # test up to 3x odds ratio of unmeasured confounding
      GammaInc = 0.25)   # step size

# Interpretation: if Gamma = 1.5 and p > 0.05, the conclusion is fragile
# (a confounder increasing treatment odds by 50% would overturn it)
# If Gamma = 3.0 and still p < 0.05, the result is robust
```

### Check 2 — Placebo Outcome

Pick a covariate that the drug *cannot possibly affect* (it was measured at baseline). Re-run the full analysis using it as the "outcome."

```r
# Example: use baseline BMI as placebo outcome
# (Drug cannot retroactively change pre-treatment BMI)
model_placebo <- lm(
  bmi ~ treatment + age + bp + severity + comorbidity,
  data    = m_data,
  weights = weights
)

placebo_est <- avg_comparisons(
  model_placebo,
  variables = "treatment",
  vcov      = ~subclass,
  newdata   = subset(m_data, treatment == 1),
  wts       = "weights"
)
print(placebo_est)
# Goal: estimate ≈ 0, p > 0.05 — no spurious "effect" on baseline BMI
# A significant estimate = residual imbalance = trouble
```

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you have your estimates, here's the structure for reporting:

> *"We estimated the average treatment effect on the treated (ATT) using 1:1 nearest-neighbor propensity score matching with a 0.2 SD caliper. The propensity score was estimated via logistic regression on age, BMI, blood pressure, disease severity, and comorbidity count.*
>
> *After matching, all standardized mean differences fell below 0.10 [show love plot], indicating adequate covariate balance.*
>
> *The estimated ATT is **[X] days** (95% CI: [lower, upper]). Patients who received the new drug recovered approximately [X] days [faster/slower] than they would have under standard care.*
>
> **Caveats**: This estimate is credible only if the measured covariates fully capture the selection process for treatment. If physicians used unmeasured information (lab values, imaging, judgment), the estimate is biased. Rosenbaum sensitivity analysis indicates that [an unmeasured confounder would need to increase treatment odds by Γ = X before overturning the conclusion / the result is sensitive at Γ = 1.5, warranting caution]."*

3. **Run the Rosenbaum bounds** — the Gamma value is your headline robustness number.

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## What You're Working With — A Candid Assessment

Before we dive into code, I want to be upfront: **this analysis has two serious problems that need to be addressed head-on**, not papered over.

---

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Treatment | Voluntary wellness program enrollment |
| Outcome | Productivity |
| Covariates in data | `age`, `tenure`, `department` |
| Known confounders **missing from data** | Health status, income |
| n | 5,000 |
| Estimand | ATT (effect on those who enrolled) |
| Language | Python |

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions — Walking Through Each One

### 🔴 1. Conditional Independence (Unconfoundedness) — **Likely Violated**

This is the big one. It says: *after conditioning on your measured covariates, there's no remaining reason why enrollees and non-enrollees would have different productivity absent the program.*

**For your study, this is very hard to believe.** Voluntary enrollment is driven by health motivation, conscientiousness, self-discipline — traits that independently drive productivity. And you've already told me two major confounders aren't in your dataset:

- **Health status** → affects both enrollment and energy/absenteeism/productivity
- **Income** → affects both ability to participate and job performance

You have `age`, `tenure`, and `department`. These help, but they don't close the back door. The omitted variables here are not marginal — they're central to why someone enrolls voluntarily.

> ⚠️ **Plain English**: Enrollees are likely just "higher-performing people" for reasons unrelated to the program. Matching on age and tenure won't fully control for that.

**Mitigation**: You can't fix this with matching alone. At minimum, include income and health proxies if you have them anywhere (claims data, salary bands, prior sick days). Then report sensitivity analysis (E-value) to quantify how bad the bias could be.

---

### 🔴 2. Overlap / Positivity — **Violated (Your Own Diagnosis)**

You've already flagged this: propensity score distributions barely overlap. This means:

- For older, lower-income, less-healthy employees → near-zero probability of enrollment
- For younger, healthier, higher-income employees → near-certain enrollment
- **Matching these groups is extrapolation, not comparison**

This also tells you something important: the ATT (effect on *enrollees*) is the only defensible estimand here. Estimating ATE (effect on everyone) would require extrapolating into regions with no data.

**Mitigation**: Trim to the region of common support. Be explicit that your estimates only apply to the subset of the population where comparison is possible.

---

### 🟡 3. SUTVA (No Interference) — **Probably Okay, Watch For Team Effects**

Do enrolled employees affect their colleagues' productivity? Possible if they share wellness tips or if managers push teams together. Worth acknowledging but likely not the primary threat here.

---

### 🟡 4. Model Specification — **Testable via Balance**

With only `age`, `tenure`, and `department`, the propensity score model is almost certainly underspecified (missing income, health). Doubly-robust estimation helps here — it only needs *one* model (PS or outcome) to be right — but if both are misspecified due to missing variables, DR doesn't save you.

---

### Summary

| Assumption | Status | Testable? |
|---|---|---|
| Conditional independence | 🔴 Likely violated (missing confounders) | No |
| Overlap | 🔴 Violated — trim required | Yes |
| SUTVA | 🟡 Probably holds | Partially |
| Model specification | 🟡 Underspecified covariates | Yes (via balance) |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Given the poor overlap and missing confounders, here's the recommended approach:
1. Visualize the overlap problem explicitly
2. Trim to common support
3. Use **Doubly-Robust (AIPW)** on the trimmed sample — it's the most defensible estimator when you're uncertain about model specification
4. Report balance before/after

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from econml.dr import LinearDRLearner
from sklearn.ensemble import GradientBoostingClassifier, GradientBoostingRegressor
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import warnings
warnings.filterwarnings('ignore')

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv('your_data.csv')

# ── 1. Define covariates ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
covariates = ['age', 'tenure', 'department']
treatment   = 'treated'
outcome     = 'productivity'

X = pd.get_dummies(df[covariates], columns=['department'], drop_first=True)
D = df[treatment].values
Y = df[outcome].values
covariate_names = X.columns.tolist()

# ── 2. Estimate propensity scores ─────────────────────────────────────────────
scaler = StandardScaler()
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000, C=1.0)
ps_model.fit(X_scaled, D)
ps = ps_model.predict_proba(X_scaled)[:, 1]
df['ps'] = ps

print("=== Propensity Score Summary ===")
print(f"Treated  — mean: {ps[D==1].mean():.3f}, range: [{ps[D==1].min():.3f}, {ps[D==1].max():.3f}]")
print(f"Control  — mean: {ps[D==0].mean():.3f}, range: [{ps[D==0].min():.3f}, {ps[D==0].max():.3f}]")

# ── 3. Visualize overlap ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 4))

# Histogram
axes[0].hist(ps[D==1], bins=50, alpha=0.6, label='Enrolled (treated)', density=True, color='steelblue')
axes[0].hist(ps[D==0], bins=50, alpha=0.6, label='Not enrolled (control)', density=True, color='coral')
axes[0].set_xlabel('Propensity Score')
axes[0].set_ylabel('Density')
axes[0].set_title('Propensity Score Overlap\n⚠ Poor overlap = limited comparability')
axes[0].legend()

# Mirrored histogram (clearer for poor overlap)
bins = np.linspace(0, 1, 51)
axes[1].barh(bins[:-1], np.histogram(ps[D==1], bins=bins, density=True)[0],
             height=np.diff(bins), alpha=0.6, label='Enrolled', color='steelblue')
axes[1].barh(bins[:-1], -np.histogram(ps[D==0], bins=bins, density=True)[0],
             height=np.diff(bins), alpha=0.6, label='Not enrolled', color='coral')
axes[1].axvline(0, color='black', lw=0.8)
axes[1].set_xlabel('← Control | Treated →')
axes[1].set_title('Mirrored Overlap Plot')
axes[1].legend()

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('overlap_plot.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 4. Trim to common support ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Drop units with PS outside the range of the treated group's PS distribution
ps_min = ps[D==1].min()
ps_max = ps[D==1].max()

# Also trim extremes (below 5th percentile of treated, above 95th of control)
ps_lo = np.percentile(ps[D==1], 5)
ps_hi = np.percentile(ps[D==0], 95)

in_support = (ps >= ps_lo) & (ps <= ps_hi)
df['in_support'] = in_support

n_dropped_treated = ((D==1) & ~in_support).sum()
n_dropped_control = ((D==0) & ~in_support).sum()
print(f"\n=== Common Support Trimming ===")
print(f"PS trimming range: [{ps_lo:.3f}, {ps_hi:.3f}]")
print(f"Treated dropped: {n_dropped_treated} ({100*n_dropped_treated/D.sum():.1f}%)")
print(f"Control dropped: {n_dropped_control} ({100*n_dropped_control/(1-D).sum():.1f}%)")
print(f"Remaining sample: {in_support.sum()} / {len(df)}")

# Warn if large fraction of treated dropped — estimand changes
if n_dropped_treated / D.sum() > 0.10:
    print(f"\n⚠ WARNING: {100*n_dropped_treated/D.sum():.0f}% of treated units dropped.")
    print("  Your estimate applies only to enrollees WITH comparable controls.")
    print("  Generalizability to all enrollees is limited.")

df_trim = df[in_support].copy()
X_trim = pd.get_dummies(df_trim[covariates], columns=['department'], drop_first=True)
D_trim = df_trim[treatment].values
Y_trim = df_trim[outcome].values
X_trim_scaled = scaler.transform(X_trim)

# ── 5. Balance diagnostics ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
def compute_smd(df_subset, treatment_col, covariates):
    """Standardized mean difference for each covariate."""
    rows = []
    for col in covariates:
        treated_vals  = df_subset.loc[df_subset[treatment_col]==1, col]
        control_vals  = df_subset.loc[df_subset[treatment_col]==0, col]
        pooled_sd = np.sqrt((treated_vals.var() + control_vals.var()) / 2)
        smd = (treated_vals.mean() - control_vals.mean()) / pooled_sd if pooled_sd > 0 else np.nan
        rows.append({'covariate': col, 'SMD': smd})
    return pd.DataFrame(rows)

# Compute SMD on numeric covariates before and after trimming
numeric_covs = ['age', 'tenure']
smd_before = compute_smd(df,      treatment, numeric_covs).rename(columns={'SMD': 'SMD_before'})
smd_after  = compute_smd(df_trim, treatment, numeric_covs).rename(columns={'SMD': 'SMD_after'})
balance    = smd_before.merge(smd_after, on='covariate')
balance['balanced'] = balance['SMD_after'].abs() < 0.1
print("\n=== Covariate Balance (SMD) ===")
print(balance.to_string(index=False))
print("\nRule of thumb: |SMD| < 0.1 = balanced, < 0.25 = acceptable")

# Love plot
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 4))
y = range(len(balance))
ax.scatter(balance['SMD_before'], y, marker='o', color='coral', label='Before trimming', zorder=3)
ax.scatter(balance['SMD_after'],  y, marker='D', color='steelblue', label='After trimming', zorder=3)
ax.axvline(0,    color='black', lw=0.8)
ax.axvline(0.1,  color='green',  lw=0.8, linestyle='--', alpha=0.7)
ax.axvline(-0.1, color='green',  lw=0.8, linestyle='--', alpha=0.7)
ax.set_yticks(y)
ax.set_yticklabels(balance['covariate'])
ax.set_xlabel('Standardized Mean Difference')
ax.set_title('Love Plot: Covariate Balance')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('love_plot.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 6. Doubly-Robust estimation on trimmed sample ─────────────────────────────
# DRLearner: consistent if EITHER propensity OR outcome model is correct
dr = LinearDRLearner(
    model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=100, max_depth=3),
    model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=100, max_depth=3),
    cv=5
)
dr.fit(Y=Y_trim, T=D_trim, X=None, W=X_trim.values)

ate     = dr.ate()
ate_ci  = dr.ate_interval(alpha=0.05)

print(f"\n=== Doubly-Robust ATT Estimate (Trimmed Sample) ===")
print(f"ATE estimate : {ate:.3f} productivity units")
print(f"95% CI       : [{ate_ci[0]:.3f}, {ate_ci[1]:.3f}]")
print(f"\n⚠ This estimate applies to the {in_support.sum()} employees in the region")
print(f"  of common support — NOT necessarily all enrollees.")

# ── 7. Naive OLS comparison (for perspective) ──────────────────────────────────
model_naive = smf.ols('productivity ~ treated + age + tenure + C(department)',
                       data=df).fit()
model_trim  = smf.ols('productivity ~ treated + age + tenure + C(department)',
                       data=df_trim).fit()

print(f"\n=== Naive OLS (for comparison — likely biased) ===")
print(f"Full sample  : {model_naive.params['treated']:.3f} "
      f"(95% CI: [{model_naive.conf_int().loc['treated', 0]:.3f}, "
      f"{model_naive.conf_int().loc['treated', 1]:.3f}])")
print(f"Trimmed only : {model_trim.params['treated']:.3f} "
      f"(95% CI: [{model_trim.conf_int().loc['treated', 0]:.3f}, "
      f"{model_trim.conf_int().loc['treated', 1]:.3f}])")
print("Note: OLS is shown for reference; DR is preferred.")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification / Robustness

Two checks I'd prioritize for your study:

**Check 1 — Placebo outcome** (most important given CIA concerns)
Run the same analysis on a covariate that *definitely shouldn't change* due to a wellness program — for example, `tenure` or `age`. If you find a significant "effect," that's a red flag for residual confounding.

```python
# Placebo: use tenure as the outcome
dr_placebo = LinearDRLearner(
    model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=100, max_depth=3),
    model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=100, max_depth=3),
    cv=5
)
dr_placebo.fit(Y=df_trim['tenure'].values, T=D_trim, X=None, W=X_trim.values)
placebo_ate = dr_placebo.ate()
placebo_ci  = dr_placebo.ate_interval(alpha=0.05)

print(f"=== Placebo Test (outcome = tenure) ===")
print(f"Placebo ATE: {placebo_ate:.3f} (95% CI: [{placebo_ci[0]:.3f}, {placebo_ci[1]:.3f}])")
print("Expectation: estimate near zero, CI includes zero.")
print("If significant → residual confounding is present.")
```

**Check 2 — E-value** (how robust is the estimate to unmeasured confounding?)

```python
import numpy as np

def e_value_md(effect_size, se):
    """
    E-value for mean differences (approximate, via risk ratio conversion).
    Converts effect to Cohen's d, then approximates E-value.
    """
    # Approximate RR from standardized effect (Chinn 2000)
    d = effect_size / se  # crude standardization
    rr_approx = np.exp(0.91 * d)
    e_val = rr_approx + np.sqrt(rr_approx * (rr_approx - 1))
    return rr_approx, e_val

rr_approx, e_val = e_value_md(ate, (ate_ci[1] - ate_ci[0]) / (2 * 1.96))
print(f"\n=== Sensitivity: E-Value ===")
print(f"Approximate RR: {rr_approx:.2f}")
print(f"E-value: {e_val:.2f}")
print(f"Interpretation: An unmeasured confounder would need to be associated")
print(f"with both enrollment and productivity by a factor of {e_val:.1f}x")
print(f"to fully explain away the observed effect.")
print(f"Given that health motivation and income are unmeasured,")
print(f"ask: could they plausibly be that strong? If yes, be skeptical.")
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you have your numbers, here's how to report this honestly:

> **Wellness Program Analysis — Estimated Effect on Productivity**
>
> Using doubly-robust estimation restricted to the region of common support (*n* = [X] employees), the estimated effect of wellness program enrollment on productivity is **[X] units** (95% CI: [lo, hi]).
>
> **Critical caveats**:
> - This estimate relies on the assumption that all confounders are captured by `age`, `tenure`, and `department`. Two known predictors of enrollment — **employee income and health status** — are not in the analysis. This is a meaningful gap.
> - The propensity score distributions showed **poor overlap**, meaning we could only compare a subset of enrolled employees to comparable non-enrollees. Results may not generalize to all enrollees.
> - The E-value for this estimate is [X]. An unmeasured confounder with an association of [X]× with both enrollment and productivity could explain away this result. Given the nature of voluntary enrollment, this threshold may be achievable.
> - **This is selection on observables, the weakest identification strategy.** If you have pre-enrollment productivity records for these employees, a difference-in-differences design would be substantially more credible.

---

## The Honest Bottom Line

| Issue | Severity | Fixable with matching? |
|---|---|---|
| Self-selection into voluntary program | 🔴 Critical | No |
| Missing confounders (income, health) | 🔴 Critical | Only if you add the data |
| Poor overlap | 🔴 Critical | Partially (via trimming, but estimate scope shrinks) |

**My recommendation**: Before relying on this analysis, check if you have **pre-program productivity data**. If yes, DiD would give you a much stronger identification strategy — I can help you build that instead.

If matching is the only option, the code above gives you the most defensible version of it, but the write-up should be explicit that unobserved confounding is a serious and unresolvable threat with this design.

</details>

### planner_pedagogy_ambiguous

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 0.5, "overall": 0.5555555555555555}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a well-identified problem — and you actually have **two overlapping identification strategies** that target different causal questions. Let me lead with the recommendation.

---

## Preliminary Recommendation: Synthetic Control + Fuzzy RDD

You have two distinct sources of causal variation, and they're both strong:

| Layer | Variation | Method | Estimand |
|---|---|---|---|
| **City-level** | 1 city changed algorithm vs. 49 that didn't | **Synthetic Control** | ATE on the treated city |
| **Driver-level** | Score 80 threshold determines early eligibility; only 30% opted in | **Fuzzy RDD** | LATE for drivers near the threshold |

These aren't competing — they answer slightly different questions and can **triangulate** each other.

---

### Why Synthetic Control for the city-level question

Your setup is almost textbook SC:
- **1 treated unit** (the city), **49 donors** — SC loves this ratio
- **12 pre-treatment months** — enough to construct a tight synthetic match
- **6 post-treatment months** — enough to see an effect

SC will build a weighted combination of the other 49 cities that matches your treated city's pre-treatment earnings trajectory, then compare post-treatment divergence. The key assumption is that no other structural shock hit your treated city at the same time as the algorithm change.

### Why Fuzzy RDD for the driver-level question

The score 80 threshold is a near-perfect instrument:
- Drivers **just above 80** were eligible to opt in early; drivers **just below 80** were not
- But only **30% of eligible drivers actually opted in** → this is *non-compliance*, which makes it a **Fuzzy RDD** (the threshold shifts the probability of treatment, not treatment itself deterministically)
- Fuzzy RDD is estimated as a ratio of the outcome discontinuity to the treatment take-up discontinuity — it's mechanically IV, using the threshold as the instrument
- This estimates the **LATE for compliers near the threshold** — drivers near score 80 who adopted because they were eligible

---

### A few things I want to clarify before saving a plan:

1. **What's the primary question?** City-level impact (aggregate driver earnings across the city) or driver-level impact (individual driver earnings)? Or both? This determines which method is the headline.

2. **Is the "driver score" time-varying or fixed?** If it changes over time, there's a risk drivers could manipulate their score to cross the threshold — a classic RDD validity concern.

3. **Did the 30% who opted in skew toward high-scoring or high-earning drivers?** This affects how we interpret the LATE and whether the RDD compliers are representative of the broader population.

4. **Is the city-level change a hard switch** (all drivers flipped at once) or did it roll out alongside the early opt-in? The timeline matters for whether SC and RDD are measuring the same treatment.

</details>

---

### planner_pedagogy_clear

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 0.5, "overall": 0.8333333333333334}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **Difference-in-Differences (DiD)** problem — specifically a classic TWFE setup with a single treatment date. You have everything you need: panel data, pre/post periods, treated and control stores observed throughout.

A couple of quick questions to sharpen the plan:

**1. Were all 10 stores launched on the same date**, or did some stores roll out earlier or later than others?

> This matters a lot. If the rollout was simultaneous → standard TWFE works cleanly. If staggered across weeks or months → naive TWFE is biased and we'd use Callaway-Sant'Anna or Sun-Abraham instead.

**2. How were the 10 stores selected for the pilot?**

> For example: were they chosen because they were underperforming, the largest stores, stores in a specific region? This affects whether parallel trends is plausible — DiD's central assumption is that treated and control stores would have grown similarly *if the program hadn't launched*.

---

While you answer those, here's a quick sketch of what the analysis will look like either way:

| Your Setup | Method |
|---|---|
| Single launch date | TWFE with `fixest::feols()` |
| Staggered launch | Callaway-Sant'Anna `did::att_gt()` |

Both will include **store fixed effects** (absorbs time-invariant store differences) and **week fixed effects** (absorbs economy-wide trends), and we'll run an **event study plot** to verify pre-trends before trusting the estimate.

</details>

---

### rdd_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.8888888888888888}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup — Confirming the Design

Great setup for a first RDD. Based on what you've shared, here's what I'm working with:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Running variable | `test_score` |
| Cutoff | 70 |
| Treatment | `scholarship` (honors program admission) |
| Outcome | `gpa` |
| N | 5,000 |
| Design | **Sharp RDD** |

**Sharp RDD** because admission is deterministic — everyone scoring ≥ 70 gets in, everyone below doesn't. That means units just above and just below the cutoff are nearly identical in every way *except* they were admitted or not, which is exactly the variation we exploit.

> ⚠️ **Quick flag**: Your column is named `scholarship` but you described it as "honors program admission." If these are actually two different things (e.g., some students above 70 get the scholarship AND the honors program), this could be a **compound treatment** problem. I'll flag this again in assumptions — for now I'll treat `scholarship` as your treatment indicator.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one in the context of your study. This is the most important part — if any of these fail, the estimate is biased regardless of how clean the code is.

---

### Assumption 1: No Manipulation of the Running Variable ⚠️ *Must test*

**What it means for you**: Can students see their score and retake the exam until they clear 70? Do teachers "round up" borderline students? If students can precisely control their score to land just above 70, then students at 71 aren't comparable to students at 69 — the 71s are motivated enough to game the system.

**Why it matters**: RDD works because being just above vs. just below 70 is essentially random. Manipulation breaks that randomness.

**Test**: McCrary density test — checks for an unusual *pile-up* of observations just above 70.

Does the exam allow retakes, or do teachers have any discretion over scores?

---

### Assumption 2: Continuity of Potential Outcomes ⚠️ *Partially testable*

**What it means for you**: In a world with no honors program, GPA would change *smoothly* as test score crosses 70. No other factor causes a jump in outcomes at exactly that threshold.

**Why it matters**: If GPA jumps at 70 for ANY reason other than the honors program, your estimate is picking up that other reason too.

**Test**: Run the RDD on pre-determined covariates — variables measured *before* treatment, like demographic characteristics. They should be smooth through the cutoff.

Do you have any pre-treatment covariates in the data (e.g., gender, prior GPA, socioeconomic status)?

---

### Assumption 3: No Compound Treatments ❓ *Requires domain knowledge*

**What it means for you**: Is honors program admission the *only* thing that changes at a test score of 70? For example, does the same 70-point cutoff also trigger: a scholarship, a different course track, access to tutoring, a different dormitory?

**Why it matters**: If multiple things change at 70, the RDD estimate captures all of them combined — you can't isolate the honors program effect.

This is where your column naming concern matters most. **Does the `scholarship` column represent *only* honors program admission, or does crossing 70 trigger other benefits too?**

---

### Assumption 4: Local Identification ℹ️ *Interpretation caveat, not a threat*

**What it means for you**: The RDD estimates the effect of the honors program **only for students who score near 70** — the marginal admits. Students scoring 95 might benefit very differently than students scoring 71.

**Why it matters**: Don't generalize "the honors program raises GPA by X" to all students. It's "the honors program raises GPA by X *for students on the margin of admission*."

This is inherent to the design, not something you can fix. Just be transparent about it.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Testable? | Status |
|---|---|---|
| No manipulation | ✅ Yes — density test | ⏳ Run test |
| Continuity of potential outcomes | 〜 Partially — covariate checks | ⏳ Needs covariates |
| No compound treatments | ❌ Domain knowledge only | ❓ Clarify `scholarship` column |
| Local identification | ❌ Inherent to RDD | ℹ️ Interpretation caveat |

Nothing fatal yet — let's run the analysis. If the density test shows bunching at 70, we'll revisit.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the complete analysis. I've annotated every block so you understand what it's doing and why.

```r
# ============================================================
# RDD Analysis: Effect of Honors Program on GPA
# Running variable: test_score | Cutoff: 70 | Outcome: gpa
# ============================================================

library(rdrobust)    # Main RDD estimation and plots
library(rddensity)   # McCrary density test for manipulation
library(ggplot2)     # Histogram for visual manipulation check
library(tidyverse)   # Data manipulation

# Load your data
# df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")

# Define key parameters
cutoff <- 70
Y <- df$gpa
X <- df$test_score

# ---- STEP 1: Visual Inspection --------------------------------
# Always look at the data first. rdplot bins observations on each
# side and fits a polynomial. A visible jump at the cutoff is the
# signal we're trying to estimate.

rdplot(
  y     = Y,
  x     = X,
  c     = cutoff,
  title   = "RD Plot: Honors Program Effect on GPA",
  x.label = "Entrance Exam Score",
  y.label = "Final GPA"
)
# What to look for: a visible jump at x = 70.
# If the lines flow smoothly through 70, there may be no effect.


# ---- STEP 2: McCrary Density Test ----------------------------
# Checks for manipulation: is there a suspicious pile-up of
# students just above 70? If p < 0.05, we have a manipulation
# problem and should investigate before trusting the estimate.

density_test <- rddensity(X = X, c = cutoff)
summary(density_test)

# Visual version of the density test
rdplotdensity(
  rdd    = density_test,
  X      = X,
  title  = "Density Test: Exam Score Distribution",
  xlabel = "Entrance Exam Score",
  ylabel = "Density"
)

# Histogram (intuitive manipulation check)
ggplot(df, aes(x = test_score)) +
  geom_histogram(binwidth = 2, fill = "steelblue", color = "white") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = cutoff, color = "red",
             linetype = "dashed", linewidth = 1) +
  labs(
    title = "Distribution of Entrance Exam Scores",
    subtitle = "Red dashed line = cutoff (70). Look for unusual bunching just above 70.",
    x = "Entrance Exam Score",
    y = "Number of Students"
  ) +
  theme_minimal()


# ---- STEP 3: Main RD Estimate --------------------------------
# rdrobust does three things automatically:
#   1. Selects the optimal bandwidth (how far from 70 to include)
#   2. Fits a local linear polynomial on each side
#   3. Computes bias-corrected, robust confidence intervals (MSE-optimal)
#
# Use `summary()` to read the results — the key row is "Robust"
# which gives the bias-corrected estimate and confidence interval.

rd_main <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff)
summary(rd_main)

# Reading the output:
#   Coef (Robust)  = your estimated effect
#   95% CI (Robust) = confidence interval
#   BW est. (h)    = bandwidth used (e.g., ±8 points around 70)
#   N (h)          = number of students within the bandwidth


# ---- STEP 4: How Much Data Are Near the Cutoff? ---------------
# Good practice: report how many students are actually driving
# the estimate (within the optimal bandwidth).

opt_bw <- rd_main$bws["h", "left"]  # optimal bandwidth
n_in_bw <- sum(abs(X - cutoff) <= opt_bw)

cat("Optimal bandwidth:", round(opt_bw, 2), "\n")
cat("Students within bandwidth:", n_in_bw,
    "(", round(100 * n_in_bw / nrow(df), 1), "% of sample)\n")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

Three checks I'd recommend for a first RDD — run at least the bandwidth sensitivity.

```r
# ---- ROBUSTNESS 1: Bandwidth Sensitivity ---------------------
# If the estimate depends heavily on which bandwidth you choose,
# that's a red flag — it suggests the local linear fit isn't
# stable. Results should be qualitatively similar across widths.

opt_h <- rd_main$bws["h", "left"]

bandwidths <- c(opt_h * 0.50,
                opt_h * 0.75,
                opt_h * 1.00,   # baseline
                opt_h * 1.25,
                opt_h * 1.50)

bw_results <- map_dfr(bandwidths, function(bw) {
  fit <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff, h = bw)
  tibble(
    bandwidth  = round(bw, 2),
    estimate   = round(fit$coef["Bias-Corrected", 1], 4),
    ci_lower   = round(fit$ci["Robust", 1], 4),
    ci_upper   = round(fit$ci["Robust", 2], 4),
    n_used     = fit$N_h[1] + fit$N_h[2]
  )
})

print(bw_results)
# Good sign: estimates stay in the same ballpark across bandwidths.
# Red flag: estimate flips sign or doubles when bandwidth changes.


# ---- ROBUSTNESS 2: Placebo Cutoffs ---------------------------
# Run the RDD at cutoffs where NO treatment change occurs
# (e.g., 60 and 80). Finding a "significant effect" at a placebo
# cutoff suggests your model is misfitting the data, not detecting
# a real treatment effect.

placebo_cutoffs <- c(55, 60, 65, 80, 85)

placebo_results <- map_dfr(placebo_cutoffs, function(c0) {
  # Exclude actual treatment region to avoid contamination
  df_placebo <- df |> filter(test_score < 70)  # left side only, or adjust

  fit <- tryCatch(
    rdrobust(y = df_placebo$gpa, x = df_placebo$test_score, c = c0),
    error = function(e) NULL
  )

  if (is.null(fit)) return(tibble(cutoff = c0, estimate = NA,
                                   ci_lower = NA, ci_upper = NA))

  tibble(
    cutoff   = c0,
    estimate = round(fit$coef["Bias-Corrected", 1], 4),
    ci_lower = round(fit$ci["Robust", 1], 4),
    ci_upper = round(fit$ci["Robust", 2], 4)
  )
})

print(placebo_results)
# Good sign: no statistically significant effects at placebo cutoffs.


# ---- ROBUSTNESS 3: Donut Hole Test ---------------------------
# Exclude students very close to the cutoff (±1 and ±2 points).
# If your estimate only holds because of the students right at 70
# (who are most likely to be manipulators), it won't survive this.

donut_results <- map_dfr(c(1, 2, 3), function(donut) {
  df_donut <- df |> filter(abs(test_score - cutoff) > donut)
  fit <- rdrobust(y = df_donut$gpa, x = df_donut$test_score, c = cutoff)

  tibble(
    donut_radius = donut,
    estimate     = round(fit$coef["Bias-Corrected", 1], 4),
    ci_lower     = round(fit$ci["Robust", 1], 4),
    ci_upper     = round(fit$ci["Robust", 2], 4),
    n_excluded   = nrow(df) - nrow(df_donut)
  )
})

print(donut_results)
# Good sign: estimate survives after excluding the most-at-risk
# manipulation zone.
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you've run the analysis, here's how to report it. Fill in the bracketed values from your `summary(rd_main)` output:

> "Using a sharp regression discontinuity design, I estimate the effect of honors program admission on final GPA for students near the 70-point admissions cutoff. Students just above the cutoff had an average GPA [X] points higher than students just below (bias-corrected estimate: [coef]; 95% robust CI: [lower, upper]; p = [p-value]).
>
> The optimal bandwidth was [h] points, including [N] students within [cutoff ± h]. The McCrary density test showed [no evidence of / evidence of] manipulation at the cutoff (p = [density p-value]).
>
> **This estimate is local**: it applies to students who scored near 70 on the entrance exam — the marginal admits — and may not reflect the program's effect on high- or low-scoring students."

---

## Quick-Reference: Reading `summary(rd_main)`

When you run `summary(rd_main)`, look for this section:

```
=============================================================================
        Method     Coef. Std. Err.         z     P>|z|      [ 95% C.I. ]
=============================================================================
  Conventional  [coef]    [se]        [z]    [p]    [lo]     [hi]
        Robust      -         -           -       -   [lo]     [hi]   ← USE THIS
=============================================================================
```

**Always report the Robust row** — it uses bias-corrected inference that's valid for the MSE-optimal bandwidth. The Conventional row undercovers.

## Stage 1: Design Confirmed

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Running variable** | Employee count (continuous) |
| **Cutoff** | 50 employees |
| **Treatment** | Subsidy receipt |
| **Outcome** | Firm revenue |

| **Sample** | ~10,000 firms |
| **Language** | Python |

The cutoff is **< 50**, so firms *below* the cutoff are treated (left-side treatment). This is the reverse of the typical convention — we'll handle this in the code by either flipping the sign or using `rdrobust`'s `c` parameter carefully.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one in the context of your specific study.

---

### 1. ⚠️ No Manipulation of the Running Variable

**What it means here:** Can firms precisely control their reported employee count to stay just below 50? If so, the firms at 48–49 employees are systematically different from firms at 51–52 — they're the ones willing and able to manipulate headcount (e.g., reclassifying workers as contractors, delaying hiring). That breaks the "as-if random" assignment near the cutoff.

**You already suspect this — that's the right instinct.** This is the most serious threat in your design.

**It's testable** with a McCrary density test. We expect a smooth distribution of firms across employee counts. If there's a suspicious spike just *below* 50, that's evidence of bunching.

**Status:** 🔴 High concern — test immediately.

---

### 2. ✅ Continuity of Potential Outcomes

**What it means here:** In the absence of the subsidy, would revenue have been smooth as you cross 50 employees? The worry is that some *other* policy, tax rule, or regulation also changes at 50 employees — making the revenue jump at the cutoff due to something other than the subsidy.

**Ask yourself:** Are there other regulations in your country that use the 50-employee threshold? (Labor law thresholds, tax brackets, reporting requirements?) This is a compound treatment risk.

**Status:** 🟡 Moderate concern — depends on regulatory context.

---

### 3. ⚠️ No Compound Treatments at the Cutoff

**What it means here:** Is the subsidy the *only* thing that changes at 50 employees? If firms below 50 also face lower payroll taxes, different labor regulations, or other benefits at that exact threshold, your estimate will capture the combined effect of everything — not just the subsidy.

**This is common in firm-size RDDs.** The 50-employee threshold is used in many countries for employment law (e.g., works council requirements in Germany, FMLA in the US).

**Status:** 🟡 Moderate concern — requires domain knowledge.

---

### 4. ✅ Local Identification

**What it means here:** Your estimate will only apply to firms *near* 50 employees. The subsidy effect for a 10-person startup may be totally different from the effect for a 48-person firm. Decision-makers should not extrapolate this result to all firm sizes.

**Status:** ✅ Not a validity threat, but important for interpretation.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Testable? |
|---|---|---|
| No manipulation of employee count | 🔴 High concern | ✅ Density test |
| Continuity of potential outcomes | 🟡 Moderate | ✅ Covariate smoothness |
| No compound treatments at cutoff | 🟡 Moderate | Partially (domain knowledge) |
| Local identification | ✅ Known limitation | N/A |

### sc_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

No existing plan. I have the template. Let me walk you through all five stages — this is actually a near-perfect synthetic control setup, so let's get it right from the start.

---

## Stage 1 — Setup ✓

Your study maps cleanly onto the classic synthetic control design:

| Element | Your study |
|---|---|
| Treated unit | California |
| Donor pool | The other 49 states |
| Pre-treatment periods | Q1 1990 – Q4 1997 (~32 quarters) |
| Post-treatment periods | Q1 1998 – Q4 2005 (~32 quarters) |
| Outcome | Restaurant employment |
| Method | **Classic Synthetic Control** (Abadie, Diamond & Hainmueller) |

**Why SC fits here perfectly**: SC was literally designed for this scenario — one unit experiences a policy change, and you want to know what would have happened without it. The abundant pre-treatment window (32 quarters) gives the optimization algorithm plenty of data to construct a good synthetic California. With 49 donor states, you have a rich pool to draw from.

**Why not DiD?** DiD would require you to argue parallel trends for all states. SC is more honest — it explicitly shows you *whether* a weighted combination of states can track California, and if it can't, you know the counterfactual is weak.

One question before we proceed: **what predictor variables do you have alongside employment?** For example: state GDP, population, income per capita, industry mix. SC works by matching both on pre-treatment outcome trajectories *and* on covariate predictors. If you only have employment, that's workable — we'll just use lagged employment averages as predictors — but additional economic covariates improve the match.

---

## Stage 2 — Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption for your specific case. For each one, tell me if it seems plausible.

---

### 1. Pre-Treatment Fit Quality 🔍 *Testable*

**What it means**: A weighted combination of other states should be able to reproduce California's restaurant employment trajectory from 1990–1997. If it can't — if the synthetic California diverges from real California even before 1998 — the post-1998 gap can't be trusted as a treatment effect.

**Why it matters**: The entire logic of SC is "the synthetic control tracked the treated unit perfectly before the treatment, therefore any post-treatment divergence is the treatment's effect." Break the premise, break the conclusion.

**How we'll check**: After fitting, we compute the pre-treatment RMSPE (root mean squared prediction error). We want it small relative to the scale of employment. We'll also plot treated vs. synthetic — you should see the lines nearly overlapping from 1990–1997.

**Your situation**: California is the largest state by employment, which could make matching harder. But you have 49 donors and 32 pre-treatment quarters — that's actually a lot of fitting power. *Does California's restaurant employment trend look broadly similar to other large states (Texas, New York, Florida) in your data?*

---

### 2. Convex Hull ⚠️ *Partially Testable*

**What it means**: The weights the algorithm finds must be non-negative and sum to 1 (a convex combination). This only works if California's pre-treatment characteristics lie *within the range* of what the donor pool collectively spans. If California is too extreme — say, its employment growth is consistently higher than every single donor state — no weighted average of donors can match it, and the algorithm will return a poor fit.

**Why it matters**: If the convex hull is violated, the synthetic control is extrapolating, and the pre-treatment fit will be poor. This is actually a common problem with California because it's atypically large. The "tell" is concentrated weights (e.g., the algorithm puts weight 0.7 on Texas and 0.3 on New York, giving up on a real match).

**Mitigation if violated**: We'd upgrade to **augmented synthetic control** (`augsynth`), which adds a ridge-regularized bias correction to handle extrapolation. Think of it as SC + a small outcome model that corrects for the part SC can't match.

*Does California look like an outlier on restaurant employment compared to the rest of your donor pool — e.g., consistently above or below all 49 states?*

---

### 3. No Interference Between Units ⚡ *Must Be Argued*

**What it means**: California's ban shouldn't affect restaurant employment in other states. If California's ban caused businesses to relocate to Nevada, or triggered copycat bans in neighboring states, the donor outcomes would be contaminated — you'd be comparing California against a "synthetic California" that was itself affected by the treatment.

**Why it matters**: Contaminated donors make the counterfactual wrong. If Nevada's restaurant employment rose because of business relocation *from California*, then Nevada is a bad donor — using it inflates the synthetic control's level and makes California look worse than it actually was.

**Your situation**: This is the most substantive assumption to argue. A few things to think about:
- Did any large neighboring states (Nevada, Arizona, Oregon) pass similar bans around 1998? If so, drop them.
- Was there a documented business migration effect from California after 1998?
- The restaurant industry is generally local (you can't move a restaurant to Nevada to serve California customers), which actually makes this assumption *more* plausible than for many goods-producing industries.

*Do you know offhand which other states had smoking bans in restaurants around this period?*

---

### 4. Donor Pool Composition 🧹 *Argue + Inspect*

**What it means**: Every state in your donor pool should be a plausible "untreated California" — meaning no major idiosyncratic shocks of their own during 1998–2005. If Texas had a major oil boom that supercharged its restaurant sector, Texas would make a poor donor: the synthetic control's post-1998 rise would partly reflect the Texas boom, not California's counterfactual.

**Standard practice**: Remove states that:
- Passed their own restaurant smoking bans during the study period
- Had major state-specific economic shocks (e.g., a state hurricane, a large industry collapse)

*Are there any states you already know you'd want to exclude from the donor pool?*

---

### 5. No Anticipation 📅 *Testable via Pre-Trends*

**What it means**: California restaurants shouldn't have started changing employment behavior *before* the ban actually took effect in January 1998 — for example, if they started pre-emptively cutting staff in Q3 1997 after the law was signed.

**Why it matters**: If anticipation exists, the true "pre-treatment" period is shorter than you think, and the pre-1998 fit will falsely look better than it should.

**How we'll check**: The pre-treatment trends plot will show this. If a gap opens in 1997 Q2–Q4 before the treatment, that's a sign of anticipation.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Pre-treatment fit | 🔍 Test after fitting | Large state — watch RMSPE |
| Convex hull | 🔍 Test after fitting | Upgrade to `augsynth` if violated |
| No interference | ❓ Needs your input | Restaurant = local industry (favorable) |
| Donor pool clean | ❓ Needs your input | Remove states with own bans |
| No anticipation | 🔍 Test after fitting | Check late 1997 trends |

Let me know your answers to the questions above, and I'll flag any fatal concerns. In the meantime, here's the full implementation code — we can refine the donor pool once you've confirmed which states to exclude.

---

## Stage 3 — Implementation

> **Note**: Using the `Synth` package (canonical Abadie et al. implementation). I'm also including `tidysynth` for the inference and plotting steps where it's cleaner, since those don't affect the core estimation.

```r
# ── 0. Install & load ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# install.packages(c("Synth", "tidysynth", "tidyverse"))
library(Synth)
library(tidyverse)

# ── 1. Data prep ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Your panel should look like:
#   state       (character or factor — state name)
#   state_id    (integer — needed by Synth)
#   quarter     (integer, e.g. 1 = Q1 1990, ..., 64 = Q4 2005)
#   emp         (restaurant employment, your outcome)
#   + any additional covariates (gdp_pc, pop, income, etc.)

# Verify the panel is balanced
df %>%
  count(state_id) %>%
  summarise(n_units = n(), min_t = min(n), max_t = max(n))

# Treatment timing: Q1 1998 = period 33 (if Q1 1990 = 1)
# California state_id: assign a numeric ID; let's say California = 5
# (use whatever ID California has in your data)

california_id <- 5          # ← replace with your California numeric ID
treatment_quarter <- 33     # ← Q1 1998; adjust if your indexing differs
pre_start  <- 1             # Q1 1990
pre_end    <- 32            # Q4 1997
post_end   <- 64            # Q4 2005

# Donor pool: all state IDs except California
# Remove states that had their own bans ~1998 (e.g., Utah, Vermont — confirm)
exclude_states <- c(california_id)   # ← add IDs of states to exclude
donor_ids <- setdiff(unique(df$state_id), exclude_states)

# ── 2. Visualize pre-treatment trends before fitting ─────────────────────────
df %>%
  mutate(group = if_else(state_id == california_id, "California", "Donor states")) %>%
  group_by(quarter, group) %>%
  summarise(mean_emp = mean(emp, na.rm = TRUE), .groups = "drop") %>%
  ggplot(aes(x = quarter, y = mean_emp, color = group)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = treatment_quarter, linetype = "dashed", color = "red") +
  labs(title = "Restaurant Employment: California vs. Donor States",
       x = "Quarter (1 = Q1 1990)", y = "Mean Employment",
       color = NULL) +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 3. dataprep() — define the optimization problem ──────────────────────────
dataprep_out <- dataprep(
  foo = as.data.frame(df),

  # Predictors: pre-treatment outcome averages + any covariates you have
  # Add your covariates here, e.g. c("emp", "gdp_pc", "pop")
  predictors        = c("emp"),
  predictors.op     = "mean",

  # Special predictors: specific lagged outcome values
  # These help the algorithm match the shape of the pre-treatment trajectory,
  # not just the average level
  special.predictors = list(
    list("emp", pre_start,              "mean"),   # early pre-period avg
    list("emp", pre_start:(pre_end/2),  "mean"),   # mid pre-period avg
    list("emp", (pre_end - 3):pre_end,  "mean")    # late pre-period avg
  ),

  dependent         = "emp",
  unit.variable     = "state_id",
  time.variable     = "quarter",
  treatment.identifier  = california_id,
  controls.identifier   = donor_ids,
  time.predictors.prior = pre_start:pre_end,
  time.optimize.ssr     = pre_start:pre_end,   # minimize pre-treatment RMSPE
  time.plot             = pre_start:post_end
)

# ── 4. synth() — find optimal donor weights ──────────────────────────────────
synth_out <- synth(dataprep_out)

# ── 5. Results table ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Balance table: how well does synthetic California match real California
# on the predictors?
synth_tables <- synth.tab(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out
)
print(synth_tables$tab.pred)   # predictor balance
print(synth_tables$tab.w)      # donor weights (non-zero ones matter)

# Pre-treatment RMSPE (rule of thumb: should be < ~10% of outcome SD)
pre_rmspe <- sqrt(synth_out$loss.v)
cat("Pre-treatment RMSPE:", round(pre_rmspe, 2), "\n")
cat("Outcome SD (pre-period):",
    round(sd(df$emp[df$state_id == california_id &
                    df$quarter  <= pre_end]), 2), "\n")

# ── 6. Plots ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Treated vs synthetic trajectory
path.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Restaurant Employment",
  Xlab         = "Quarter (1 = Q1 1990)",
  Ylim         = NULL,
  Legend       = c("California", "Synthetic California"),
  Legend.position = "bottomright",
  abline.v     = treatment_quarter
)

# Gap plot: treatment effect period by period
gaps.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Gap (California - Synthetic)",
  Xlab         = "Quarter (1 = Q1 1990)",
  Ylim         = NULL,
  abline.v     = treatment_quarter
)
abline(h = 0, lty = 2)

# ── 7. Extract post-treatment ATT ────────────────────────────────────────────
gaps <- dataprep_out$Y1plot - (dataprep_out$Y0plot %*% synth_out$solution.w)

att_avg   <- mean(gaps[rownames(gaps) >= treatment_quarter, ])
att_cumul <- sum( gaps[rownames(gaps) >= treatment_quarter, ])

cat("Average per-quarter effect:", round(att_avg, 2), "\n")
cat("Cumulative effect (Q1 1998 – Q4 2005):", round(att_cumul, 2), "\n")
```

---

## Stage 4 — Falsification

Two tests, in order of importance:

### Test 1 — In-Space Placebo (Permutation Inference)

Apply the exact same SC procedure to every donor state as if *it* were the treated unit in 1998. This gives you a distribution of "effects" for states that weren't actually treated. If California's gap is extreme relative to that distribution, the effect is unlikely to be noise.

```r
# ── In-space placebo loop ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
placebo_gaps <- matrix(NA,
                       nrow = length(pre_start:post_end),
                       ncol = length(donor_ids),
                       dimnames = list(pre_start:post_end, donor_ids))

for (placebo_id in donor_ids) {

  # Donor pool for this placebo: everyone except the placebo unit
  placebo_donors <- setdiff(donor_ids, placebo_id)

  tryCatch({
    dp_placebo <- dataprep(
      foo = as.data.frame(df),
      predictors            = c("emp"),
      predictors.op         = "mean",
      special.predictors    = list(
        list("emp", pre_start,             "mean"),
        list("emp", pre_start:(pre_end/2), "mean"),
        list("emp", (pre_end - 3):pre_end, "mean")
      ),
      dependent             = "emp",
      unit.variable         = "state_id",
      time.variable         = "quarter",
      treatment.identifier  = placebo_id,
      controls.identifier   = placebo_donors,
      time.predictors.prior = pre_start:pre_end,
      time.optimize.ssr     = pre_start:pre_end,
      time.plot             = pre_start:post_end
    )

    syn_placebo <- synth(dp_placebo)
    placebo_gaps[, as.character(placebo_id)] <-
      dp_placebo$Y1plot - (dp_placebo$Y0plot %*% syn_placebo$solution.w)

  }, error = function(e) {
    message("Skipping state ", placebo_id, ": ", e$message)
  })
}

# ── Compute RMSPE ratios for inference ────────────────────────────────────────
compute_ratio <- function(gap_vec) {
  pre_rmspe_  <- sqrt(mean(gap_vec[1:pre_end]^2,  na.rm = TRUE))
  post_rmspe_ <- sqrt(mean(gap_vec[(pre_end+1):length(gap_vec)]^2, na.rm = TRUE))
  post_rmspe_ / pre_rmspe_
}

ca_ratio      <- compute_ratio(gaps[,1])
placebo_ratios <- apply(placebo_gaps, 2, compute_ratio)

# Pseudo p-value: fraction of placebos with ratio >= California's ratio
p_value <- mean(c(placebo_ratios, ca_ratio) >= ca_ratio, na.rm = TRUE)
cat("California post/pre RMSPE ratio:", round(ca_ratio, 2), "\n")
cat("Pseudo p-value:", round(p_value, 3), "\n")

# ── Plot: California gap vs. placebo distribution ─────────────────────────────
quarters <- pre_start:post_end

# Filter out placebos with very poor pre-treatment fit
# (standard practice: drop placebos with pre-RMSPE > 2x California's)
pre_rmspe_placebos <- apply(placebo_gaps[1:pre_end, ], 2,
                             function(x) sqrt(mean(x^2, na.rm = TRUE)))
keep <- pre_rmspe_placebos <= 2 * pre_rmspe

plot(quarters, gaps, type = "l", lwd = 3, col = "black",
     ylim = range(c(gaps, placebo_gaps[, keep]), na.rm = TRUE),
     xlab = "Quarter (1 = Q1 1990)",
     ylab = "Gap (Treated - Synthetic)",
     main = "In-Space Placebo Test")
for (j in which(keep)) {
  lines(quarters, placebo_gaps[, j], col = "gray80", lwd = 0.8)
}
lines(quarters, gaps, lwd = 3, col = "black")
abline(v = treatment_quarter, lty = 2, col = "red")
abline(h = 0, lty = 3)
legend("topleft", legend = c("California", "Placebos"),
       col = c("black", "gray70"), lwd = c(3, 1))
```

**What to look for**: California's black line should be an *outlier* in the post-1998 distribution. If it sits comfortably in the middle of the gray placebo lines, the evidence for an effect is weak.

### Test 2 — Leave-One-Out Donor Analysis

Re-estimate the synthetic control dropping one high-weight donor at a time. If your results hinge entirely on one state, that's fragile.

```r
# ── Leave-one-out: drop each high-weight donor in turn ───────────────────────
top_donors <- synth_tables$tab.w %>%
  filter(w.weights > 0.05) %>%    # focus on donors with >5% weight
  pull(unit.numbers)

loo_gaps <- list()

for (drop_id in top_donors) {

  loo_donors <- setdiff(donor_ids, drop_id)

  dp_loo <- dataprep(
    foo = as.data.frame(df),
    predictors            = c("emp"),
    predictors.op         = "mean",
    special.predictors    = list(
      list("emp", pre_start,             "mean"),
      list("emp", pre_start:(pre_end/2), "mean"),
      list("emp", (pre_end - 3):pre_end, "mean")
    ),
    dependent             = "emp",
    unit.variable         = "state_id",
    time.variable         = "quarter",
    treatment.identifier  = california_id,
    controls.identifier   = loo_donors,
    time.predictors.prior = pre_start:pre_end,
    time.optimize.ssr     = pre_start:pre_end,
    time.plot             = pre_start:post_end
  )

  syn_loo <- synth(dp_loo)
  loo_gaps[[as.character(drop_id)]] <-
    dp_loo$Y1plot - (dp_loo$Y0plot %*% syn_loo$solution.w)
}

# Plot all LOO gaps vs. baseline
plot(quarters, gaps, type = "l", lwd = 3, col = "black",
     ylim = range(c(gaps, do.call(cbind, loo_gaps)), na.rm = TRUE),
     xlab = "Quarter", ylab = "Gap",
     main = "Leave-One-Out Robustness")
for (g in loo_gaps) lines(quarters, g, col = "steelblue", lwd = 1, lty = 2)
lines(quarters, gaps, lwd = 3, col = "black")
abline(v = treatment_quarter, lty = 2, col = "red")
abline(h = 0, lty = 3)
legend("topleft", legend = c("Baseline SC", "Leave-one-out"),
       col = c("black", "steelblue"), lwd = c(3, 1), lty = c(1, 2))
```

## Stage 5 — Interpretation Template

Once you've run the code, here's the narrative structure to fill in:

> *"We estimate the effect of California's 1998 restaurant smoking ban on quarterly restaurant employment using a synthetic control constructed from a weighted combination of other U.S. states.*
>
> *The synthetic California closely matched real California in the pre-treatment period (RMSPE = **[X]**, or **[X%]** of the pre-treatment standard deviation), lending credibility to the counterfactual.*
>
> *The primary donors to the synthetic control were **[State A]** (weight **[W]**), **[State B]** (weight **[W]**), and **[State C]** (weight **[W]**).*
>
> *The average quarterly effect is estimated at **[ATT_avg]** jobs, and the cumulative effect over the 1998–2005 post-period is **[ATT_cumul]** jobs.*
>
> *Permutation inference assigns California a pseudo p-value of **[p]**, meaning only **[p×49]** of the 49 placebo states had a post/pre RMSPE ratio as large as California's.*
>
> **Caveats**: Pre-treatment fit was [good/moderate]. The donor pool excluded [list states] which had their own bans. Effects are specific to California and may not generalize to other states or time periods.*"*

## What to Do Next

Run the code and report back with:
1. **The pre-treatment RMSPE** and outcome SD — this tells us if the fit is good enough to trust
2. **Which states get the highest weights** — tells us if California is in the convex hull
3. **Whether the gap plot shows movement before 1998** — rules out anticipation

If the pre-treatment fit is poor (RMSPE > ~10% of SD), we upgrade to `augsynth`. Otherwise, your synthetic California is ready for interpretation.
