### sc_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmation

You have a clean synthetic control setup — **one treated unit, 15 donors, 20 annual periods (10/10 split)**. Before I generate code, one flag needs to be addressed head-on.

> **⚠️ The volatile pre-treatment trajectory is a serious concern.** If the treated country's GDP growth fluctuated more than any donor country, it likely sits *outside the convex hull* of the donor pool — meaning no weighted combination of donors can faithfully reproduce its pre-treatment trajectory. Standard SCM will produce poor pre-treatment fit, and a poor-fitting synthetic control produces an unreliable counterfactual. I'll build in diagnostics to confirm this and address it directly.

**Determined variant**: `scpi` (Python) for estimation + prediction intervals, with explicit convex hull diagnostics.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

### 1. 🔴 Pre-Treatment Fit (High Risk — flagged by your data)

**Why it matters**: If the synthetic control can't reproduce the treated country's GDP growth during the 10 pre-treatment years, there's no reason to trust its projection of what would have happened after the carbon tax. The post-treatment gap would conflate "treatment effect" with "model can't match this country."

**Your situation**: Unusually volatile pre-treatment growth makes this hard. With only 10 pre-treatment periods and a volatile outcome, the optimizer has limited data to work with.

**We will test**: Pre-treatment RMSPE and a visual fit plot.

---

### 2. 🔴 Convex Hull (High Risk — flagged by your data)

**Why it matters**: Standard SCM assigns weights between 0 and 1 that sum to 1 (a *convex combination*). If your treated country's volatility is genuinely outside the range of any donor, no convex combination can match it — the method will concentrate weight on 1–2 "closest" donors and still fit poorly.

**Your situation**: "Different from all donors" is essentially a direct description of a convex hull violation.

**We will test**: Check whether the treated unit's outcome range (min/max) falls within the donor pool's range, and inspect weight concentration after fitting.

---

### 3. 🟡 No Interference (Moderate — must argue substantively)

**Why it matters**: If the carbon tax in the treated country caused other countries to import more carbon-intensive goods (carbon leakage), or to accelerate their own climate policies in response, those donor outcomes are contaminated. The synthetic control would be built from units that were themselves affected by the treatment.

**Your situation**: Carbon leakage is a well-documented phenomenon. Countries with strong trade ties to the treated country are higher-risk donors.

**Question for you**: Are any of the 15 donor countries major trading partners of the treated country? If so, consider excluding them.

---

### 4. 🟡 Donor Pool Composition

**Why it matters**: Including donors that experienced their own major shocks (financial crises, commodity price collapses, other carbon policies) during the study period contaminates the synthetic control. The optimizer may match on a structural break, not a real similarity.

**Question**: Did any of the 15 donor countries implement their own carbon tax, major austerity programs, or experience a commodity shock during the 20-year window?

---

### 5. 🟢 No Anticipation

**Why it matters**: If firms or households in the treated country changed behavior in anticipation of the carbon tax *before* it officially started, the "pre-treatment" period is already contaminated.

**Testable**: Look for a trend break in the treated unit's outcome in the last 1–2 pre-treatment periods.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Pre-treatment fit | 🔴 High risk | Measure RMSPE; run augmented SC if poor |
| Convex hull | 🔴 High risk | Check range; weight concentration diagnostic |
| No interference | 🟡 Moderate | Exclude major trading partners |
| Donor pool composition | 🟡 Moderate | Exclude countries with own carbon policies |
| No anticipation | 🟢 Low risk | Visual check on last pre-treatment periods |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from scpi_pkg.scdata import scdata
from scpi_pkg.scest import scest
from scpi_pkg.scpi import scpi
from scpi_pkg.scplot import scplot

# ── 0. Parameters ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
TREATED_UNIT  = "A"
TREATMENT_PERIOD = 11          # first post-treatment period
PRE_PERIODS   = list(range(1, 11))   # periods 1–10
POST_PERIODS  = list(range(11, 21))  # periods 11–20

# ── 1. Load data ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")   ← swap in your path
df["state"] = df["state"].astype(str)

donor_list = sorted(df.loc[df["treated"] == 0, "state"].unique().tolist())
print(f"Treated unit : {TREATED_UNIT}")
print(f"Donor pool   : {len(donor_list)} units → {donor_list}")

# ── 2. Convex hull diagnostic (before fitting) ─────────────────────────────────
treated_pre = (
    df.query("state == @TREATED_UNIT and period < @TREATMENT_PERIOD")["outcome"]
)
donors_pre = df.query("state in @donor_list and period < @TREATMENT_PERIOD")["outcome"]

print("\n── Convex Hull Check (pre-treatment outcome range) ──")
print(f"Treated  | min={treated_pre.min():.2f}  max={treated_pre.max():.2f}  "
      f"std={treated_pre.std():.2f}")
print(f"Donors   | min={donors_pre.min():.2f}  max={donors_pre.max():.2f}  "
      f"std={donors_pre.std():.2f}")

outside_hull = (
    treated_pre.min() < donors_pre.min() or
    treated_pre.max() > donors_pre.max()
)
if outside_hull:
    print("⚠️  Treated unit falls OUTSIDE donor outcome range — convex hull likely violated.")
    print("   Standard SCM may extrapolate. Inspect pre-treatment fit carefully.")
else:
    print("✓  Treated unit within donor outcome range.")

# ── 3. Pre-treatment trajectory plot ──────────────────────────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(11, 5))
for unit in donor_list:
    unit_pre = df.query("state == @unit and period < @TREATMENT_PERIOD")
    ax.plot(unit_pre["period"], unit_pre["outcome"],
            color="steelblue", alpha=0.25, linewidth=1)

treated_ts = df.query("state == @TREATED_UNIT")
ax.plot(treated_ts["period"], treated_ts["outcome"],
        color="black", linewidth=2.5, label="Treated country")
ax.axvline(TREATMENT_PERIOD - 0.5, color="red", linestyle="--", label="Carbon tax")
ax.set_title("Pre-treatment GDP growth: treated vs. donor pool")
ax.set_xlabel("Period")
ax.set_ylabel("GDP growth (outcome)")
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("pretreament_trajectories.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
# ▶ If the black line is clearly outside the blue band, the convex hull is violated.

# ── 4. Fit synthetic control ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
scd = scdata(
    df=df,
    id_var="state",
    time_var="period",
    outcome_var="outcome",
    period_pre=PRE_PERIODS,
    period_post=POST_PERIODS,
    unit_tr=TREATED_UNIT,
    unit_co=donor_list
)

# Point estimate (simplex weights = standard SCM)
sc_est = scest(scd, w_constr={"name": "simplex"})
print("\n── Synthetic Control Estimates ──")
print(sc_est)

# ── 5. Donor weights ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
weights = pd.Series(sc_est.w.flatten(), index=donor_list, name="weight")
weights = weights[weights > 0.01].sort_values(ascending=False)
print("\n── Donor Weights (> 1%) ──")
print(weights.round(4).to_string())

if weights.iloc[0] > 0.80:
    print("⚠️  Weight concentrated on one donor — synthetic control ≈ single comparison.")
    print("   This often signals a convex hull violation.")

# ── 6. Pre-treatment RMSPE ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fitted_pre   = sc_est.Y_pre_fit.flatten()
actual_pre   = sc_est.Y_pre.flatten()
rmspe_pre    = np.sqrt(np.mean((actual_pre - fitted_pre) ** 2))
outcome_sd   = actual_pre.std()

print(f"\n── Pre-treatment Fit ──")
print(f"RMSPE        : {rmspe_pre:.4f}")
print(f"Outcome SD   : {outcome_sd:.4f}")
print(f"RMSPE / SD   : {rmspe_pre / outcome_sd:.3f}")

if rmspe_pre / outcome_sd > 0.10:
    print("⚠️  Poor pre-treatment fit (RMSPE > 10% of SD).")
    print("   Consider augmented synthetic control (augsynth in R) for bias correction.")
else:
    print("✓  Acceptable pre-treatment fit.")

# ── 7. Estimation with prediction intervals ───────────────────────────────────
sc_pred = scpi(scd, w_constr={"name": "simplex"}, u_order=1, u_lags=0)
print("\n── Treatment Effect Estimates (with prediction intervals) ──")
print(sc_pred)

# ── 8. Main gap plot ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
scplot(sc_pred)  # treated vs synthetic + prediction intervals
# Save manually if needed:
# plt.savefig("sc_gap_plot.png", dpi=150)

# ── 9. Gap series (effect by period) ─────────────────────────────────────────
actual_post  = sc_pred.Y_post.flatten()
synth_post   = sc_pred.Y_post_fit.flatten()
gap          = actual_post - synth_post

print("\n── Post-treatment Gap (Treated − Synthetic) ──")
gap_df = pd.DataFrame({
    "period":    POST_PERIODS,
    "actual":    actual_post.round(3),
    "synthetic": synth_post.round(3),
    "gap":       gap.round(3)
})
print(gap_df.to_string(index=False))
print(f"\nAverage per-period effect : {gap.mean():.4f}")
print(f"Cumulative effect         : {gap.sum():.4f}")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Given the convex hull concern, I recommend **two checks** in priority order:

### Check 1 — In-space placebo (permutation test)

Apply the exact same SCM to each donor unit as if *it* were treated. Compute post/pre RMSPE ratio for each. If the treated country's ratio is in the top tail of this distribution, that's evidence for a real effect.

```python
# ── In-space placebo ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
def rmspe(actual, fitted):
    return np.sqrt(np.mean((actual - fitted) ** 2))

rmspe_ratios = {}

# Treated unit ratio
rmspe_pre_tr  = rmspe(sc_est.Y_pre.flatten(), sc_est.Y_pre_fit.flatten())
rmspe_post_tr = rmspe(sc_pred.Y_post.flatten(), sc_pred.Y_post_fit.flatten())
rmspe_ratios[TREATED_UNIT] = rmspe_post_tr / rmspe_pre_tr

# Placebo: each donor acts as "treated"
for placebo_unit in donor_list:
    placebo_donors = [u for u in donor_list if u != placebo_unit]
    try:
        scd_p = scdata(
            df=df,
            id_var="state",
            time_var="period",
            outcome_var="outcome",
            period_pre=PRE_PERIODS,
            period_post=POST_PERIODS,
            unit_tr=placebo_unit,
            unit_co=placebo_donors
        )
        est_p = scest(scd_p, w_constr={"name": "simplex"})
        pred_p = scpi(scd_p, w_constr={"name": "simplex"}, u_order=1, u_lags=0)
        r_pre  = rmspe(est_p.Y_pre.flatten(),  est_p.Y_pre_fit.flatten())
        r_post = rmspe(pred_p.Y_post.flatten(), pred_p.Y_post_fit.flatten())
        rmspe_ratios[placebo_unit] = r_post / r_pre if r_pre > 0 else np.nan
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"  Skipped {placebo_unit}: {e}")

# Compute pseudo p-value
ratio_df = pd.Series(rmspe_ratios).dropna().sort_values(ascending=False)
rank = (ratio_df > ratio_df[TREATED_UNIT]).sum() + 1
p_val = rank / len(ratio_df)

print(f"\n── In-space Placebo Inference ──")
print(f"Treated unit post/pre RMSPE ratio : {rmspe_ratios[TREATED_UNIT]:.3f}")
print(f"Rank among all units              : {rank} / {len(ratio_df)}")
print(f"Pseudo p-value                    : {p_val:.3f}")

# Plot
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 4))
ax.bar(ratio_df.index, ratio_df.values, color=["crimson" if u == TREATED_UNIT else "steelblue"
                                                 for u in ratio_df.index])
ax.set_xlabel("Unit")
ax.set_ylabel("Post/Pre RMSPE Ratio")
ax.set_title("In-space Placebo: RMSPE Ratios")
plt.xticks(rotation=45)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("placebo_rmspe_ratios.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
```

> **Note**: Standard practice is to *exclude* placebo units whose pre-treatment RMSPE exceeds 2× the treated unit's pre-RMSPE. They're a poor match regardless of treatment, and including them inflates the placebo distribution, making real effects harder to detect.

### Check 2 — Leave-one-out donor analysis

```python
# ── Leave-one-out ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
loo_gaps = {}

for drop_unit in [u for u, w in zip(donor_list, sc_est.w.flatten()) if w > 0.05]:
    loo_donors = [u for u in donor_list if u != drop_unit]
    try:
        scd_loo = scdata(
            df=df, id_var="state", time_var="period", outcome_var="outcome",
            period_pre=PRE_PERIODS, period_post=POST_PERIODS,
            unit_tr=TREATED_UNIT, unit_co=loo_donors
        )
        est_loo  = scest(scd_loo, w_constr={"name": "simplex"})
        post_act = est_loo.Y_post.flatten()
        post_syn = est_loo.Y_post_fit.flatten()  # check attr name in your version
        loo_gaps[f"Drop {drop_unit}"] = (post_act - post_syn).mean()
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"  LOO failed for {drop_unit}: {e}")

loo_gaps["Full model"] = gap.mean()
loo_df = pd.Series(loo_gaps).sort_values()
print("\n── Leave-one-out: Average Post-treatment Gap ──")
print(loo_df.round(4).to_string())
# Large spread = results driven by a single donor → fragile finding
```

If the RMSPE diagnostics confirm poor fit, these results should be treated as directional only. Replication using the augmented synthetic control (`augsynth`, R) with ridge regularization is recommended to assess robustness."

If the RMSPE check comes back **poor** (ratio > 0.10), the honest path forward is to run `augsynth` in R — it adds a ridge-regularized outcome model on top of SCM weights, which is specifically designed for cases like yours where the treated unit doesn't sit neatly within the donor pool. ## Stage 1: Setup

Here's what I have from your description:

| Parameter | Value |
|---|---|
| Pre-treatment observations | **42 months** ✅ (exceeds the 30–50 minimum) |
| Post-treatment observations | 6 months |
| Intervention time | `time = 43` (where `post` flips to 1) |
| Control series | None |
| Data frequency | Monthly |
| Language | Python |

**Method selection**: No control series → **Segmented regression (ITS)** as the primary method, with a **ARIMA counterfactual** as a robustness check. CausalImpact would be the stronger option *if* you can find a control series (e.g., revenue from an unaffected region or product line) — worth revisiting after you see the results.

One flag before we proceed: monthly data often has **annual seasonality** (holiday effects, fiscal cycles). I'll include a seasonality check and seasonal dummies in the code. If revenue is relatively flat month-to-month, this may not matter — but it's worth verifying.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption for your specific setup.

### ✳️ 1. No Concurrent Events
> *Did anything else happen in month 43 — or the months around it — that could have shifted revenue?*

This is the **single biggest threat** to your analysis and is untestable from data alone. Think through: Did you change pricing? Launch a new product? Did a competitor enter or exit? Was there a macro shock (recession, supply chain disruption)?

**Action**: Make a list of anything that changed around month 43. The analysis can estimate the size of the shift, but it can't tell you *why* it happened. If concurrent events exist, the campaign effect is confounded.

### ✳️ 2. Pre-Period Model Fit
> *Can the model accurately predict revenue from the pre-intervention trend alone?*

With 42 months, the model has enough data to learn the trend. The diagnostic question is whether a simple linear trend (plus any seasonal pattern) fits well. If MAPE > 10% in the pre-period, the counterfactual is unreliable. The code below tests this explicitly.

### ⚠️ 3. Stationarity
> *Is the pre-intervention revenue series mean-reverting, or does it drift?*

If revenue has a strong upward trend (it often does for growing businesses), the series is non-stationary. This can make a natural trend continuation look like a campaign effect. The segmented regression handles this by explicitly modeling the pre-period slope — but we should verify this is adequate. ADF and KPSS tests included below.

### ⚠️ 4. Structural Breaks in Pre-Period
> *Did anything unusual happen in the 42 months before the campaign?*

A hidden level shift or trend break in the pre-period (e.g., a COVID disruption in month 15) would corrupt the baseline model. I'll include a CUSUM test to catch this.

### ✅ 5. Pre-Period Length
> *42 months (3.5 years) — adequate for monthly data.*

Covers 3.5 seasonal cycles if annual seasonality exists. Borderline but workable. If you have additional historical data, extending further back would strengthen the analysis.

**Assumption summary:**

| Assumption | Status | Verdict |
|---|---|---|
| No concurrent events | Untestable | 🔴 Must argue from context |
| Pre-period model fit | Testable | Run diagnostics below |
| Stationarity | Testable | Run ADF/KPSS below |
| No structural breaks in pre-period | Testable | Run CUSUM below |
| Adequate pre-period length | ✅ 42 months | OK |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.gridspec as gridspec
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import statsmodels.api as sm
from statsmodels.tsa.stattools import adfuller, kpss
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import acorr_ljungbox
from statsmodels.graphics.tsaplots import plot_acf
from scipy.stats import shapiro

# ── 0. Load your data ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Replace this with your actual load (e.g., pd.read_csv)
# df must have columns: time (int), outcome (float), post (int 0/1)

# Intervention point: last pre-treatment time index
intervention_time = df.loc[df['post'].diff() == 1, 'time'].values[0] - 1
# → 42 (time 43 is the first post-treatment month)
print(f"Intervention at end of time period: {intervention_time}")

# ── 1. Feature engineering ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = df.copy()
df['time_since'] = np.maximum(0, df['time'] - intervention_time)

# Optional: monthly seasonal dummies (drop one to avoid multicollinearity)
# Requires a 'month' column — create from time if you don't have actual dates
df['month'] = ((df['time'] - 1) % 12) + 1          # 1–12 cycling
season_dummies = pd.get_dummies(df['month'], prefix='m', drop_first=True)
df = pd.concat([df, season_dummies], axis=1)

# ── 2. Pre-period diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
pre = df[df['post'] == 0].copy()
pre_series = pre['outcome'].values

print("\n── Stationarity Tests (pre-period) ──")
adf = adfuller(pre_series, autolag='AIC')
print(f"ADF statistic: {adf[0]:.4f}  |  p-value: {adf[1]:.4f}")
print("  → p < 0.05 means stationary (good)" if adf[1] < 0.05
      else "  ⚠️  p ≥ 0.05: possible unit root — consider differencing")

kpss_result = kpss(pre_series, regression='c', nlags='auto')
print(f"KPSS statistic: {kpss_result[0]:.4f}  |  p-value: {kpss_result[1]:.4f}")
print("  → p > 0.05 means stationary (good)" if kpss_result[1] > 0.05
      else "  ⚠️  p ≤ 0.05: non-stationary signal")

print("\n── Structural Break Check (CUSUM) ──")
# Fit a simple trend model on pre-period; inspect CUSUM of residuals
pre_trend_model = smf.ols('outcome ~ time', data=pre).fit()
cusum = pre_trend_model.resid.cumsum()
cusum_bounds = 1.36 * np.sqrt(len(pre))           # ~5% critical value
print(f"CUSUM max absolute value: {cusum.abs().max():.2f}  |  Bound: {cusum_bounds:.2f}")
if cusum.abs().max() > cusum_bounds:
    print("  ⚠️  CUSUM exceeds bounds — possible structural break in pre-period.")
    print("     Inspect the CUSUM plot and investigate what happened.")
else:
    print("  ✅ No strong evidence of structural break in pre-period.")

fig_cusum, ax_cusum = plt.subplots(figsize=(10, 3))
ax_cusum.plot(pre['time'], cusum, color='steelblue', label='CUSUM of residuals')
ax_cusum.axhline(cusum_bounds, color='red', linestyle='--', label='±5% bounds')
ax_cusum.axhline(-cusum_bounds, color='red', linestyle='--')
ax_cusum.axhline(0, color='black', linewidth=0.8)
ax_cusum.set(title='CUSUM Test — Pre-Period Structural Stability',
             xlabel='Time', ylabel='Cumulative Sum')
ax_cusum.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# ── 3. Segmented regression (ITS) ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# Model: outcome = β0 + β1*time + β2*post + β3*time_since + seasonal + ε
# β2 = level shift at intervention
# β3 = slope change after intervention
# HAC standard errors correct for autocorrelation (Newey-West, 4 lags ~ sqrt(42))

season_cols = [c for c in df.columns if c.startswith('m_')]
season_formula = ' + '.join(season_cols) if season_cols else ''
formula = f'outcome ~ time + post + time_since'
if season_formula:
    formula += ' + ' + season_formula

model = smf.ols(formula, data=df).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4})
print("\n── Segmented Regression Results ──")
print(model.summary())

level_change = model.params['post']
slope_change = model.params['time_since']
level_ci = model.conf_int().loc['post']
slope_ci = model.conf_int().loc['time_since']

print(f"\n📌 Level shift at campaign launch:  {level_change:+.3f}  "
      f"(95% CI: [{level_ci[0]:.3f}, {level_ci[1]:.3f}])")
print(f"📌 Slope change post-campaign:      {slope_change:+.3f}  "
      f"(95% CI: [{slope_ci[0]:.3f}, {slope_ci[1]:.3f}])")

# Cumulative effect over 6 post-treatment months
post_data = df[df['post'] == 1].copy()
post_data['counterfactual'] = (
    model.params['Intercept']
    + model.params['time'] * post_data['time']
    + sum(model.params.get(c, 0) * post_data[c]
          for c in season_cols)
)
post_data['observed'] = post_data['outcome']
post_data['point_effect'] = post_data['observed'] - post_data['counterfactual']
cumulative_effect = post_data['point_effect'].sum()
print(f"\n📌 Cumulative effect over 6 post-months: {cumulative_effect:+.3f}")

# ── 4. Pre-period fit assessment ───────────────────────────────────────────────
pre_fitted = model.fittedvalues[df['post'] == 0]
pre_actual = df.loc[df['post'] == 0, 'outcome']
pre_residuals = pre_actual - pre_fitted

mape = (np.abs(pre_residuals / pre_actual)).mean() * 100
print(f"\n── Pre-Period Model Fit ──")
print(f"MAPE: {mape:.2f}%  ", end='')
if mape < 5:
    print("✅ Excellent fit")
elif mape < 10:
    print("✅ Acceptable fit")
else:
    print("⚠️  Poor fit — counterfactual may be unreliable")

lb = acorr_ljungbox(pre_residuals, lags=10, return_df=True)
lb_p = lb['lb_pvalue'].min()
print(f"Ljung-Box (min p across 10 lags): {lb_p:.4f}  ", end='')
print("✅ Residuals are white noise" if lb_p > 0.05
      else "⚠️  Residual autocorrelation — consider ARIMA robustness check")

sw_stat, sw_p = shapiro(pre_residuals)
print(f"Shapiro-Wilk normality: p = {sw_p:.4f}  ", end='')
print("✅ Normal" if sw_p > 0.05 else "⚠️  Non-normal residuals")

# ── 5. Main results plot ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 5))

# Counterfactual: project pre-period trend into post-period (no campaign)
df['counterfactual'] = (
    model.params['Intercept']
    + model.params['time'] * df['time']
    + sum(model.params.get(c, 0) * df[c] for c in season_cols)
)

ax.axvline(intervention_time + 0.5, color='red', linestyle='--',
           linewidth=1.5, label='Campaign launch')
ax.plot(df['time'], df['outcome'], 'o-', color='steelblue',
        label='Observed revenue', markersize=4, linewidth=1.5)
ax.plot(df['time'], model.fittedvalues, color='steelblue',
        linestyle='--', linewidth=1, alpha=0.6, label='Model fit')
ax.plot(df.loc[df['post'] == 1, 'time'],
        df.loc[df['post'] == 1, 'counterfactual'],
        'o--', color='gray', label='Counterfactual (no campaign)',
        markersize=4, linewidth=1.5)

# Shade the effect
ax.fill_between(
    df.loc[df['post'] == 1, 'time'],
    df.loc[df['post'] == 1, 'counterfactual'],
    df.loc[df['post'] == 1, 'outcome'],
    alpha=0.2, color='green', label='Estimated campaign effect')

ax.set(title='Interrupted Time Series — Campaign Effect on Revenue',
       xlabel='Time (months)', ylabel='Revenue')
ax.legend(loc='upper left')
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# ── 6. Residual diagnostics plot ──────────────────────────────────────────────
fig2, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 4))
axes[0].plot(pre['time'], pre_residuals, 'o-', color='steelblue', markersize=4)
axes[0].axhline(0, color='black', linewidth=0.8)
axes[0].set(title='Pre-Period Residuals', xlabel='Time', ylabel='Residual')
plot_acf(pre_residuals, ax=axes[1], title='ACF of Pre-Period Residuals')
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Run a **placebo intervention date** test — apply the same model using a fake treatment date in the middle of the pre-period. If the model finds a "significant effect" there too, it's a sign the model is over-fitting noise rather than detecting a real signal.

```python
# ── Placebo test: fake intervention at time = 21 (midpoint of pre-period) ──────
PLACEBO_TIME = 21   # adjust as needed — pick a "boring" period with no events

df_placebo = df[df['time'] <= intervention_time].copy()  # pre-period only
df_placebo['post_placebo'] = (df_placebo['time'] > PLACEBO_TIME).astype(int)
df_placebo['time_since_placebo'] = np.maximum(
    0, df_placebo['time'] - PLACEBO_TIME)

season_cols_p = [c for c in df_placebo.columns if c.startswith('m_')]
formula_p = 'outcome ~ time + post_placebo + time_since_placebo'
if season_cols_p:
    formula_p += ' + ' + ' + '.join(season_cols_p)

model_placebo = smf.ols(formula_p, data=df_placebo).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4})

placebo_level = model_placebo.params['post_placebo']
placebo_p = model_placebo.pvalues['post_placebo']
print(f"\n── Placebo Test (fake intervention at time={PLACEBO_TIME}) ──")
print(f"Level shift:  {placebo_level:+.3f}  |  p-value: {placebo_p:.4f}")
if placebo_p < 0.05:
    print("⚠️  Significant placebo effect — model may be unreliable "
          "or a concurrent event/structural break exists in the pre-period.")
else:
    print("✅ No significant placebo effect — supports model validity.")
```

Also worth running: **apply the same model to a metric that should not have been affected** by the campaign (e.g., costs, headcount, or revenue from a product the campaign didn't cover). A significant effect there would suggest a concurrent event, not the campaign.

---

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.8888888888888888}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## One Critical Issue Before We Model

Your data collection change around month 15–20 is a **structural break in the pre-treatment period** — and this directly threatens the ITS counterfactual. Here's why it matters:

ITS works by fitting a model to the pre-period, then projecting it forward as "what would have happened without the regulation." If the pre-period has an unmodeled level shift or slope change, the model learns the wrong trajectory and the counterfactual is off. This is flagged explicitly in the method's assumption checklist.

You have two ways to handle it. Let's check which is needed first.

---

## Step 1: Diagnose the Break Formally

Before touching the ITS model, run this:

```r
library(strucchange)
library(tidyverse)

df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")

# --- Visual inspection first ---
ggplot(df, aes(x = time, y = outcome)) +
  geom_line() +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 37, color = "red", linetype = "dashed", linewidth = 1) +
  annotate("rect", xmin = 15, xmax = 20, ymin = -Inf, ymax = Inf,
           alpha = 0.15, fill = "orange") +
  annotate("text", x = 17.5, y = max(df$outcome) * 0.98,
           label = "Methodology\nchange", size = 3.5, color = "darkorange") +
  labs(title = "Outcome over time",
       subtitle = "Red dashed = regulation (month 37); orange = suspected break",
       x = "Month", y = "Outcome") +
  theme_minimal()

# --- Formal Chow test at month 15 and 20 ---
pre_df <- filter(df, post == 0)  # months 1–36 only

# Test for break at month 15
chow_15 <- sctest(outcome ~ time, data = pre_df,
                  type = "Chow", point = which(pre_df$time == 15))
cat("Chow test at month 15: F =", round(chow_15$statistic, 3),
    "  p =", round(chow_15$p.value, 4), "\n")

# Test for break at month 20
chow_20 <- sctest(outcome ~ time, data = pre_df,
                  type = "Chow", point = which(pre_df$time == 20))
cat("Chow test at month 20: F =", round(chow_20$statistic, 3),
    "  p =", round(chow_20$p.value, 4), "\n")

# --- CUSUM test: detects instability across the whole pre-period ---
cusum_test <- efp(outcome ~ time, data = pre_df, type = "OLS-CUSUM")
plot(cusum_test, main = "CUSUM test — pre-period stability")
# Bands crossed → instability; bands NOT crossed → stable enough
```

**What to look for:**
| Result | Meaning |
|--------|---------|
| Chow p < 0.05 | Confirmed structural break — must address it |
| CUSUM crosses bounds | Pre-period is unstable — counterfactual will be unreliable |
| Chow p > 0.05, CUSUM stable | The visible shift may just be noise — proceed with standard ITS |

---

## Step 2: ITS Model — Two Variants Depending on Diagnosis

### Variant A — Break confirmed: model it explicitly

This is the preferred approach. You include the methodology change as covariates so the model learns the "true" post-break trajectory for its counterfactual projection.

```r
library(sandwich)
library(lmtest)

# --- Build ITS variables ---
break_point <- 17  # set to confirmed break month (refine after Chow test)

df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    # Standard ITS terms
    time_since_treat = pmax(0, time - 37),

    # Methodology change terms
    break_dummy     = as.integer(time >= break_point),  # level shift at break
    time_since_break = pmax(0, time - break_point)       # slope change at break
  )

# --- Model: baseline trend + methodology break + intervention break ---
model <- lm(
  outcome ~ time                # baseline slope
          + break_dummy         # level shift at methodology change
          + time_since_break    # slope change at methodology change
          + post                # level shift at regulation
          + time_since_treat,   # slope change at regulation
  data = df
)

# HAC standard errors (accounts for autocorrelation in monthly data)
coeftest(model, vcov = vcovHAC(model))
```

**Interpreting the coefficients:**
- `post` → **immediate level change** when regulation hits (month 37)
- `time_since_treat` → **change in monthly slope** after regulation
- `break_dummy` / `time_since_break` → the methodology artifact (nuisance terms, not the effect of interest)

```r
# --- Counterfactual plot ---
df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    fitted = predict(model),
    # Counterfactual: zero out the regulation terms
    counterfactual = predict(model, newdata = mutate(df,
                              post = 0, time_since_treat = 0))
  )

ggplot(df, aes(x = time)) +
  geom_line(aes(y = outcome), color = "black") +
  geom_line(aes(y = fitted), color = "steelblue", linetype = "dashed") +
  geom_line(aes(y = counterfactual), color = "tomato", linetype = "dotted") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 37, color = "red", linetype = "dashed") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = break_point, color = "orange", linetype = "dashed") +
  annotate("text", x = 37.5, y = max(df$outcome), label = "Regulation", color = "red", hjust = 0) +
  annotate("text", x = break_point + 0.5, y = min(df$outcome), label = "Break", color = "darkorange", hjust = 0) +
  labs(title = "ITS: Observed vs Fitted vs Counterfactual",
       subtitle = "Black = observed | Blue dashed = fitted | Red dotted = counterfactual (no regulation)",
       x = "Month", y = "Outcome") +
  theme_minimal()
```

---

### Variant B — Break not confirmed: standard ITS

If the Chow test is non-significant and CUSUM is stable, the simpler model is fine:

```r
df <- df %>%
  mutate(time_since_treat = pmax(0, time - 37))

model_simple <- lm(outcome ~ time + post + time_since_treat, data = df)
coeftest(model_simple, vcov = vcovHAC(model_simple))
```

---

## Step 3: Pre-Period Fit Check (Either Variant)

After fitting, always verify the counterfactual is trustworthy:

```r
pre_fitted    <- fitted(model)[df$post == 0]
pre_observed  <- df$outcome[df$post == 0]
pre_residuals <- pre_observed - pre_fitted

# MAPE
mape <- mean(abs(pre_residuals / pre_observed)) * 100
cat("Pre-period MAPE:", round(mape, 2), "%\n")
# < 5% = good | 5–10% = acceptable | > 10% = model is unreliable

# Autocorrelation in residuals (HAC SEs help, but still worth checking)
Box.test(pre_residuals, lag = 10, type = "Ljung-Box")
# p > 0.05 → residuals are white noise (good)
acf(pre_residuals, main = "ACF of pre-period residuals")
```

<details><summary>Response</summary>

--- Step 1 ---
Great setup — clean panel, single treatment date, two groups. This is a **classic TWFE DiD**, which is the right tool here. Let me walk you through all 5 stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup ✅

Here's what I'm working with:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | New checkout system, rolled out at month 12 |
| **Control** | Stores never treated |
| **Outcome** | Sales |
| **Design** | Single treatment date → Classic TWFE DiD |
| **Data** | 100 stores × 24 months balanced panel |
| **Language** | R |

Because all treated stores get the treatment at the same time (month 12), we don't need a staggered estimator — standard TWFE with unit + time fixed effects is valid.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption for your specific context, one by one.

---

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Must verify*

**What it means here**: Without the new checkout system, would treated and control stores have followed the same sales trajectory? They don't need to have the same *level* of sales — just the same *trend*. For example, if your treated stores were already growing faster before month 12, DiD would falsely attribute that pre-existing advantage to the checkout system.

**Can we test it?** Partially. We can run an event study on months 1–11 and check whether pre-treatment coefficients are near zero. This is the most important diagnostic. I'll generate that code below.

**Your context**: Think about *how* stores were assigned to the first wave. If higher-volume stores got it first (e.g., rollout prioritized busy stores), trends may differ. Worth flagging.

---

### 2. No Anticipation ⚠️ *Worth checking*

**What it means here**: Did treated stores start changing behavior *before* month 12 — e.g., pre-training staff, reorganizing layouts in anticipation of the new system? If so, the treatment effect "bleeds" into the pre-period, making both the pre-trend test and the post-treatment estimate unreliable.

**How to check**: Look at the event study coefficient at t = −2. If it's already moving in the direction of the post-treatment effect, anticipation is likely.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Likely fine*

**What it means here**: Stores shouldn't enter or leave your sample *because of* the checkout system. If the new system caused some low-performing stores to close and drop out of your data, your treated group survivors would be positively selected.

**Your context**: With 100 stores over 24 months in a retail chain, attrition is unlikely — but I'll include a balance check in the code.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ✅ *Probably fine*

**What it means here**: The new checkout system at treated stores shouldn't change sales at control stores. This could be violated if, for example, faster checkout at treated stores pulled customers *away* from nearby control stores (cannibalization).

**Your context**: If treated and control stores are in different geographies, this is low risk. If they're in the same markets competing for the same customers, it's worth thinking about.

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Not a concern here*

**What it means here**: The worry about biased TWFE weights only applies to *staggered* rollouts where units are treated at different times. Since all your treated stores got the system at month 12, this is a non-issue. Standard TWFE is valid.

---

**Assumption Summary**

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | ⚠️ Verify | Event study + pre-trends test |
| No anticipation | ⚠️ Check | Inspect t = −1, −2 coefficients |
| Stable composition | ✅ Likely OK | Quick attrition check |
| No spillovers | ✅ Context-dependent | Flag if stores compete locally |
| Correct functional form | ✅ Not applicable | Single treatment date |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the complete analysis. I've structured it so you can run it top to bottom.

```r
# ── 0. Packages ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. Data prep ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns: store_id, month, sales, treated
# treated = 1 for stores that received the new checkout system, 0 otherwise

# Create post indicator and interaction term
df <- df |>
  mutate(
    post          = as.integer(month >= 12),
    treated_post  = treated * post,
    # Time-to-treatment (relative time, for event study)
    time_to_treat = month - 12
  )

# Sanity check
df |>
  count(treated, post) |>
  print()

# ── 2. Balance / attrition check ──────────────────────────────────────────────
panel_balance <- df |>
  group_by(store_id) |>
  summarize(
    n_months = n_distinct(month),
    treated  = max(treated)
  ) |>
  group_by(treated) |>
  summarize(
    n_stores        = n(),
    mean_months     = mean(n_months),
    fully_observed  = sum(n_months == 24)
  )

print(panel_balance)
# Both groups should show n_months = 24. Any shortfall = attrition to investigate.

# ── 3. Pre-trends visualization (raw) ─────────────────────────────────────────
df |>
  group_by(month, treated) |>
  summarize(mean_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop") |>
  mutate(group = if_else(treated == 1, "Treated", "Control")) |>
  ggplot(aes(x = month, y = mean_sales, color = group, group = group)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_point(size = 2) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 11.5, linetype = "dashed", color = "gray40") +
  annotate("text", x = 12.2, y = Inf, label = "Treatment", vjust = 1.5,
           hjust = 0, color = "gray40", size = 3.5) +
  scale_color_manual(values = c("Control" = "#2166ac", "Treated" = "#d6604d")) +
  labs(title = "Sales Trends: Treated vs. Control Stores",
       x = "Month", y = "Mean Sales", color = NULL) +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 13) +
  theme(legend.position = "top")
```

```r
# ── 4. Event study (formal pre-trends test) ───────────────────────────────────
# Reference period: t = -1 (month 11, one month before treatment)
# NOTE: `treated` is absorbed by unit FE — do NOT include it as a regressor.
# The i() interaction with treated already handles group differences over time.

es <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store_id + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

# Plot: pre-treatment coefficients should hover near zero
iplot(es,
      main = "Event Study: Effect of New Checkout System on Sales",
      xlab = "Months Relative to Treatment (Month 12)",
      ylab = "Estimated Effect on Sales",
      ref.line = TRUE)
abline(v = -0.5, col = "gray50", lty = 2)

# Joint test of pre-treatment coefficients (H₀: all pre-treatment βs = 0)
# A rejection here = evidence of pre-existing trends → parallel trends violated
wald(es, keep = "time_to_treat::-")
```

```r
# ── 5. Main DiD estimate (TWFE) ────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Unit FE absorbs time-invariant store characteristics (size, location, etc.)
# Time FE absorbs common shocks affecting all stores each month
# Clustered SEs at store level account for within-store serial correlation

model_did <- feols(
  sales ~ treated_post | store_id + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

summary(model_did)

# Clean results table
modelsummary(
  model_did,
  stars     = c("*" = 0.10, "**" = 0.05, "***" = 0.01),
  gof_map   = c("nobs", "r.squared", "FE: store_id", "FE: month"),
  title     = "DiD Estimate: Effect of New Checkout System on Sales"
)
```

```r
# ── 6. Effect size summary ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
coef_est <- coef(model_did)["treated_post"]
ci       <- confint(model_did)["treated_post", ]
mean_pre <- df |>
  filter(treated == 1, post == 0) |>
  pull(sales) |>
  mean()

cat(sprintf(
  "\n--- DiD Estimate ---\n
  ATT:             %.2f (units of sales)
  95%% CI:         [%.2f, %.2f]
  Pre-treat mean:  %.2f (treated stores, pre-period)
  Effect size:     %.1f%% relative to pre-treatment baseline\n",
  coef_est, ci[1], ci[2], mean_pre,
  100 * coef_est / mean_pre
))
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Two robustness checks I recommend for your setup:

```r
# ── Robustness Check 1: Placebo test (fake treatment date) ───────────────────
# Restrict to pre-treatment data only (months 1–11) and test a fake cutoff
# at month 6. If DiD detects an "effect" here, something is wrong.

df_pre <- df |>
  filter(month <= 11) |>
  mutate(
    post_placebo         = as.integer(month >= 6),
    treated_post_placebo = treated * post_placebo
  )

model_placebo <- feols(
  sales ~ treated_post_placebo | store_id + month,
  data    = df_pre,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

cat("Placebo estimate (should be near zero):\n")
summary(model_placebo)
```

```r
# ── Robustness Check 2: Pre-trends joint significance test ───────────────────
# Already embedded in the event study (wald() call above), but report clearly:
# If p > 0.10 → no evidence against parallel trends
# If p < 0.05 → pre-trends problem, interpret main estimate cautiously
```

## Stage 5: Interpretation

Once you run the code, fill in the blanks:

> **The new checkout system is associated with a sales change of `[coef_est]` per store-month** (95% CI: `[ci_low]`, `[ci_high]`), or approximately **`[X]%`** relative to treated stores' pre-treatment mean.

> This estimate is identified under the assumption that — absent the checkout system — treated and control stores would have followed parallel sales trends. The pre-trends test `[supports / does not support]` this assumption.

**Caveats to include**:
- If the parallel trends test is borderline, note that the estimate may partially reflect pre-existing differences rather than the checkout system's effect.
- DiD estimates the **ATT** — the effect on the stores that were treated. It doesn't tell you what would happen if you rolled out to different stores.
- If treated stores tend to be higher-volume, the effect may not generalize to the control stores.

---

```markdown
# Audit Report: Checkout System DiD

**Date**: 2026-04-01
**Method audited**: Difference-in-Differences (Classic TWFE, single treatment date)
**Overall assessment**: 🟡 Yellow — No fatal structural problems, but four serious
threats that need direct responses before this analysis is credible.

## Summary

The method skill produced a solid foundation — correct estimator, properly clustered
SEs, good pre-trends diagnostics. Four issues escaped scrutiny: (1) the reference
period choice leaves anticipation undetectable at the most likely window; (2) the
store selection mechanism was waved away rather than interrogated; (3) a new checkout
system almost certainly means a new POS system, creating a potential measurement
confound; (4) month 12 may be December, introducing differential seasonal exposure.

---

## Findings

### Serious: Anticipation undetectable at t = -1
**Category**: 1 — Assumption Violations
**Explanation**: ref = -1 normalizes the most anticipation-prone period to zero.
Staff training and pre-installation prep happen in month 11. Shift reference to t = -4.
**Diagnostic**: feols(sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -4) | store_id + month, ...)
**Fix**: Re-run with ref = -4. If t = -1 coefficient is significant and positive,
redefine treatment date as announcement/notification date, or use anticipation = 1.

### Serious: Store selection mechanism not interrogated
**Category**: 2 — Identification Threats
**Explanation**: Flagship-first vs. problem-store-first selection produce opposite
bias directions. Pre-trends test may not catch a divergence that predates the panel.
**Diagnostic**: Compare pre-treatment sales growth rates (t.test(sales_growth ~ treated))
**Fix**: Document selection governance. If performance-based, consider synthetic control.

### Serious: New checkout system may mean new POS — measurement confound
**Category**: 3 — Data Threats
**Explanation**: Different POS recording logic could produce apparent sales changes
that are artifacts of measurement, not behavior. Not correctable by FEs.
**Diagnostic**: Confirm with data team that sales metric is identical pre/post swap.
Run DiD on transaction counts as parallel outcome.
**Fix**: Operational verification required. No regression fix available.

### Serious: Month 12 may be December — differential seasonal exposure
**Category**: 3 — Data Threats
**Explanation**: Time FEs handle common seasonality but not differential seasonality.
If treated stores are in higher-holiday-traffic locations, December onset confounds.
**Diagnostic**: Confirm calendar mapping. Compare December patterns across groups
using prior-year data if available.
**Fix**: If month 12 = December, extend pre-treatment window to include a prior
December, or match control stores on market/location type.

### Minor: No pre-treatment covariate balance check
**Category**: 3 — Data Threats
**Fix**: Add t-test and SMD on baseline sales and store characteristics. SMD > 0.3
warrants concern about the comparison group's validity.

### Minor: Constant effect assumption across 12 post-months
**Category**: 4 — Statistical Threats
**Fix**: Estimate early (months 12–15) vs. late (months 16–24) ATT separately.
Distinguishes learning curves from novelty fadeout.

---

## Recommendations (Prioritized)

1. Re-run event study with ref = -4 to expose t = -1 (5 min, high value)
2. Verify POS measurement consistency with data team (operational, not statistical)
3. Confirm calendar month mapping — if month 12 = December, threat is serious
4. Document store selection mechanism — if performance-based, reconsider method
5. Add pre-treatment balance table
6. Add early vs. late post-treatment split to results
```

---

**One update to `references/lessons.md`** — the method skill missed this and it's worth recording:

```markdown
### DiD: Anticipation reference period in retail technology rollouts
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: DiD analysis of technology/system rollouts using ref = -1 in event study
**Mistake**: Used t = -1 as the reference period without flagging that pre-installation
activity (training, hardware delivery, layout changes) makes t = -1 the highest-risk
anticipation window in this context. Anticipation at the reference period is undetectable.
**Rule**: In rollout contexts where preparation precedes go-live, flag ref = -1 as a
potential blind spot. Recommend ref = -3 or -4 so that t = -1 and t = -2 are both
estimated and visible in the event study.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

---

**Bottom line for you**: The two findings I'd prioritize above everything else are the **reference period fix** (10 lines of code, run it now) and the **POS measurement question** (not a code problem — you need to ask your data team whether "sales" is recorded identically before and after the system swap). The measurement confound is the one that can produce a perfectly clean event study and a perfectly wrong conclusion.

</details>

--- Step 1 ---

---

## 📊 Scenario

You're a data analyst at **RetailCo**, a mid-sized brick-and-mortar chain with 100 stores. In month 13, the company rolled out a new in-store layout (better signage, product repositioning) in 50 randomly selected stores. The other 50 stores kept the original layout. You have 24 months of monthly revenue data.

**Your manager's question:** Did the new layout increase monthly revenue, and by how much?

---

## Python — Generate & Save the Data

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(303)
n_stores = 100
n_months = 24
treat_month = 13

store_id = np.repeat(np.arange(1, n_stores + 1), n_months)
month = np.tile(np.arange(1, n_months + 1), n_stores)

treated = (store_id <= 50).astype(int)
post = (month >= treat_month).astype(int)

store_fe = np.repeat(np.random.normal(0, 3, n_stores), n_months)
time_trend = 0.2 * month

revenue = (
    50 + store_fe + time_trend + 5.0 * treated * post
    + np.random.normal(0, 2, n_stores * n_months)
)

df = pd.DataFrame({
    "store_id": store_id,
    "month": month,
    "treated": treated,
    "post": post,
    "revenue": np.round(revenue, 2),
})

df.to_csv("exercise_data.csv", index=False)
print(df.head(10))
print(f"\nShape: {df.shape}")
print(f"Treated stores: {df['treated'].sum() // n_months}")
print(f"Control stores: {(1 - df['treated']).sum() // n_months}")
```

Run that and you'll have `exercise_data.csv` in your working directory.

---

## ✅ True Treatment Effect

Since you asked for it upfront: **the true ATT is $5,000/month** (revenue is in thousands). The DGP is a clean 2×2 DiD — parallel trends hold, no anticipation effects, balanced panel. A well-specified DiD should recover this closely.

---

## Your Task

Load `exercise_data.csv` and estimate the ATT using DiD. A few things to think through:

1. What's your identifying assumption, and how would you check it?
2. How are you handling store-level heterogeneity?
3. What does an event study plot tell you pre-treatment?

Let me know your estimate when you're done and I'll walk through the debrief. 🎯

--- Step 2 ---
## RetailCo DiD Walkthrough

Nice clean DGP to work with. Let's go stage by stage.

---

## Stage 1: Design Confirmation

Before touching code, let's orient:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Treatment | New store layout, month 13 |
| Groups | 50 treated, 50 control (random assignment) |
| Outcome | Monthly revenue |
| Structure | Balanced panel — 100 stores × 24 months |
| Variant | **Classic 2×2 DiD → TWFE** (single treatment date) |

Random assignment of stores is a strong gift here — it directly shores up the parallel trends assumption. We'll still verify it empirically.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let's work through each one in your specific context.

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Partially testable — most important*

**What it means here:** Without the layout change, treated stores (1–50) and control stores (51–100) would have followed the same revenue trajectory month over month. The layout didn't cause the two groups to diverge on their own.

**Why random assignment helps but doesn't eliminate the need to check:** Random assignment makes parallel trends *much* more plausible — it removes selection into treatment. But it doesn't guarantee identical pre-trends in finite samples. You still want to see this empirically.

**How to check:** An event study plot. Pre-treatment coefficients (months 1–12 relative to month 13) should all sit near zero with no visible trend. We'll generate this below.

---

### 2. No Anticipation ✅ *Low concern here*

**What it means here:** Store managers and staff didn't start behaving differently before month 13 (e.g., by rearranging the store informally, or pushing sales harder in anticipation of the new layout).

**Why it's low concern:** The treatment is a physical layout change — it either happened or it didn't. There's no announcement effect that would shift customer behavior before installation. In real data you'd want to confirm this, but the DGP is clean.

**Signal to watch:** In the event study, t = −2 and t = −3 coefficients that creep positive would suggest anticipation.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Low concern — balanced panel*

**What it means here:** The same 100 stores are observed all 24 months. No store opened or closed *because* of the layout treatment.

**Why it matters:** If weak stores were closed after the layout underperformed, you'd only observe surviving treated stores — biasing the ATT upward. With a balanced panel (100 stores × 24 months = 2,400 rows), this is clean.

**Quick check:** `assert df.shape == (2400, 5)` — if that passes, your panel is complete.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ⚠️ *Untestable — worth thinking through*

**What it means here:** The layout change in treated stores doesn't affect revenue at control stores. In a retail context, the main threat is **within-market cannibalization** — if a treated store draws foot traffic away from a nearby control store, the control store's revenue drops, making the treatment look more effective than it is.

**Why it may be okay here:** The exercise doesn't specify geography, but if treated and control stores serve distinct local markets, spillovers are minimal. If stores compete locally, this is a real threat.

**What you can't test directly:** Whether control store outcomes shifted *because* treated stores nearby changed. You'd need geographic data to even attempt this.

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Not a concern — single treatment date*

**What it means here:** The "biased TWFE with heterogeneous effects" problem only arises in **staggered** designs where different cohorts are treated at different times. With a single treatment date and two clean groups, standard TWFE gives you the correct ATT. No need for Callaway-Sant'Anna or Sun-Abraham here.

**The intuition for why:** In staggered designs, TWFE implicitly uses already-treated units as controls for later-treated units — "forbidden comparisons." With a single date, there are no such comparisons. Treated vs. control is the only comparison made.

---

**Assumption Summary**

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | 🟡 Testable — check pre-trends | Event study plot |
| No anticipation | 🟢 Low concern | Visual confirmation in event study |
| Stable composition | 🟢 Clean balanced panel | Verify row count |
| No spillovers | 🟡 Untestable — think through geography | Flag as caveat |
| Correct functional form | 🟢 Single date, no issue | None |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

A few things to note in this code before you run it:

- **Don't include `treated` as a standalone regressor.** It's time-invariant (same value for a store across all months), so `EntityEffects` absorbs it completely. Including it causes perfect multicollinearity and will crash PanelOLS. Only the *interaction* `treated × post` varies over time — that's your treatment indicator.
- **Cluster standard errors at the store level.** Revenue within a store is correlated across months. Clustering accounts for this within-unit serial correlation.

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.ticker as mticker
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS

# ── 1. Load & prep ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = pd.read_csv("exercise_data.csv")

# Verify balanced panel
assert df.shape == (2400, 5), f"Unexpected shape: {df.shape}"
print(f"Panel: {df['store_id'].nunique()} stores × {df['month'].nunique()} months")

# Interaction term: the DiD treatment indicator
# Note: treated alone is time-invariant → absorbed by EntityEffects (do NOT include it)
# post alone is unit-invariant → absorbed by TimeEffects (do NOT include it)
# treated_post varies in both dimensions → this is your causal variable
df["treated_post"] = df["treated"] * df["post"]

# ── 2. Main TWFE estimate ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
panel_df = df.set_index(["store_id", "month"])

model = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    "revenue ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects",
    data=panel_df
)
res = model.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)
print(res.summary.tables[1])

# Extract ATT with 95% CI
att    = res.params["treated_post"]
ci_low = res.conf_int().loc["treated_post", "lower"]
ci_hi  = res.conf_int().loc["treated_post", "upper"]

print(f"\n{'─'*45}")
print(f"  ATT estimate  : ${att:,.2f}K/month")
print(f"  95% CI        : [${ci_low:,.2f}K, ${ci_hi:,.2f}K]")
print(f"  True effect   : $5.00K/month")
print(f"{'─'*45}")

# ── 3. Event study ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Build relative-time dummies; omit t = -1 as the reference period
treat_month = 13
df["rel_time"] = df["month"] - treat_month          # ranges from -12 to +11

# Dummies for all periods except reference (rel_time = -1)
periods = sorted(df["rel_time"].unique())
ref_period = -1
event_periods = [p for p in periods if p != ref_period]

for p in event_periods:
    df[f"ttp_{p}"] = ((df["rel_time"] == p) & (df["treated"] == 1)).astype(int)

ttp_cols = [f"ttp_{p}" for p in event_periods]

panel_es = df.set_index(["store_id", "month"])
es_formula = "revenue ~ " + " + ".join(ttp_cols) + " + EntityEffects + TimeEffects"

es_model = PanelOLS.from_formula(es_formula, data=panel_es)
es_res   = es_model.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)

# Collect coefficients and CIs
coefs    = [es_res.params[f"ttp_{p}"] for p in event_periods]
ci_lower = [es_res.conf_int().loc[f"ttp_{p}", "lower"] for p in event_periods]
ci_upper = [es_res.conf_int().loc[f"ttp_{p}", "upper"] for p in event_periods]

# Insert zero at the reference period for plotting continuity
plot_periods = sorted(event_periods + [ref_period])
plot_coefs   = []
plot_lower   = []
plot_upper   = []

for p in plot_periods:
    if p == ref_period:
        plot_coefs.append(0); plot_lower.append(0); plot_upper.append(0)
    else:
        idx = event_periods.index(p)
        plot_coefs.append(coefs[idx])
        plot_lower.append(ci_lower[idx])
        plot_upper.append(ci_upper[idx])

errors = [
    np.array(plot_coefs) - np.array(plot_lower),
    np.array(plot_upper) - np.array(plot_coefs)
]

# ── 4. Plot ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(11, 5))

pre_mask  = np.array(plot_periods) < 0
post_mask = np.array(plot_periods) >= 0

ax.errorbar(
    np.array(plot_periods)[pre_mask], np.array(plot_coefs)[pre_mask],
    yerr=[errors[0][pre_mask], errors[1][pre_mask]],
    fmt="o-", color="#4C72B0", capsize=4, label="Pre-treatment", linewidth=1.8
)
ax.errorbar(
    np.array(plot_periods)[post_mask], np.array(plot_coefs)[post_mask],
    yerr=[errors[0][post_mask], errors[1][post_mask]],
    fmt="s-", color="#DD8452", capsize=4, label="Post-treatment", linewidth=1.8
)

ax.axhline(0, color="black", linewidth=0.8, linestyle="--")
ax.axvline(-0.5, color="gray", linewidth=1.2, linestyle=":", label="Treatment onset")
ax.axhline(5.0, color="green", linewidth=1, linestyle="--", alpha=0.6, label="True ATT ($5K)")

ax.set_xlabel("Month Relative to Treatment (month 13)", fontsize=12)
ax.set_ylabel("Revenue Difference vs. Control ($K)", fontsize=12)
ax.set_title("Event Study: New Layout Effect on Monthly Revenue\n(RetailCo DiD — 100 stores, 24 months)", fontsize=13)
ax.yaxis.set_major_formatter(mticker.FormatStrFormatter("$%.1fK"))
ax.legend(fontsize=10)
ax.grid(axis="y", alpha=0.3)
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("event_study.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Saved → event_study.png")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Check

After the main estimate, run a **pre-period placebo test** — it's the single most useful check here:

```python
# ── Placebo: fake treatment at month 7, pre-treatment data only ─────────────
# Logic: if parallel trends hold for real, a fake treatment date mid-pre-period
# should produce an ATT ≈ 0. A significant placebo estimate is a red flag.

df_pre = df[df["month"] < treat_month].copy()   # months 1–12 only
fake_treat_month = 7

df_pre["post_placebo"]    = (df_pre["month"] >= fake_treat_month).astype(int)
df_pre["treated_post_pl"] = df_pre["treated"] * df_pre["post_placebo"]

panel_pl = df_pre.set_index(["store_id", "month"])
placebo_model = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    "revenue ~ treated_post_pl + EntityEffects + TimeEffects",
    data=panel_pl
)
pl_res = placebo_model.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)

pl_att = pl_res.params["treated_post_pl"]
pl_p   = pl_res.pvalues["treated_post_pl"]

print(f"\nPlacebo test (fake treatment at month {fake_treat_month}):")
print(f"  Placebo ATT : ${pl_att:,.2f}K  |  p-value: {pl_p:.3f}")
print(f"  Verdict     : {'✅ Not significant — supports parallel trends' if pl_p > 0.05 else '⚠️  Significant — parallel trends concern'}")
```

**What you're looking for:** A placebo ATT near zero, p > 0.05. If you get a significant placebo effect, the pre-period trends weren't actually parallel — and your main estimate is suspect.

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation

Once you run the code, here's the frame for writing up results:

> **Effect:** The new in-store layout increased monthly revenue by approximately **$X.XXK per store** (95% CI: [$X.XX, $X.XX]), relative to what those stores would have earned under the original layout.
>
> **Pre-trends:** The event study shows no meaningful pre-treatment divergence between treated and control stores — consistent with the parallel trends assumption. Post-treatment, the gap opens immediately and stabilizes around $5K, with no evidence of fading or growth over time.
>
> **Caveats:**
> - *SUTVA:* If treated and control stores serve overlapping markets, the revenue gain for treated stores may partly reflect displaced foot traffic from control stores — overstating the true market-level effect.
> - *External validity:* This is an ATT for the 50 randomly selected stores. Stores not in this sample (e.g., flagship locations or underperformers) may respond differently.

---

## What to Expect When You Run It

With the true ATT of $5K and this DGP (2,400 obs, low noise), your estimate should land in the range of **$4.8K–$5.2K** with tight confidence intervals. The pre-treatment event study coefficients should be statistically indistinguishable from zero — that's the parallel trends check passing visually and empirically.

Run it and share your ATT estimate — happy to debrief the numbers. 🎯

## Stage 1: Setup ✅

Your setup is clear:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Endogenous treatment** | Private tutoring (take-up) |
| **Instrument** | Distance to nearest tutoring center |
| **Outcome** | Test scores |
| **N** | 3,000 students |
| **Language** | R |

A couple of quick questions before we move to assumptions — these will shape the analysis and the interpretation:

1. **Is tutoring binary** (took it / didn't take it) or continuous (hours of tutoring)?
2. **Compliance structure**: Is distance used as a pure instrument, or does this map onto an encouragement design (e.g., students near a center were *invited* to participate)? This affects whether we interpret the result as a **LATE** (effect for students whose tutoring decision was shifted by distance) or something else.
3. **Any covariates** you want to control for? (e.g., household income, prior test scores, school fixed effects)
4. **Is your data cross-sectional** (one observation per student) or panel (same students over multiple periods)?

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions — Let's Work Through These Now

While you answer those, here's a preview of what we'll stress-test. These are the four pillars your instrument must satisfy:

---

### 1. 🔬 Relevance (Testable)
> Does distance to tutoring center actually predict whether a student gets tutoring?

**Why it matters**: A weak first stage means your IV estimate will be biased toward OLS and your confidence intervals will be unreliable — even with 3,000 observations, a weak instrument can make your results worse than just running OLS.

**Threshold**: First-stage F-statistic ≥ 10 is the classic rule, but modern standards (Lee et al. 2022) recommend F ≥ 100 for reliable inference. We'll compute this explicitly.

✅ *This is testable — we'll get the F-stat in Stage 3.*

---

### 2. 🚫 Exclusion Restriction (Not Testable — Argued)
> Does distance affect test scores **only** through tutoring take-up, and through no other channel?

**Why it matters**: This is the hardest assumption and the one most likely to fail here. Let me flag two concerns right away:

- **Urban/rural confounding**: Students who live far from tutoring centers may also live in areas with weaker schools, less parental education, or fewer educational resources — all of which directly affect test scores. Distance would then have a *direct* effect on scores, violating exclusion.
- **Socioeconomic sorting**: Families that can afford to live near tutoring centers may be systematically wealthier, creating a direct link from distance to scores through SES.

We can partially mitigate this by controlling for income, school quality, or neighborhood characteristics — but with observational data, we can never fully rule it out. This will be the central credibility challenge.

❓ *I'll ask you to argue this — does distance affect scores through any channel besides tutoring?*

---

### 3. 🎲 Independence (Partially Testable)
> Is distance "as good as randomly assigned" — independent of unobserved factors that affect test scores?

**Why it matters**: If families strategically choose where to live based on educational resources (tutoring center proximity included), distance isn't random. This is called **residential sorting**, and it's a serious threat here.

We can check balance: does distance correlate with observable covariates like income or parent education? Imbalance on observables is a red flag for imbalance on unobservables.

---

### 4. ↕️ Monotonicity (Not Directly Testable)
> Does shorter distance push all students toward more tutoring (no "defiers")?

**Why it matters**: For LATE interpretation — that the IV estimate applies to students whose tutoring decision was *changed* by distance — we need everyone to respond in the same direction. This is usually plausible here: it's hard to imagine shorter distance *reducing* tutoring take-up for any student.

---

**Before I generate the code**: answer the 4 setup questions above, particularly around covariates and whether you have controls for SES or school quality. The exclusion restriction concern is real and I want to build those controls into the model from the start.

--- Step 2 ---
I don't have a local write tool, so I've saved the audit report to Notion at the link above. Here's the lesson entry you should manually append to `references/lessons.md`:

```markdown
### IV: School-level clustering not flagged in cross-sectional student analyses
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: IV (or any student-level regression) where students are nested in schools
**Mistake**: Analysis plan generated no mention of clustering. Standard errors
  left as heteroskedasticity-robust, inflating t-statistics.
**Rule**: Whenever the unit of observation is a student (or any unit nested in a
  higher-level cluster), always specify cluster = ~school_id (or equivalent) in
  every model — first stage, second stage, balance tests, and reduced form.
  Explicitly note the effective N ≈ number of clusters, not number of observations.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

---

## Summary of what the plan got right vs. what was missing

| | Plan | Audit adds |
|---|---|---|
| Relevance / F-stat threshold | ✅ F>10 and F>100 flagged | ➕ First-stage functional form may understate F; test nonlinearity |
| Exclusion (SES sorting) | ✅ Flagged | ➕ Root cause: **endogenous center placement** (supply-side) — controlling for student SES doesn't fix this |
| Exclusion sensitivity analysis | ❌ Not mentioned | ➕ Conley et al. (2012) bounds — run this regardless |
| Independence (residential sorting) | ✅ Flagged | — |
| Monotonicity | ✅ Argued plausible | — |
| **Clustering** | ❌ Completely absent | ➕ Must cluster at school level; effective N << 3,000 |
| **LATE characterization** | ❌ Not raised | ➕ Who are the compliers? Does LATE answer the policy question? |
| **Wu-Hausman test** | ❌ Not mentioned | ➕ Test endogeneity before committing to IV |
| Reduced form | ❌ Not mentioned | ➕ Always report; requires no untestable assumptions |
| Distance measurement | ❌ Not asked | ➕ Straight-line vs. road network; MNAR geocoding failures |

The two issues I'd push hardest on before moving to Stage 3: **center placement** (does your exclusion argument survive it?) and **clustering** (get the school ID and use it everywhere). Everything else is important, but those two determine whether the analysis is publishable.
