--- Step 1 ---
Based on what you've described, this is a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — classic two-group DiD with a single treatment date. Let me ask a couple of questions to sharpen the plan.

**A few quick questions:**

**1. Was the rollout to those 200 stores randomly assigned, or were specific stores chosen?**

This matters a lot. If stores were selected based on characteristics (e.g., underperforming stores, or stores with better IT infrastructure), the treated and control groups may have had different pre-treatment trends — which is the key assumption DiD needs to hold.

**2. Did all 200 stores go live at exactly the same time in January, or did rollout happen in waves across January?**

If it was truly simultaneous, we use a standard TWFE specification. If some stores went live in week 1 and others in week 4, we're in staggered DiD territory, which requires a more careful estimator (Callaway-Sant'Anna or Sun-Abraham instead of naive TWFE).

**3. What's your outcome metric exactly — total weekly revenue, units sold, something else?**

---

While you answer those: your setup is actually quite strong for DiD. You have **18 months of pre-treatment weekly data**, which gives you excellent statistical power to test whether pre-trends were parallel before January. That's one of the best things you can have for this design.

--- Step 2 ---
Since you haven't answered the setup questions yet, I'll proceed with the **most common scenario** for this type of rollout — non-random store selection, simultaneous January launch, weekly revenue as the outcome — and I'll flag exactly where your answers would change things.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Testable — most important*

**What it means for you**: If the new checkout system had never been installed, your 200 treated stores and the control stores would have grown (or declined) at the same rate week over week. The *level* of revenue can differ — a flagship store doesn't need to look like a small store — but the *trajectory* must be similar.

**Why it matters**: You have 18 months of pre-treatment weekly data. That's ~78 weeks of pre-period, which is genuinely excellent. We can run an event study and visually inspect whether the two groups were tracking together before January. If treated stores were already accelerating before rollout, DiD will overstate the effect.

**The threat**: If stores were *selected* for rollout (e.g., highest-volume stores got it first, or stores where the IT team thought it would succeed), those stores may have had different pre-trends. This is why your answer to question 1 matters.

**Status**: 🟡 Testable. We'll generate the pre-trends test in Stage 3.

---

### 2. No Anticipation ⚠️ *Testable via event study*

**What it means for you**: Store managers and staff didn't change behavior *before* the system went live — no early training drives, no pre-launch marketing, no inventory buildup in December in anticipation of the new system.

**Why it matters**: If managers knew in October that their store was getting the new system in January, they might have started operating differently. That would bleed the "treatment effect" backward into the pre-period, making your pre-trends test look artificially clean *and* underestimating the true effect (the pre-period baseline is already elevated).

**The threat**: Retail rollouts often have pre-launch prep. If stores were told in Q4, you may see a ramp-up at t = -2 or t = -3 in the event study.

**Mitigation if violated**: The `did` package has an `anticipation =` parameter that shifts the "clean" pre-period back by k weeks.

**Status**: 🟡 Testable. Watch the event study coefficients at t = -2, -3.

---

### 3. Stable Composition 🟢 *Likely fine, worth a quick check*

**What it means for you**: The same stores appear in your data before and after January. No stores closed because of the rollout, and no new stores opened into the treated group.

**Why it matters**: If treated stores that struggled with the new system closed down, you'd only be measuring the effect on stores that *survived* — that's survivorship bias, and your estimate would be too optimistic.

**The threat**: Low for a checkout system rollout. Stores don't typically close because of a POS change. But worth a quick panel balance check.

**Status**: 🟢 Probably fine. One-liner diagnostic included in the code.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) 🔴 *Untestable — context-dependent*

**What it means for you**: The faster checkout at treated stores shouldn't draw customers *away from* nearby control stores. If it does, control stores look artificially bad after January — and DiD would overstate the system's effect.

**Why it matters**: This is the most common threat in retail studies. If your stores compete geographically, a better checkout experience at store A might cannibalize store B.

**The threat**: Depends on your store network. Urban clusters where stores are close together are more at risk than rural standalone stores. If you have store location data, we can test whether control stores near treated stores behaved differently post-treatment.

**Mitigation**: Use geographically distant stores as controls, or explicitly model spillovers if you have location data.

**Status**: 🔴 Untestable by default — needs your judgment on competitive overlap.

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form 🟢 *Not a concern for your design*

**What it means for you**: The "functional form" issue is specific to *staggered* rollouts, where different units are treated at different times. Standard TWFE breaks down there because it uses already-treated units as implicit controls for later-treated units.

**For your design**: If all 200 stores went live simultaneously in January, you have a single treatment cohort — standard TWFE is fine and there are no forbidden comparisons to worry about.

**If rollout was staggered across January**: This becomes 🟡 and we'd switch to Callaway-Sant'Anna. Flag it if that's the case.

**Status**: 🟢 No issue (assuming simultaneous rollout).

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | 🟡 Testable | Run event study pre-trends test |
| No anticipation | 🟡 Testable | Inspect event study at t = -2, -3 |
| Stable composition | 🟢 Likely fine | Quick balance check |
| No spillovers (SUTVA) | 🔴 Untestable | Judgment call on store geography |
| Correct functional form | 🟢 Not applicable | Only matters if staggered |

No fatal violations assumed — let's build the analysis.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

I'm generating this in **R** (with `fixest` + `did`). Let me know if you need Python.

```r
# ============================================================
# DiD Analysis: Checkout System Rollout
# Design: Classic 2x2 DiD, single treatment date (January)
# 200 treated stores, control stores observed over ~18 months
# ============================================================

library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(did)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 0. Data preparation ─────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns:
#   store_id  : unit identifier (integer or character)
#   week      : time identifier (integer week number, or Date)
#   revenue   : outcome (weekly revenue, numeric)
#   treated   : 1 if store received checkout system, 0 otherwise
#   post      : 1 if week >= treatment_week, 0 otherwise
#
# If your data is already in this shape, skip to Section 1.

# Example structure check:
glimpse(df)

# Create post indicator if not already present
# Replace '2024-01-07' with your actual go-live date
treatment_date <- as.Date("2024-01-07")

df <- df |>
  mutate(
    post          = as.integer(week >= treatment_date),
    treated_post  = treated * post,          # DiD interaction term
    log_revenue   = log(revenue + 1)         # optional: log scale
  )

# ── 1. Stable composition check ─────────────────────────────
# Are the same stores present across all weeks?

total_weeks <- n_distinct(df$week)

panel_balance <- df |>
  group_by(store_id) |>
  summarize(
    observed_weeks = n_distinct(week),
    treated        = max(treated),
    .groups        = "drop"
  ) |>
  group_by(treated) |>
  summarize(
    n_stores       = n(),
    pct_complete   = mean(observed_weeks == total_weeks) * 100,
    .groups        = "drop"
  )

print(panel_balance)
# Concern: if pct_complete < 95% and differs between groups, flag it.

# ── 2. Pre-trends visualization (raw) ───────────────────────
# Visually inspect whether treated and control stores
# tracked together before January.

pre_trends_plot <- df |>
  group_by(week, treated) |>
  summarize(mean_revenue = mean(revenue, na.rm = TRUE), .groups = "drop") |>
  mutate(group = if_else(treated == 1, "Treated (n=200)", "Control")) |>
  ggplot(aes(x = week, y = mean_revenue, color = group)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 0.8) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = as.numeric(treatment_date),
             linetype = "dashed", color = "gray40") +
  annotate("text", x = treatment_date, y = Inf,
           label = " Treatment", hjust = 0, vjust = 1.5, size = 3.5) +
  scale_color_manual(values = c("Treated (n=200)" = "#2196F3",
                                 "Control"         = "#FF9800")) +
  labs(title    = "Mean Weekly Revenue: Treated vs. Control Stores",
       subtitle = "Dashed line = checkout system go-live",
       x = "Week", y = "Mean Weekly Revenue ($)", color = NULL) +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 13) +
  theme(legend.position = "bottom")

print(pre_trends_plot)

# ── 3. Main DiD estimate ─────────────────────────────────────
# TWFE with unit (store) + time (week) fixed effects.
# Standard errors clustered at the store level.
#
# NOTE: Do NOT include `treated` as a separate regressor.
# Store fixed effects already absorb all time-invariant
# store characteristics, including treatment group membership.
# Only the interaction term `treated_post` is needed.

model_main <- feols(
  revenue ~ treated_post | store_id + week,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

summary(model_main)

# Optional: log-scale specification
model_log <- feols(
  log_revenue ~ treated_post | store_id + week,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

# Results table
modelsummary(
  list("Revenue ($)" = model_main, "Log Revenue" = model_log),
  stars      = c("*" = 0.1, "**" = 0.05, "***" = 0.01),
  gof_map    = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title      = "DiD Estimates: Effect of Checkout System on Weekly Revenue"
)

# ── 4. Effect size ───────────────────────────────────────────
att      <- coef(model_main)["treated_post"]
ci_lower <- confint(model_main)["treated_post", 1]
ci_upper <- confint(model_main)["treated_post", 2]

# Baseline mean (control group, pre-treatment period)
baseline_mean <- df |>
  filter(treated == 0, post == 0) |>
  summarize(mean_revenue = mean(revenue, na.rm = TRUE)) |>
  pull(mean_revenue)

pct_effect <- (att / baseline_mean) * 100

cat(sprintf(
  "\n── Effect Size ─────────────────────────────────────\n",
))
cat(sprintf("  ATT (DiD):         $%.0f per store-week\n", att))
cat(sprintf("  95%% CI:            [$%.0f, $%.0f]\n", ci_lower, ci_upper))
cat(sprintf("  Baseline mean:     $%.0f (control, pre-period)\n", baseline_mean))
cat(sprintf("  Relative effect:   %.1f%%\n", pct_effect))
cat(sprintf("────────────────────────────────────────────────\n\n"))

# ── 5. Event study (pre-trends test + dynamic effects) ───────
# Creates time-relative-to-treatment dummies.
# Reference period = week -1 (the week before go-live).
# Pre-treatment coefficients should be near zero if parallel
# trends hold. Post-treatment coefficients show how the effect
# builds or fades over time.

# Create numeric week index and time-to-treatment variable
treatment_week_num <- df |>
  filter(week == treatment_date) |>
  pull(week) |>
  unique() |>
  as.integer()

df <- df |>
  mutate(time_to_treat = as.integer(week) - treatment_week_num)

# Event study regression
# i() creates the relative-time dummies; ref = -1 drops week -1
es_model <- feols(
  revenue ~ i(time_to_treat, ref = -1) | store_id + week,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

# Plot: confidence intervals centered on zero pre-treatment = good
iplot(
  es_model,
  main  = "Event Study: Effect of Checkout System on Weekly Revenue",
  xlab  = "Weeks Relative to Rollout",
  ylab  = "Estimated Effect on Revenue ($)",
  ref.line = TRUE   # adds horizontal line at zero
)
abline(v = -0.5, lty = 2, col = "gray50")
text(-0.5, par("usr")[4] * 0.95, " Go-live", adj = 0, cex = 0.85)

# Joint test of pre-treatment coefficients
# H0: all pre-period effects = 0 (parallel trends)
pre_coef_names <- grep("time_to_treat::-", names(coef(es_model)), value = TRUE)

cat("\n── Pre-Trends Joint Test ────────────────────────────\n")
cat("H0: all pre-treatment coefficients = 0\n")
wald(es_model, keep = pre_coef_names)
cat("────────────────────────────────────────────────────\n\n")
# Interpretation:
#   p > 0.05 → no evidence against parallel trends ✓
#   p < 0.05 → pre-trends concern, investigate further ⚠️
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification Check

I'll generate a **placebo treatment date test** — the single most useful robustness check for your design:

```r
# ── Placebo: fake treatment date in the pre-period ───────────
# Pick a date roughly midway through your pre-period.
# Apply the same DiD estimator. The effect should be near zero.
# If it's not, something is wrong with the parallel trends assumption.

placebo_date <- treatment_date - weeks(36)   # ~9 months before rollout

df_placebo <- df |>
  filter(week < treatment_date) |>           # pre-period data only
  mutate(
    post_placebo          = as.integer(week >= placebo_date),
    treated_post_placebo  = treated * post_placebo
  )

model_placebo <- feols(
  revenue ~ treated_post_placebo | store_id + week,
  data    = df_placebo,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

placebo_att <- coef(model_placebo)["treated_post_placebo"]
placebo_p   <- pvalue(model_placebo)["treated_post_placebo"]

cat(sprintf(
  "\n── Placebo Test (fake date: %s) ─────────────────────\n",
  format(placebo_date, "%Y-%m-%d")
))
cat(sprintf("  Placebo ATT:  $%.0f\n", placebo_att))
cat(sprintf("  p-value:      %.3f\n", placebo_p))
cat(sprintf("  Verdict:      %s\n",
  if_else(placebo_p > 0.05,
    "✓ No spurious effect — parallel trends plausible",
    "⚠️  Significant placebo effect — investigate pre-trends"
  )
))
cat(sprintf("──────────────────────────────────────────────────\n\n"))
```

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you run the code, fill in the brackets:

> **The checkout system increased weekly revenue by $[ATT] per store (95% CI: $[lower] to $[upper]), equivalent to a [X]% lift relative to the control group baseline of $[baseline]/week.**
>
> The event study shows [flat / trending] pre-treatment coefficients, [supporting / raising concerns about] the parallel trends assumption. The placebo test using a fake treatment date of [date] yielded an effect of $[placebo_att] (p = [p]), [consistent / inconsistent] with no pre-existing differences between groups.
>
> **Caveats**:
> - If treated stores were selected non-randomly, unmeasured differences in store trajectory may bias the estimate upward.
> - The estimate captures the **average effect across all 200 stores** — there may be meaningful heterogeneity by store size, region, or baseline revenue.
> - Geographic spillovers (customers shifting from control to treated stores) are not accounted for and would cause the estimate to overstate the system's true incremental revenue impact.

---

**Two things that would sharpen this immediately:**

1. **Were stores randomly assigned?** → If yes, the parallel trends assumption is much stronger and we can say so explicitly. If no, the pre-trends test becomes load-bearing.
2. **Were all 200 stores live on the same date?** → If there were waves, I'd switch the estimator to `att_gt()` from the `did` package and you'd get cleaner cohort-specific effects.

### workflow_planner_to_matching

**Run 1**: {"handoff_quality": 1.0, "questions_passed": 5, "questions_total": 5}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

--- Step 1 ---
Based on what you've described, this is a **matching / doubly-robust (DR)** problem — specifically, selection on observables. Let me be upfront about what that means before we go further.

---

**Why this method**: You have no randomization, no panel (rules out DiD), no cutoff (rules out RDD), and no obvious instrument (rules out IV). That leaves conditional independence — the assumption that your rich covariate data captures *everything* that drove who opted in. With 10k observations and good covariates, doubly-robust estimation (AIPW) is the right call over pure PSM.

**The core concern — self-selection**: "Opted in" is a strong signal of pre-existing engagement. Customers who chose premium likely already buy more frequently. Your covariates need to fully explain *why* someone opted in, or the estimate will be upward-biased. This is the assumption you can never fully verify.

---

A couple of quick questions to sharpen the plan:

1. **What variables do you have?** Specifically — do you observe *pre-treatment* purchase frequency (before they opted in), or only current purchase frequency? If it's the same period, you have a simultaneity problem.

2. **How was the premium service made available?** Was it offered to everyone equally, or targeted to certain segments (e.g., high-spend customers, specific regions)? Targeted rollout often violates overlap — certain customer types only appear in one group.

3. **What's your estimand?** Are you trying to estimate the effect on *all customers* (ATE), or just on those who opted in (ATT)? This matters for method choice and interpretation.

--- Step 2 ---
## Stage 2: Assumptions

I'll proceed with what's described. I'm making three working assumptions — flag any that are wrong:
- **Treatment**: opted into premium service (binary)
- **Outcome**: purchase frequency (post-treatment)
- **Estimand**: ATT — effect on customers who opted in
- **Language**: Python (econml for DR). Say "switch to R" and I will.
- **Covariates**: pre-treatment purchase frequency, tenure, spend level, category breadth, region, age (adjust to your actual columns)

Let me walk through each assumption. I'll explain *why* it matters, not just what to check.

---

### Assumption 1: Conditional Independence (CIA) ⚠️ CRITICAL

**What it means here**: After conditioning on your covariates, the decision to opt into premium is unrelated to how frequently a customer *would have purchased* without it. In plain English: two customers who look identical on paper (same pre-treatment frequency, tenure, spend, etc.) had equal *potential* to increase purchasing — the only difference is one opted in.

**Why this is hard to believe**: "Opted in" is an active choice. Customers who self-select into premium are almost certainly more engaged, more brand-loyal, and more *already trending upward* in their purchasing — in ways your covariates may not fully capture. Motivation, price sensitivity, life stage events — these drive both the opt-in decision and future purchases, and are rarely in your data.

**Not testable**, but you can assess fragility:
- Does the treatment coefficient change a lot as you add more controls? Large changes suggest more unobserved confounders could matter further.
- Compute the E-value after estimation: "How strong would an unmeasured confounder need to be to explain away this effect?" A small E-value means a weak confounder could overturn your result.

**My read**: This assumption is under strain. The biggest risk is that customers who opted in were *already trending upward* (a pre-treatment trajectory you can't see if you only have a single pre-period snapshot). If you have **multiple pre-treatment periods**, you can test for trending — that's the single most important data question here.

**Status**: 🔴 Not verifiable — run sensitivity analysis post-estimation. If you have a pre-treatment time series, we can build a stronger test.

---

### Assumption 2: Overlap / Positivity ✅ Testable

**What it means here**: Every customer type had a real chance of either opting in or not. If high-spend customers *always* opt in (propensity score → 1) or low-engagement customers *never* opt in (propensity score → 0), you have no valid comparison group for those segments.

**Why it matters**: Without overlap, the DR estimator has to extrapolate — estimate what an "always-opts-in" customer would look like without premium, using data that doesn't exist. That's not identification, it's interpolation fiction. Extreme propensity scores also create numerically unstable weights (one unit can dominate the whole estimate).

**We'll check this** in Stage 3 with a propensity score overlap histogram and flag units outside [0.05, 0.95].

**Status**: 🟡 Testable — will generate diagnostic code.

---

### Assumption 3: SUTVA (No Interference) 🟡

**What it means here**: One customer's premium membership doesn't affect another customer's purchase frequency. This is plausible for most e-commerce or retail settings — but worth flagging one exception.

**The exception**: If premium membership comes with referral bonuses, social sharing incentives, or if treated customers share households with control customers, you have interference. Control-group customers could be exposed to the treatment indirectly, contaminating your comparison.

**Status**: 🟢 Likely satisfied — unless referrals or household sharing are part of the product. Flag if so.

---

### Assumption 4: Correct Model Specification 🟡 (DR gives you a safety net)

**What it means here**: This is where doubly-robust earns its name. You need to specify:
1. A **propensity score model**: what predicts who opts in
2. An **outcome model**: what predicts purchase frequency conditional on covariates

**The key DR property**: If *either* model is correctly specified, your estimate is consistent. You don't need both to be right — just one. This is a meaningful safety net over pure PSM or pure IPW.

**We'll use gradient boosted trees for both** (via `econml`), which is more flexible than logistic regression and less likely to suffer from linear misspecification.

**Balance check**: After fitting the propensity score, we'll verify covariate SMDs are < 0.1. Poor balance = propensity model is wrong AND the DR safety net is under more strain.

**Status**: 🟡 Partially mitigated by DR — monitor balance diagnostics.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Testable? | Status | Risk |
|---|---|---|---|
| Conditional independence | No | 🔴 Run sensitivity analysis | High — self-selection |
| Overlap / positivity | Yes | 🟡 Check PS distribution | Medium — targeted rollout? |
| SUTVA | No | 🟢 Likely satisfied | Low |
| Correct specification | Partially | 🟡 Check balance; DR helps | Medium — mitigated by DR |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
# ============================================================
# Doubly-Robust (AIPW) Estimation — Premium Service Opt-In
# econml + sklearn + statsmodels
# ============================================================

import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.gridspec as gridspec
from sklearn.ensemble import GradientBoostingClassifier, GradientBoostingRegressor
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_predict
from econml.dr import LinearDRLearner
from econml.dml import LinearDML
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import warnings
warnings.filterwarnings("ignore")

# ── 0. Load data ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# Replace with your actual data source
df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. Define variables ───────────────────────────────────────
# Adjust column names to match your data exactly
TREATMENT   = "opted_in_premium"      # binary: 1 = opted in, 0 = did not
OUTCOME     = "purchase_frequency"    # numeric: post-treatment purchases
COVARIATES  = [
    "pre_purchase_frequency",   # MOST IMPORTANT: pre-treatment baseline
    "tenure_days",
    "pre_total_spend",
    "pre_category_breadth",
    "region",                   # encode to dummies below
    "age",
]

# ── 2. Preprocessing ──────────────────────────────────────────
# One-hot encode categoricals, drop first to avoid multicollinearity
df_enc = pd.get_dummies(df[COVARIATES], drop_first=True)
covariate_cols = df_enc.columns.tolist()

Y = df[OUTCOME].values
T = df[TREATMENT].values
X = df_enc.values

print(f"Sample size : {len(df):,}")
print(f"Treated (opted in): {T.sum():,} ({T.mean():.1%})")
print(f"Control (did not) : {(1-T).sum():,} ({(1-T).mean():.1%})")
print(f"Covariates        : {len(covariate_cols)}")


# ── 3. Overlap check: propensity score distribution ──────────
# Estimate propensity scores via logistic regression first (interpretable)
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegressionCV

ps_model = LogisticRegressionCV(max_iter=1000, cv=5)
ps_model.fit(X, T)
ps = ps_model.predict_proba(X)[:, 1]
df["ps"] = ps

# Overlap histogram
fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(14, 5))

ax = axes[0]
ax.hist(ps[T == 1], bins=50, alpha=0.55, color="#e05c5c", label="Opted in (treated)", density=True)
ax.hist(ps[T == 0], bins=50, alpha=0.55, color="#5c7ae0", label="Did not opt in (control)", density=True)
ax.axvline(0.05, color="black", linestyle="--", linewidth=0.8, label="PS = 0.05 / 0.95")
ax.axvline(0.95, color="black", linestyle="--", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlabel("Propensity Score")
ax.set_ylabel("Density")
ax.set_title("Propensity Score Overlap\n(good overlap = distributions mix in the middle)")
ax.legend()

ax = axes[1]
extreme_low  = np.mean(ps < 0.05)
extreme_high = np.mean(ps > 0.95)
ax.bar(["PS < 0.05\n(near-certain\nnon-takers)",
        "0.05 ≤ PS ≤ 0.95\n(common support)",
        "PS > 0.95\n(near-certain\ntakers)"],
       [extreme_low, 1 - extreme_low - extreme_high, extreme_high],
       color=["#e0a05c", "#5cb85c", "#e05c5c"])
ax.set_ylabel("Fraction of sample")
ax.set_title("Common Support Breakdown")
ax.yaxis.set_major_formatter(plt.FuncFormatter(lambda y, _: f"{y:.1%}"))

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("overlap_check.png", dpi=150, bbox_inches="tight")
plt.show()

print(f"\nOverlap summary:")
print(f"  PS < 0.05 (will be dropped): {extreme_low:.1%} of sample")
print(f"  PS > 0.95 (will be dropped): {extreme_high:.1%} of sample")
print(f"  Common support             : {1 - extreme_low - extreme_high:.1%} of sample")

# Trim to common support
trim_mask = (ps >= 0.05) & (ps <= 0.95)
df_trim = df[trim_mask].copy()
Y_trim  = Y[trim_mask]
T_trim  = T[trim_mask]
X_trim  = X[trim_mask]
print(f"\nAfter trimming: {trim_mask.sum():,} units retained")


# ── 4. Covariate balance check (SMD) ─────────────────────────
def compute_smd(X, T, weights=None):
    """Standardized mean differences before/after weighting."""
    results = []
    for j, col in enumerate(covariate_cols):
        x_col = X[:, j]
        xt = x_col[T == 1]
        xc = x_col[T == 0]
        pooled_sd = np.sqrt((np.var(xt) + np.var(xc)) / 2)
        if pooled_sd < 1e-10:
            continue
        smd_raw = (xt.mean() - xc.mean()) / pooled_sd
        if weights is not None:
            wt = weights[T == 0]
            xc_wtd = np.average(xc, weights=wt)
            smd_wtd = (xt.mean() - xc_wtd) / pooled_sd
        else:
            smd_wtd = None
        results.append({"covariate": col, "SMD_before": smd_raw, "SMD_after": smd_wtd})
    return pd.DataFrame(results)

# IPW weights for ATT (for balance check only — DR uses them differently)
ps_trim = df_trim["ps"].values
ipw_weights = np.where(T_trim == 1, 1.0, ps_trim / (1 - ps_trim))

balance_df = compute_smd(X_trim, T_trim, weights=ipw_weights)
print("\nCovariate balance (SMD < 0.10 = well-balanced):")
print(balance_df.to_string(index=False))

# Love plot
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, max(5, len(balance_df) * 0.4)))
y_pos = np.arange(len(balance_df))
ax.barh(y_pos, balance_df["SMD_before"].abs(), 0.4, align="center",
        color="#cccccc", label="Before weighting")
ax.barh(y_pos + 0.4, balance_df["SMD_after"].abs(), 0.4, align="center",
        color="#5c7ae0", label="After IPW weighting")
ax.axvline(0.1, color="red", linestyle="--", linewidth=1, label="SMD = 0.10 threshold")
ax.set_yticks(y_pos + 0.2)
ax.set_yticklabels(balance_df["covariate"], fontsize=9)
ax.set_xlabel("|Standardized Mean Difference|")
ax.set_title("Love Plot: Covariate Balance\n(all bars should cross the red line after weighting)")
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("love_plot.png", dpi=150, bbox_inches="tight")
plt.show()

n_unbalanced = (balance_df["SMD_after"].abs() > 0.1).sum()
if n_unbalanced > 0:
    print(f"\n⚠️  {n_unbalanced} covariate(s) remain unbalanced after weighting.")
    print("   → Consider adding interaction terms or using a more flexible PS model.")
else:
    print("\n✅ All covariates balanced (SMD < 0.10 after weighting).")


# ── 5. Doubly-Robust estimation (AIPW via econml) ────────────
# Uses gradient boosted trees for both propensity score and outcome model.
# Estimate: ATT (average treatment effect on the treated — opted-in customers)

dr = LinearDRLearner(
    model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=200, max_depth=4,
                                                 learning_rate=0.05, random_state=42),
    model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=200, max_depth=4,
                                                learning_rate=0.05, random_state=42),
    cv=5,          # cross-fitting to prevent overfitting bias
    random_state=42
)

dr.fit(Y=Y_trim, T=T_trim, X=X_trim)

# ATT estimate with 95% CI
att        = dr.ate(X=X_trim[T_trim == 1])   # restrict to treated for ATT
att_lb, att_ub = dr.ate_interval(X=X_trim[T_trim == 1], alpha=0.05)

print("\n" + "="*55)
print("DOUBLY-ROBUST (AIPW) ESTIMATE — ATT")
print("="*55)
print(f"  ATT estimate : {att:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI       : [{att_lb:.4f}, {att_ub:.4f}]")
print(f"  Outcome units: {OUTCOME}")
outcome_mean = Y_trim[T_trim == 1].mean()
print(f"  Treated mean (post) : {outcome_mean:.4f}")
print(f"  Relative effect     : {att / outcome_mean:.1%}")
print("="*55)
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Three checks I'd prioritize for this problem, in order of importance:

```python
# ── 6a. Sensitivity: E-value ──────────────────────────────────
# How strong would an unmeasured confounder need to be (as a risk
# ratio) to fully explain away the observed effect?
# Small E-value = fragile result. Large E-value = more credible.

def e_value_additive(estimate, se, outcome_sd):
    """E-value approximation for additive (mean difference) effects.
    Converts to approximate risk ratio via delta method, then computes E-value."""
    # Rough approximation: standardize the effect
    d = estimate / outcome_sd
    # Convert Cohen's d to approximate RR using standard formula
    rr = np.exp(0.91 * d)
    ev = rr + np.sqrt(rr * (rr - 1))
    ev_ci = max(1.0, rr - np.sqrt(rr * (rr - 1)))  # for CI lower bound
    return rr, ev, ev_ci

outcome_sd = Y_trim.std()
rr_approx, ev, ev_ci = e_value_additive(att, (att_ub - att_lb) / (2*1.96), outcome_sd)

print("\nSensitivity Analysis — E-value")
print("-"*45)
print(f"  Approximate RR      : {rr_approx:.3f}")
print(f"  E-value (point est) : {ev:.3f}")
print(f"  E-value (CI bound)  : {ev_ci:.3f}")
print(f"\n  Interpretation: An unmeasured confounder would need")
print(f"  to be associated with both treatment and outcome by")
print(f"  a risk ratio of >{ev:.2f}x to fully explain away this effect.")
print(f"  The confidence interval survives confounding up to {ev_ci:.2f}x.")

# ── 6b. Placebo outcome ───────────────────────────────────────
# Replace the outcome with a variable that should NOT be affected
# by premium membership (e.g., purchase frequency in a category
# the premium doesn't cover, or a demographic attribute).
# Finding a significant "effect" here suggests residual confounding.

PLACEBO_OUTCOME = "unrelated_category_purchases"  # ← replace with your placebo

if PLACEBO_OUTCOME in df_trim.columns:
    Y_placebo = df_trim[PLACEBO_OUTCOME].values

    dr_placebo = LinearDRLearner(
        model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=200, max_depth=4,
                                                     random_state=42),
        model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=200, max_depth=4,
                                                    random_state=42),
        cv=5, random_state=42
    )
    dr_placebo.fit(Y=Y_placebo, T=T_trim, X=X_trim)
    att_p    = dr_placebo.ate(X=X_trim[T_trim == 1])
    att_p_lb, att_p_ub = dr_placebo.ate_interval(X=X_trim[T_trim == 1])

    print(f"\nPlacebo Test — {PLACEBO_OUTCOME}")
    print(f"  ATT (should be ~0): {att_p:.4f}  95% CI: [{att_p_lb:.4f}, {att_p_ub:.4f}]")
    if att_p_lb <= 0 <= att_p_ub:
        print("  ✅ CI includes zero — consistent with no spurious effect.")
    else:
        print("  ⚠️  CI excludes zero — possible residual confounding.")
else:
    print(f"\nPlacebo test skipped: '{PLACEBO_OUTCOME}' not found in data.")
    print("  → Identify a pre-treatment or unrelated outcome to run this check.")

# ── 6c. Propensity score trimming sensitivity ─────────────────
# How sensitive is the ATT to the trimming threshold?
# If results change dramatically, overlap is the binding problem.

print("\nTrimming sensitivity:")
print(f"  {'Threshold':<12} {'ATT':>8} {'CI lower':>10} {'CI upper':>10} {'N retained':>12}")
print("-"*58)

for trim_threshold in [0.02, 0.05, 0.10, 0.15]:
    mask_t = (ps >= trim_threshold) & (ps <= 1 - trim_threshold)
    Y_t, T_t, X_t = Y[mask_t], T[mask_t], X[mask_t]
    dr_t = LinearDRLearner(
        model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=100, random_state=42),
        model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=100, random_state=42),
        cv=3, random_state=42
    )
    dr_t.fit(Y=Y_t, T=T_t, X=X_t)
    att_t = dr_t.ate(X=X_t[T_t == 1])
    lb_t, ub_t = dr_t.ate_interval(X=X_t[T_t == 1])
    print(f"  [{trim_threshold:.2f}, {1-trim_threshold:.2f}]   {att_t:>8.4f} {lb_t:>10.4f} {ub_t:>10.4f} {mask_t.sum():>12,}")
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you run the code, fill in the bracketed values:

> Using doubly-robust (AIPW) estimation with gradient-boosted propensity and outcome models, the average treatment effect on the treated (ATT) is **[att]** additional purchases (95% CI: [att_lb, att_ub]). This represents a **[relative_effect]%** increase relative to the treated group's mean of [outcome_mean].
>
> Covariate balance was [achieved / not fully achieved] after weighting (all/most SMDs < 0.10). The propensity score overlap plot showed [good / limited] common support, with [X%] of the sample trimmed.
>
> **Critical caveat**: This estimate relies on the assumption that all important drivers of premium opt-in are captured in the observed covariates. The E-value of [ev] means an unmeasured confounder would need to be associated with both opt-in and purchase frequency by a factor of >[ev]x to fully explain away the effect. Given the self-selection nature of this treatment, that threshold may not be high. Interpret with appropriate caution — this is the weakest available identification strategy.
